Buyu — Sales and Trade

8 min
When Does a Slave's Absconding Become a Defect?
Definition and conditions of ibāq as a defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of ibāq as a defect in slaves and identify the conditions under which it entitles the buyer to return the slave.

Core Idea
Ibāq is a defect only when the slave rebelliously absconds without oppression, reducing its price.

In sales of slaves, the Hanafi school recognizes certain defects that give the buyer a right of return (khiyar al-ayb). One such defect is ibāq – the slave’s rebellious running away. This lesson unpacks the precise definition, conditions, and several nuanced scenarios from the classical texts.

Guided Path
1
Define ibāq linguistically and legally
Identify the root and meaning of ibāq: running away from the master without fear or toil, with rebellious intent.
Contrast with hurb (fleeing from oppression).
2
Distinguish ibāq from hurb
Note that only rebellious absconding (ibāq) is a defect; fleeing from oppression is not.
3
Identify conditions for ibāq as a defect
Conditions include: motivation must be rebellion (not fear); must reduce price according to traders; for children, must be discerning; recurrence may be required if not continuous.
4
Apply to various scenarios
Consider absconding from buyer to seller without hiding (not a defect), from a small town (not a defect if easily located), and from hirees/borrowers (is a defect).
5
Note subsidiary issues
Review the case of a bull (three opinions) and the effect of the seller's statements on the right to return.
Also note that the buyer cannot claim reduction before the slave returns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كالإباق) من أبق العبد أبقاً من باب تعب وقتل في لغة، والأكثر من باب ضرب إذا هرب من سيده من غير خوف ولا كد. والإباق ـ بالكسر - اسم منه فهو آبق والجمع إباق مثل كافر وكفار؛ كذا في المصباح. وفي الجوهرة من بابه قال الثعالبي : الآبق الهارب من غير ظلم السيد فإن هرب من الظلم لا يسمى أبقاً بل يسمى هارباً فعلى هذا الإباق عيب والهروب ليس يعيب اهـ. وفي خزانة الفقة : الإباق الاستخفاء عن مولاه تمرداً. وفي القاموس إنه من باب ضرب ومنع وسمع اهـ. فعلى هذا له أبواب أربعة : الثلاثة وقتل كما في المصباح فسره في القاموس بالذهاب من غير خوف ولا كد عمله أو استخفى ثم ذهب. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا أبق من المولى أو من غيره مستأجراً أو مستعيراً أو مودعاً إلا من غاصب إلى المولى أو غيره إن لم يعرف منزله أو لم يقو على الرجوع إليه، ويرد على إطلاقهم ما إذا أبق من المشتري إلى البائع ولم يختف عنده فإنه ليس بعيب كما في القنية. وشمل ما إذا كان مسيرة سفر أو أقل وما إذا خرج من البلد أو لم يخرج لكن الأشبه أن البلدة إذا كانت وعدنا بها وحقها أن تكتب هنا لكنه كتبها هنا ولم يذكر هل له الرد على الصغير إذا كبر فراجعه . قوله : (وذلك بانتقاص القيمة يفيد أن المراد بالثمن القيمة يدل عليه ما نقله عن المحيط قبيل التنبيهات في المسألة الحادية عشر . قوله : (وكما في جارية تركية لا تعرف التركية) أي فله الرد لأن ذلك عيب، وإذا اشترى جارية هندية فوجدها لا تحسن الهندية إذا كان الناس يعدونه عيباً فله الرد وإلا فلا؛ كذا في النهر عن المحيط. وسوى بينهما في البزازية فقال : اشترى تركية أو هندية لا تحسنها إن عده أ ، أهل الخبرة عيباً فكذلك وإلا لا . قوله : وقيد في المعراج الظفر الأسود الخ) قال في النهر : والظاهر إطلاق ما في الفتح. قوله : (وهو أحسن مما في الكتاب قال في النهر : وكان وجهه أن نقصان الثمن بسبب نقص العين أو المنفعة مما يعرفه كل أحد لا أنه مقيد بالتجار كما يوهمه كلام المصنف. قوله: (ويرد على إطلاقهم ما إذا أبق الخ قال في النهر : يمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن الكلام في الإباق الذي يوجب نقص الثمن عند التجار ليصح كونه جزئياً . هذا من الكلي وهذا لا يوجبه . كتاب البيع باب خيار العيب ٢٥ يسمى كبيرة كالقاهرة فهو عيب، وإن كانت صغيرة بحيث لا يخفى عليه أهلها وبيوتها لا يكون عيباً كما ذكره الشارح . وشمل الصغير والكبير لكن إذا كان غير مميز لا يكون عيباً والعذر له أنه ضالاً لا آبقاً كما في السراج الوهاج، فلذا لم يقيده وسيأتي أنه لا بد من المعاودة عند المشتري واتحاد السبب. وفي البزازية قال لآخر اشتره لا عيب فيه فاشتراه ثم وجد به عيباً له أن يرده على بائعه ولو قال اشتر هذا العبد فإنه غير آبق والمسألة بحالها لا يرد بعيب الإباق. وفي الصغرى: قول المشتري ليس به عيب لا يكون إقراراً بانتفاء العيوب، ولو عين فقال ليس بآبق لا يكون إقراراً بانتفائه شهدا أنه باعه بشرط البراءة من كل عيب أو من الإباق ثم اشتراه الشاهد ووجد به عيباً أو قال إنه أبق له الرد عبدي هذا آبق فاشتراه وباع من آخر فوجده الثاني آبقاً وأراد الرد بإقرار بائعه لا يقبل، وإن قال عند البيع بعته على أنه أبق أو على أنه بريء من إباقه يرده ولو قال إنه بريء من الإباق لا لعدم الإضافة اهـ. وفي جامع الفصولين: ولو شراه وأبق من عنده وكان أبق عند البائع لا يرجع بنقصان ما دام القن حياً ابقاً عند أبي حنيفة، وكذا لو سرق المبيع فعلم بعيبه لا يرجع بنقصه ليس للمشتري أن يطلب البائع بثمنه قبل عود الآبق .اهـ. وفي الصغرى قبل عوده أو موته وشمل إطلاقه أيضاً إباق الثور ولكن فيه ثلاثة أقوال في القنية قيل : إذا أبق الثور من قرية المشتري إلى قرية البائع لا يكون عيباً، وقيل في الغلام عيب وقيل في الثور عيب كخلع الرسن عيب فهذا أولى، وقيل إن دام فعيب أما المرتان والثلاثة فلا قال رحمه الله تعالى : والثاني أحسن. وفيها أيضاً : اشترى عبداً فأبق ثم وجده ولم يأبق عند بائعه بل أبق عند بائع بائعه ثم فله الرد اهـ.
His statement: (like absconding) – from 'abaqa al-ʿabdu ibāqan' from the measure of taʿiba and qutila in one language, but most commonly from the measure of ḍaraba when he runs away from his master without fear or toil. Al-ibāq (with kasrah) is the noun, thus he is ābiq, plural ibāq like kāfir and kuffār; thus in al-Miṣbāḥ. And in al-Jawharah from its chapter, al-Thaʿālibī said: Al-ābiq is one who flees without oppression from the master; if he flees from oppression, he is not called ābiq but hārib. Thus based on this, ibāq is a defect, and fleeing is not a defect. And in Khazānat al-Fiqh: Al-ibāq is hiding from the master out of rebellion. And in al-Qāmūs it is from the measure of ḍaraba and manaʿa and samiʿa. Thus based on this, it has four measures: the three and qutila as in al-Miṣbāḥ, interpreted in al-Qāmūs as going away without fear or toil, or hiding then going. He gave it unrestrictedly, so it includes when he absconds from the master or from another (hiree, borrower, depositee) except from a usurper to the master or another if he does not know his home or cannot return. And an objection is raised to their unrestricted statement: if he absconds from the buyer to the seller and does not hide with him, it is not a defect as in al-Qunyah. And it includes whether it is a journey's distance or less, and whether he leaves the town or not, but the more appropriate is that if the town is large like Cairo, it is a defect; if small such that its people and houses are not hidden from him, it is not a defect as the commentator mentioned. And it includes the small and the big, but if he is non-discerning, it is not a defect, and the excuse is that he is lost, not absconding, as in al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj. Thus he did not restrict it, and it will come that recurrence with the buyer and unity of cause are necessary. In al-Bazzāziyyah: He said to another 'Buy it, there is no defect in it,' so he bought it and then found a defect in it; he may return it to its seller. If he said 'Buy this slave, for he is not absconding,' and the case is the same, he does not return due to the defect of absconding. And in al-Ṣughrā: The buyer's statement 'There is no defect in it' is not an acknowledgment of the absence of defects. If he specified and said 'He is not absconding,' it is not an acknowledgment of its absence...
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed treatment of absconding (ibāq) as a defect in slaves. It begins by defining the term linguistically, noting that ibāq is running away from the master without fear or toil, as opposed to fleeing from oppression (which is not a defect). The definition is refined through several authoritative sources (al-Miṣbāḥ, al-Jawharah, Khazānat al-Fiqh, al-Qāmūs). The key distinction is that the slave must be in a state of rebellion (tamarrud). The passage then discusses various scenarios: absconding from a hirer, borrower, or depositee is included, except if the slave returns to the seller without hiding (then not a defect). The size of the city matters: absconding from a large city like Cairo is a defect, but from a small town where houses are not hidden is not. Non-discerning children are exempt because they are lost, not absconding. The passage also covers legal consequences: if a buyer purchases a slave who later absconds, he cannot claim a reduction in price until the slave returns (according to Abu Hanifa). There are discussions about whether a bull can abscond as a defect, with multiple opinions. The unifying principle is that ibāq must cause a decrease in price as recognized by traders. The author includes numerous references to Hanafi works like al-Bazzāziyyah, al-Ṣughrā, Jāmiʿ al-Fuṣūlayn, etc., showing the richness of the tradition.

Key Terms
الإباق (al-ibāq): A slave's rebellious running away from his master without fear or toil; considered a defect in sales.
الآبق (al-ābiq): The slave who commits ibāq; must be motivated by rebellion, not fear of injustice.
العيب (al-ʿayb): A defect entitling return; here specifically ibāq when it causes a decrease in price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a slave's absconding (ibāq) NOT constitute a defect allowing return?
A) When the slave runs away from the buyer and returns to the seller without hiding.
B) When the slave runs away from a small town where his whereabouts are not hidden.
C) When the slave is a non-discerning child who is lost.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Ibāq is a defect in slaves only when it is rebellious absconding that reduces the market price, with specific exceptions for small towns, non-discerning children, and seller-directed escape without hiding.

Bed-Wetting as a Defect in Slaves
Ruling on bed-wetting as a defect with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which bed-wetting constitutes a defect in a slave in Hanafi law and apply the condition of recurrence to distinguish a valid return from a new defect.

Core Idea
Bed-wetting is a defect only if it recurs after puberty, not from infant weakness.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq explains when a slave's bed-wetting allows the buyer to return the slave. The author clarifies that the ruling applies broadly but excludes non-discerning children, and crucially requires the wetting to recur at the buyer's side. The reasoning distinguishes between childhood weakness and a persistent internal disease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is bed-wetting (al-bawl fī al-firāsh). The ruling is that it is considered a defect, giving the buyer the option of return (khiyar al-'ayb).
2
Note the exception
The exception is for non-discerning children (ghayr al-mumayyiz), where bed-wetting is not a defect because it is due to weak bladder, not a disease.
The child must be old enough to control bladder normally; the exact age is determined by custom.
3
Understand the condition of recurrence (mu'awadah)
Bed-wetting must occur at least once at the buyer's place after the sale. If it only happened at the seller's, it is not a defect.
Recurrence distinguishes a latent defect from a one-time incident.
4
Differentiate scenarios
If the slave wet in childhood at seller's and after puberty at buyer's: it is a new defect (childhood due to weak bladder, adulthood due to disease). If wet at both places in same stage (both childhood or both adulthood): same cause, so it is a defect.
Unity of cause (ittihad al-sabab) is key.
5
Examine the complex case
From al-Fawāʾid al-Ẓuhriyyah: if a buyer buys a young slave who wets the bed, he can return. If another defect occurs at buyer's, he may claim reduction. If he claims reduction and then bed-wetting stops at puberty, what happens? No explicit narration, but deduced from analogous cases.
This shows ijtihad by istidlal (qa'is) within Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والبول في الفراش من العيوب أطلقه فشمل الكبير والصغير ويستثنى منه غير المميز فإنه لا يكون عيباً ولا بد من معاودته عند المشتري في حالة واحدة فإن بال في الصغر عند البائع ثم بعد البلوغ عند المشتري لا يرده لأنه في الصغر لضعف المثانة، وبعد البلوغ لداء في باطنه فهو عيب حادث بخلاف ما إذا بال عندهما في الصغر أو في الكبر لاتحاد السبب. وفي الفوائد الظهرية هنا مسألة عجيبة هي ! من اشترى عبداً صغيراً فوجده يبول في الفراش كان له الرد، ولو تعيب بعيب آخر عند المشتري كان له أن يرجع بنقصان العيب، فإذا رجع به ثم النقصان لزوال ذلك العيب بالبلوغ؟ لا رواية فيها قال : وكان استدلالاً بمسألتين : إحداهما إذا اشترى جارية فوجدها ذات
His statement: (And bed-wetting in the mattress is from the defects). He gave it unrestrictedly, so it includes the old and the young. And an exception is made for the non-discerning, for it is not a defect. And it must recur with the buyer in one instance. For if he wet the bed in childhood at the seller's, then after puberty at the buyer's, he does not return it because in childhood it is due to weak bladder, and after puberty due to an internal disease, so it is a new defect. Contrary to if he wet the bed at both places in childhood or in adulthood due to unity of cause. And in al-Fawāʾid al-Ẓuhriyyah there is an amazing case: He who buys a young slave and finds him wetting the bed has the right to return him. And if another defect occurs at the buyer's, he may claim a reduction for the defect. If he claims reduction, then the defect disappears upon puberty? There is no narration. He said: And it is inferred from two cases: one is if he buys a female slave and finds her ...
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage addresses bed-wetting (al-bawl fī al-firāsh) as a defect in a slave. The author clarifies that the ruling applies to both adults and children, with the exception of non-discerning children (who lack awareness). A critical condition is that the bed-wetting must recur (muʿāwadah) at the buyer's side. If the slave wet the bed only in childhood at the seller's but then does not wet after puberty at the buyer's, it is not a defect; the childhood wetting is due to weak bladder, and after puberty it may be due to disease, so it is a new defect. However, if the slave wet the bed in both childhood and adulthood at both places, it is a defect because the cause is the same. The passage then presents a complex case from al-Fawāʾid al-Ẓuhriyyah: If a buyer purchases a young slave who wets the bed, he can return him. If the slave develops another defect at the buyer's, the buyer can claim a reduction in price for that defect. But if he claims the reduction and then the bed-wetting stops upon puberty, what happens? There is no explicit narration on this, but it is deduced from two other cases (one about a female slave). The passage is cut off, but the reasoning would involve whether the diminution in value has ceased. This showcases the Mālikī-like analogical reasoning (istidlāl) within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
البول في الفراش (al-bawl fī al-firāsh): The act of urinating in bed, considered a defect in a slave if recurring after maturity.
المعاودة (al-muʿāwadah): Recurrence of the defect; here, the bed-wetting must occur at least once at the buyer's place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a slave's bed-wetting NOT constitute a defect under Hanafi law?
A) When it occurs only once at the seller's place in childhood.
B) When it occurs both in childhood and adulthood at both the seller's and buyer's places.
C) When it occurs at the buyer's place after the slave has reached puberty.
D) When it occurs every night for a month at the buyer's place.

Key Takeaway
Bed-wetting is a defect only if it recurs after puberty, excluding non-discerning children; one-time infant wetting does not count.

When Does Running Away (Ibaq) Constitute a Defect in Sales?
Explanation of when running away constitutes a defect and rulings on return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which ibaq is considered a defect and identify the preferred Hanafi opinion on the ruling for an ox.

Core Idea
Ibaq is a defect only if it indicates a habit; straying is not a defect.

In this passage, Ibn Nujaym examines the complex question of whether a slave's or animal's running away qualifies as a defect giving the buyer the right to return the item. The answer depends on the nature of the escape, its frequency, and the type of item. The passage cites multiple authorities and presents an intra-Hanafi dispute over the ox, ultimately preferring one opinion.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between ibaq and dalal
Identify the key distinction: ibaq (running away with intent) vs. dalal (straying). The text says if the creature is indistinguishable, it is considered straying, not a defect.
This distinction hinges on the animal's or slave's intention, which is inferred from behavior.
2
Identify the scope of ibaq as a defect
Determine whether ibaq applies to slaves and animals. The passage discusses both a boy (ghulam) and an ox, indicating it applies to both.
The text uses 'ghulam' for a young slave, but the principle extends to adult slaves and animals.
3
Analyze the three opinions on the ox
Understand the three views from al-Qunyah: (1) not a defect if it runs from buyer's village to seller's; (2) a defect for both boy and ox, by analogy to removal of a halter; (3) a defect only if persistent. Note that the author prefers the second.
The third opinion introduces a condition of persistence (i.e., repeated behavior).
4
Examine the tarjih mechanism
The author says 'wa al-thani ahsan' (the second is better), showing how the preferred position is selected within the school.
Tarjih is based on the strength of analogy and practical implications.
5
Apply the ruling to the buyer's options
The option to return (khiyar al-'ayb) is available if ibaq is a defect. The text also notes that the buyer cannot demand compensation before the runaway's return, and the seller's statement 'not a runaway' is not an acknowledgment.
The buyer must prove the defect was present at the time of sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب خيار العيب ٢٥ يسمى كبيرة كالقاهرة فهو عيب، وإن كانت صغيرة بحيث لا يخفى عليه أهلها وبيوتها لا يكون عيباً كما ذكره الشارح . وشمل الصغير والكبير لكن إذا كان غير مميز لا يكون عيباً والعذر له أنه ضالاً لا آبقاً كما في السراج الوهاج، فلذا لم يقيده وسيأتي أنه لا بد من المعاودة عند المشتري واتحاد السبب. وفي البزازية قال لآخر اشتره لا عيب فيه فاشتراه ثم وجد به عيباً له أن يرده ولو قال اشتر هذا العبد فإنه غير آبق والمسألة بحالها لا يرد بعيب الإباق. وفي الصغرى: قول المشتري ليس به عيب لا يكون إقراراً بانتفاء العيوب، ولو عين فقال ليس بآبق لا يكون إقراراً بانتفائه شهدا أنه باعه بشرط البراءة من كل عيب أو من الإباق ثم اشتراه الشاهد ووجد به عيباً أو قال إنه أبق له الرد عبدي هذا آبق فاشتراه وباع من آخر فوجده الثاني آبقاً وأراد الرد بإقرار بائعه لا يقبل، وإن قال عند البيع بعته على أنه أبق أو على أنه بريء من إباقه يرده ولو قال إنه بريء من الإباق لا لعدم الإضافة اهـ. وفي جامع الفصولين: ولو شراه وأبق من عنده وكان أبق عند البائع لا يرجع بنقصان ما دام القن حياً ابقاً عند أبي حنيفة، وكذا لو سرق المبيع فعلم بعيبه لا يرجع بنقصه ليس للمشتري أن يطلب البائع بثمنه قبل عود الآبق .اهـ. وفي الصغرى قبل عوده أو موته وشمل إطلاقه أيضاً إباق الثور ولكن فيه ثلاثة أقوال في القنية قيل : إذا أبق الثور من قرية المشتري إلى قرية البائع لا يكون عيباً، وقيل في الغلام عيب وقيل في الثور عيب كخلع الرسن عيب فهذا أولى، وقيل إن دام فعيب أما المرتان والثلاثة فلا قال رحمه الله تعالى : والثاني أحسن. وفيها أيضاً : اشترى عبداً فأبق ثم وجده ولم يأبق عند بائعه بل أبق عند بائع بائعه ثم فله الرد اهـ.
Book of Sales, Chapter of Option of Defect: What is called a major (defect) like madness is a defect, and if it is small such that its people and houses do not hide it, it is not a defect as the commentator mentioned. It includes small and large, but if it is indistinguishable it is not a defect; his excuse is that it is straying, not running away, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Thus he did not restrict it, and it will come that recurrence with the buyer and unity of cause are necessary. In al-Bazzaziyya: He said to another 'Buy it, there is no defect in it' so he bought it, then found a defect in it; he may return it to his seller. Even if he said 'Buy this slave, for he is not a runaway', the case is the same; he does not return due to the defect of running away. In al-Sughra: The buyer's statement 'There is no defect in it' is not an acknowledgment of the absence of defects. And if he specified and said 'He is not a runaway', it is not an acknowledgment of its absence. If two witnesses testify that he sold it with a condition of immunity from every defect or from running away, then the witness bought it and found a defect, or he said that he had run away, he has the right to return. 'My slave is a runaway' - he bought him and sold to another, then the second found him a runaway and wanted to return based on his seller's acknowledgment; it is not accepted. If he said at the time of sale 'I sold him on the condition that he is a runaway' or 'on immunity from his running away', he returns him. Even if he said 'He is immune from running away', no, due to lack of addition. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he bought him and he ran away from him, and he had run away at the seller's, he does not claim compensation as long as the slave is alive and a runaway according to Abu Hanifa. Similarly, if the sold item was stolen and he knew of its defect, he does not claim compensation; the buyer may not demand its price from the seller before the return of the runaway. In al-Sughra: Before his return or his death, and its absolute application also includes the running away of an ox. But there are three opinions in al-Qunyah: it was said if the ox runs away from the buyer's village to the seller's village, it is not a defect; it was said in the case of a boy it is a defect; it was said in the case of an ox it is a defect like the removal of a halter, so this is more appropriate; and it was said if it continues it is a defect, as for two or three times, no. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The second is better. And in it also: He bought a slave, then he ran away, then he found him, and he had not run away at his seller's, but rather he had run away at his seller's seller's, then he has the right to return.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof type, from the commentary of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, specifically on the chapter of khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect). The author, Ibn Nujaym, discusses the defect of ibaq (running away) in sales. He begins by distinguishing between major and minor defects: a major defect like insanity is always a defect, while a minor defect that is not hidden by the people or the houses of the locality is not considered a defect. The commentary notes that the ruling applies to both small and large animals, but if the running away is indistinguishable (e.g., a young child who wanders off), it is not a defect because the excuse is that he is straying (dal) not absconding (abiq). This is a key distinction: the intent to escape matters. The passage then cites multiple sources: al-Bazzaziyya states that if someone instructs another to buy something claiming it has no defect, and a defect is later found, the buyer can return it. However, if the seller says 'this slave is not a runaway', but the slave does run away, the buyer cannot return on the basis of ibaq because the seller's statement is not a warranty against ibaq unless explicitly stated. Al-Sughra provides further nuances: the buyer's general statement 'no defect' is not an acknowledgment of no defects; specifying 'not a runaway' is also not an acknowledgment. If witnesses testify that the sale included immunity from all defects including ibaq, then the buyer can return if a defect appears. The passage then discusses complex scenarios of acknowledgement and sale of a known runaway. From Jami' al-Fusulayn: if a slave runs away from the buyer and had previously run away at the seller's, the buyer cannot claim compensation from the seller according to Abu Hanifa, as long as the slave remains alive and a runaway. Similarly, if the slave is stolen, the buyer cannot claim compensation. The buyer cannot demand the price before the slave's return. The passage then covers a specific case of an ox running away, citing three opinions from al-Qunyah: (1) it is not a defect if it runs from buyer's village to seller's; (2) it is a defect for a boy (ghulam) and also for an ox like removal of halter; (3) it is a defect only if persistent (continues), not if it happens once or twice. The commentator prefers the second opinion (that it is a defect for both boy and ox). Finally, from al-Sughra: if a buyer purchases a slave who later runs away, but the slave had only run away at a previous seller's (not the immediate seller), the slave still has the right to return, meaning the buyer can return the slave to the immediate seller for this defect. The overall thrust is that ibaq is a defect only under specific conditions, and not every instance of running away gives the buyer the right to return.

Key Terms
عيب ('ayb): A defect that diminishes the value or desirability of the sold item, giving the buyer the option to return.
إباق (ibaq): The act of a slave or animal running away from its owner, considered a defect under certain conditions.
ضال (daal): Straying without intention to escape, which is not a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the preferred opinion on whether the running away of an ox is a defect?
A) It is not a defect if it runs from the buyer's village to the seller's.
B) It is a defect, analogous to the removal of a halter.
C) It is a defect only if it continues persistently.
D) It is never a defect because oxen are not subject to this ruling.

Key Takeaway
Running away (ibaq) is a defect in sales only when it indicates a habit of escaping; the preferred Hanafi view treats it as a defect for both humans and animals.

Ruling on Urinating in Bed as a Defect: Conditions and Recurrence
Explanation of the defect of urinating in bed with conditions and a qiyas-based compensation ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which urinating in bed is considered a defect in Hanafi sales law and apply the analogy for compensation when the defect disappears naturally.

Core Idea
Urinating in bed is a defect only if it recurs after maturity; natural disappearance allows seller to reclaim compensation.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on the defect of urinating in bed (al-bawl fi al-firash) in sales. The text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains when this condition is considered a defect, the exception for non-discerning children, and the requirement of recurrence. It also delves into a nuanced case from al-Fawa'id al-Zahriyya regarding compensation when the defect disappears naturally through puberty, resolved by analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect and its general ruling
The text states that urinating in bed is a defect, covering both old and young, but an exception is made for a child who is not discerning (ghayr mumayyiz). The ruling is absolute but qualified.
The defective act is urination in bed, and it is considered a hidden defect (ayb khafi).
2
Understand the condition of recurrence
The defect must recur (mu'awadah) at the buyer's place in a consistent state. If the cause differs (childhood due to weak bladder vs. adulthood due to disease), it is a new defect and the buyer cannot return.
If urination occurs at both places in same life stage, it is the same defect.
3
Examine the compensation case
When a returned slave acquires another defect at the buyer's, compensation is claimed. If the original defect disappears naturally (e.g., puberty), the seller can reclaim the compensation.
This is derived by analogy, not direct narration.
4
Follow the qiyas reasoning
The case is analogous to two examples: a slave girl with a defect who is divorced (external event) — seller reclaims compensation; and a sick slave cured by treatment — seller does not reclaim. Puberty is like natural cure, so seller reclaims.
The illa is that the defect disappeared without the buyer's action by a natural cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والبول في الفراش من العيوب أطلقه فشمل الكبير والصغير ويستثنى منه غير المميز فإنه لا يكون عيباً ولا بد من معاودته عند المشتري في حالة واحدة فإن بال في الصغر عند البائع ثم بعد البلوغ عند المشتري لا يرده لأنه في الصغر لضعف المثانة، وبعد البلوغ لداء في باطنه فهو عيب حادث بخلاف ما إذا بال عندهما في الصغر أو في الكبر لاتحاد السبب. وفي الفوائد الظهرية هنا مسألة عجيبة هي ! من اشترى عبداً صغيراً فوجده يبول في الفراش كان له الرد، ولو تعيب بعيب آخر عند المشتري كان له أن يرجع بنقصان العيب، فإذا رجع به ثم النقصان لزوال ذلك العيب بالبلوغ؟ لا رواية فيها قال : وكان استدلالاً بمسألتين : إحداهما إذا اشترى جارية فوجدها ذات كبر العبد هل للبائع أن يسترد والدي يقول: ينبغي أن يسترد أن ا. زوج كان له أن يردها ولو تعيبت ب آخر يرجع بالنقصان فإذا رجع ثم أبانها الزوج كان للبائع أن يسترد النقصان . كذا الثانية اشترى عبداً فوجده مريضاً له الرد فإذا تعيب بعيب آخر رجع بنقصان العيب، فإذا رجع ثم برئ بالمداواة لا يسترد وإلا استرد والبلوغ هنا لا بالمداواة فينبغي أن يسترد ؛ في المعراج والنهاية.
His statement: (And urinating in the bed is among the defects. He made it absolute, so it includes the old and the young, but an exception is made for the one who is not discerning; for it is not a defect. And recurrence with the buyer is necessary in one case: if he urinated in childhood at the seller's, then after puberty at the buyer's, he does not return him because in childhood it is due to weakness of the bladder, and after puberty it is due to an internal disease, so it is a newly occurring defect, contrary to if he urinated at both places in childhood or in adulthood due to unity of cause. In al-Fawa'id al-Zahriyya, there is a strange issue: If someone bought a young slave and found him urinating in the bed, he has the right to return. And if he became defective with another defect at the buyer's, he may claim compensation for the defect. Then if he claimed compensation, then [the defect] disappears due to reaching puberty: is there no narration about it? He said: And he deduced by two cases: One of them is if he bought a slave girl and found her having the problem of... (unclear). The father says: It is fitting that he should take back... The husband had the right to return her, and if she became defective with another [defect], he would claim compensation for the defect. Then if he claimed compensation and then the husband divorced her, the seller could claim back the compensation. Similarly, the second: He bought a slave and found him sick; he has the right to return. Then if he became defective with another defect, he claimed compensation for the defect. Then if he claimed compensation and then he was cured by treatment, he does not take back [the compensation]; otherwise he takes back. And puberty here is not like treatment, so it is fitting that he takes back. In al-Mi'raj and al-Nihaya.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof type, continuing the commentary on the defect of urinating in bed (al-bawl fi al-firash). The text explains that this is considered a defect, but it is absolute, covering both young and old. However, an exception is made for a child who does not yet have discernment (ghayr mumayyiz); his urination is not a defect because it is due to natural weakness. The key condition for it to be a defect is recurrence (mu'awadah) at the buyer's place in a single state: if the child urinated at the seller's house in childhood, and then after reaching puberty he urinates at the buyer's house, the buyer cannot return him because the childhood urination was due to weak bladder, while the post-puberty urination is due to an internal disease, making it a new defect. But if he urinated at both places in childhood or in adulthood, then it is the same cause and the buyer can return. The text then presents a complex case from al-Fawa'id al-Zahriyya: a buyer purchases a young slave who urinates in bed, so he returns him. If the slave then acquires another defect at the buyer's, the buyer can claim compensation for that defect. But if after claiming compensation, the original defect (urination) disappears due to puberty, can the seller claim back the compensation? There is no explicit narration, so the author uses analogy (istidlal) with two cases: (1) a buyer purchases a slave girl and finds a defect, returns her, but she becomes defective again at the buyer's; the buyer claims compensation, then the husband divorces her; the seller can reclaim the compensation because the defect disappeared. (2) A buyer purchases a sick slave, returns him, the slave gets another defect, compensation is claimed, then the slave is cured by treatment; the seller does not reclaim the compensation because the cure was through treatment (human intervention). But if the cure happened naturally (like puberty), the seller should reclaim it. The author concludes that for the urination case, since puberty is natural, the seller should reclaim the compensation. This is recorded in al-Mi'raj and al-Nihaya.

Key Terms
البول في الفراش (al-bawl fi al-firash): Urinating in bed, considered a defect (عيب) in sales when it recurs after maturity.
معاودة (mu'awadah): Recurrence of the defect, meaning it happens repeatedly, indicating it is a chronic condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The buyer can return the slave because it is the same defect.
B) The buyer cannot return because the post-puberty urination is a new defect.
C) The buyer can return only if the slave also urinated at the buyer's in childhood.
D) The buyer can return regardless because urination is always a defect.

Key Takeaway
Urinating in bed is a defect only if it recurs with the same cause; natural disappearance allows the seller to reclaim compensation.

Returning a Slave Due to Recurring Eye Defect
Intra-Hanafi dispute on defect recurrence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two Hanafi positions on when a recurring defect allows the buyer to return the slave girl, and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
A defect that clears and returns is treated differently depending on whose hands it returns into.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Ibn Nujaym resolves an intra-Hanafi conflict regarding whether a white spot that appears and disappears constitutes the same defect. The key factor is whether the second occurrence happens before or after the buyer takes possession, and whether the buyer had prior knowledge. We will trace the conflicting rulings from al-Ziyadat and al-Waqi'at al-Hussamiyya and identify the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting rulings
From al-Ziyadat: if the whiteness clears at the buyer's and then returns, the buyer cannot return because the second is considered a different defect. From al-Waqi'at al-Hussamiyya: if it clears and returns at the seller's before possession, the buyer can return because the second occurred in the seller's hands.
Both rulings are found in the text.
2
Understand the distinction based on possession
The key distinction is whether the defect returns in the seller's hands (before possession) or in the buyer's hands (after possession). If at the seller's, it is considered a new defect; if at the buyer's, it is considered the same defect that the buyer accepted.
Possession transfers responsibility for defects.
3
Recognize the role of buyer's knowledge
If the buyer knew of the defect at the time of sale and it later returns at the seller's, some say he cannot return because he consented. But the mufta bihi (from al-Waqi'at al-Hussamiyya) allows return because the second occurrence is a new defect.
The text cites al-Waqi'at al-Hussamiyya explicitly giving the buyer the right to return.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Ibn Nujaym sides with al-Waqi'at al-Hussamiyya, stating 'wa bihadha zahara an la ishkala' — the buyer can return if the defect recurs at the seller's. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
The author's conclusion resolves the contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى قاضيخان اشترى جارية وادعى أنها لا تحيض واسترد بعض الثمن ثم حاضت قالوا : إن كان البائع أعطاه على وجه الصلح عن العيب كان للبائع أن يسترد ذلك. وفيها أيضاً : اشترى عبداً فقبضه وحم عنده وكان يحم عند البائع. قال الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل : المسألة محفوظة عن أصحابنا أنه إن حم في الوقت الذي كان يحم عند البائع كان له أن يرده أو في غيره فلا قبل له فلو اشترى أرضاً فنزت عند المشتري وقد كانت تنز عند البائع كان له أن يرد لأن سبب النز واحد وهو تسفل الأرض وقرب الماء إلا أن يجيء ماء غالب أو كان المشتري رفع من ترابها فيكون النز غير ذلك أو يشتبه فلا يدري أنه عينه أو غيره. قال القاضي الإمام : يشكل ما في الزيادات اشترى جارية بيضاء إحدى العينين ولا يعلم ذلك فانجلى البياض عنده ثم عاد ليس له أن يرد وجعل الثاني غير الأول. ولو اشترى جارية بيضاء إحدى العينين وهو يعلم بذلك فلم يقبضها حتى انجلى ثم عاد وهو يشاورني فيما كان مشكلاً إذا اجتمعنا فشاورته في هذه المسألة فما استفدت منه فرقاً ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. فالحاصل ليس له الرد في المسألتين لكن في الأولى لجعله غير الأول إذ لو كان عينه لملك الرد لعدم العلم به، وفي الثانية لجعله عين الأول إذ لو كان غيره لملك الرد لكونه لم يرض به وفي جامع الفصولين : شراه فوجده يبول في الفراش يضعه القاضي عند عدل ينظر فيه. وفي الواقعات الحسامية : اشترى جارية فوجد في إحدى عينيها بياضاً فانجلى البياض ثم عاد فقبض المشتري وهو لا يعلم بذلك ثم علم فله هذا أن يرد، فرق بين وبين ما إذا قبض وفي إحدى عينيها بياض وهو لا يعلم ثم انجلى البياض ثم عاد ليس له أن يرد، والفرق أن البياض الثاني غير الأول حقيقة إلا أن في الصورة الأولى الثاني حدث في يد البائع فيوجب الرد، وفي الثانية البياض الثاني حدث في يد المشتري هذه المسألة فما استفدت منه (فرقاً قال في النهر : يمكن أن يقال And in Fatawa Qadikhan: He bought a slave girl and claimed that she does not menstruate, and he recovered part of the price, then she menstruated. They said: If the seller had given it as a settlement for the defect, the seller may take it back. And also in it: He bought a slave and took possession, and he had a fever at his place, and he used to have fever at the seller's. Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: The matter is preserved from our companions that if he had fever at the same time as he used to have fever at the seller's, he may return him; otherwise, no. So if he bought land and it became moist at the buyer's while it used to be moist at the seller's, he may return because the cause of moisture is one: the lowering of the ground and proximity of water, unless a strong water came or the buyer removed soil, then the moisture is different, or it is ambiguous and it is not known whether it is the same or different. Al-Qadi al-Imam said: What is in al-Ziyadat is problematic: He bought a slave girl with whiteness in one eye and he does not know that; the whiteness cleared at his place, then returned; he does not have the right to return, and he considered the second different from the first. And if he bought a slave girl with whiteness in one eye and he knows that, then he did not take possession until it cleared, then it returned at the seller's; the buyer does not have the right to return, and he considered the second the same as the first which he was satisfied with, if the second occurred at the seller's; and he did not consider it the same if the whiteness returned at the buyer's, and he said no, he does not return. Then al-Qadi al-Imam said: I used to consult Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, and he used to consult me regarding what was problematic. When we gathered, I consulted him about this issue and I did not benefit from a distinction. So it is in Fath al-Qadir. So the conclusion is that he does not have the right to return in the two issues, but in the first because he considered it different from the first, for if it were the same he would have the right to return due to lack of knowledge; and in the second because he considered it the same as the first, for if it were different he would have the right to return because he did not consent to it. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He bought him and found him urinating in the bed; the judge places him with a trustworthy person to inspect him. And in al-Waqi'at al-Hussamiyya: He bought a slave girl and found whiteness in one eye; the whiteness cleared, then returned; the buyer had taken possession without knowing that; then he knew; he has the right to return. There is a distinction between this and the case where he took possession and there was whiteness in one eye without him knowing, then the whiteness cleared, then returned; he does not have the right to return. The distinction is that the second whiteness is truly different from the first, except that in the first scenario the second occurred in the hands of the seller, so it necessitates the right to return; in the second scenario the second whiteness occurred in the hands of the buyer. This issue? I did not benefit from a distinction. He said in al-Nahr: It can be said that it is necessary that the second is different from the first, and yet he does not return if it returns at the seller's because the buyer consented to it; and there is no difference between the first and second where it did not increase and did not move to another place. Furthermore, the ruling that he does not return when it clears then returns in the hands of the seller is not agreed upon; rather, what is mentioned in al-Waqi'at al-Hussamiyya is that he does return. His statement: (And by this it is clear that there is no problem and no need for consultation). He said.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute type, covering various rulings on the defect of whiteness in the eye (bayad al-'ayn) and other recurring defects. The text begins by citing Fatawa Qadikhan: a buyer claims a slave girl does not menstruate, recovers part of the price, then she menstruates. The ruling: if the seller gave the compensation as a settlement for the defect, he can take it back. Then another case: a slave has fever at the buyer's and used to have fever at the seller's; the ruling from Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl is that if the fever occurs at the same time as before, the buyer can return; otherwise not. An analogy with land moisture is given. Then a problematic case from al-Ziyadat: a slave girl with whiteness in one eye; it clears then returns. The ruling in al-Ziyadat is that if the whiteness clears at the buyer's then returns, he cannot return, because the second whiteness is considered different from the first. But if the buyer knew of the whiteness and it cleared before possession, then returned at the seller's, the buyer cannot return because the second is considered the same as the first which he accepted. However, if the whiteness returns at the buyer's, it is not considered the same, so he can return. Al-Qadi al-Imam consulted Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani but found no clear distinction. The text then cites Fath al-Qadir: the conclusion is that the buyer cannot return in both scenarios, but for different reasons: in the first (buyer unaware), because the second is not the same as the first; in the second (buyer aware), because the second is the same as the first. Then a ruling from Jami' al-Fusulayn about urinating in bed: the judge appoints a trustworthy person to investigate. Then al-Waqi'at al-Hussamiyya provides a distinction: if the whiteness appears, clears, and returns before possession (at seller's), the buyer can return because the second whiteness occurred in the seller's hands; if it clears and returns after possession (at buyer's), the buyer cannot return because it occurred in his own hands. However, there is disagreement: some say the buyer does not return even if it returns at the seller's because he consented. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq concludes with the statement from the matn: 'And by this it is clear that there is no problem and no need for consultation', meaning the correct ruling is that the buyer can return if the defect recurs at the seller's, as per al-Waqi'at al-Hussamiyya.

Key Terms
بياض العين (bayad al-'ayn): A white spot or cataract in the eye, considered a defect if it affects vision or appearance.
انجلاء (injila'): Clearing or disappearance of a defect (e.g., whiteness).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi in this passage, what is the ruling when the whiteness in the eye clears before possession and then returns at the seller's?
A) The buyer cannot return because he consented to the first whiteness.
B) The buyer can return because the second whiteness is a new defect in the seller's hands.
C) The buyer cannot return because the whiteness cleared, so there is no defect.
D) The buyer can return only if he did not know about the first whiteness.

Key Takeaway
The right to return a sold item due to a recurring defect depends on whether the defect returns in the seller's custody (before possession) or in the buyer's custody (after possession), with the mufta bihi favoring return when it recurs at the seller's.

Recovery from Illness in a Purchased Slave: Effect on Reduction Claim
States ruling when a sick slave recovers after defect claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between natural and medicated recovery to determine whether a seller can reclaim a price reduction paid for a defect.

Core Idea
Natural recovery allows seller to reclaim reduction; medicated recovery does not.

This passage expands on khiyar al-'ayb by addressing a consequential question: what happens when the very defect that led to a price reduction disappears? The Hanafi ruling turns on the cause of recovery—whether it is natural or through medication—reflecting a nuanced understanding of defects and remedies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base scenario
Recognize that the buyer has already claimed and received a reduction for a defect (illness) found in a purchased slave.
This is the starting point before recovery occurs.
2
Identify the new event
The slave recovers from the illness. Determine the cause of recovery: was it through medication (human intervention) or natural (spontaneous)?
The distinction is decisive for the ruling.
3
Apply the ruling per recovery type
If recovery was through medication, the seller does not reclaim the reduction. If natural, the seller does reclaim it.
Remember: medication = no reclaim; natural = reclaim.
4
Understand the rationale
Medication introduces a new cause; the original defect was not a mere temporary condition. Natural recovery shows the defect was not permanent, so the reduction was unwarranted.
The illa (effective cause) is human intervention vs. nature.
5
Confirm the ruling's scope
This ruling applies specifically to the reduction claim, not to the option of return (radd). It also assumes no other defects intervened.
If a new defect appears, different rules apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية اشترى عبداً فوجده مريضاً له الرد فإذا تعيب بعيب آخر رجع بنقصان العيب، فإذا رجع ثم برئ بالمداواة لا يسترد وإلا استرد والبلوغ هنا لا بالمداواة فينبغي أن يسترد
The second: He bought a slave and found him sick; he has the right to return. If he becomes defective with another defect, he claims reduction for the defect. If he claimed reduction and then he recovered through medication, he does not recover (the reduction); otherwise he recovers. And recovery here is not through medication, so it is fitting that he recovers.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage states a ruling about a purchased slave who is found to be sick, giving the buyer an option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb). The buyer may either return the slave or keep him and claim a reduction in price for the defect. If the slave later acquires another defect, the buyer claims reduction for that new defect. If the buyer has already claimed reduction for the original illness and then the slave recovers from that illness through medication, the seller cannot recover the reduction paid. However, if the recovery occurs naturally without medication, the seller can reclaim the reduction. The reasoning is that recovery through medication indicates a new cause, while natural recovery suggests the illness was not a true defect at the time of sale.

Key Terms
الرد (ar-radd): The act of returning a defective item to the seller and cancelling the sale.
النقصان (an-naqsān): The reduction in price claimed by the buyer for a defect, calculated as the difference between the price and the value of the defective item.
البلوغ (al-bulūgh): Recovery from illness; here it refers to the condition of the slave returning to health.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, what is the effect of recovery through medication on the reduction claim?
A) Seller reclaims the reduction.
B) Seller does not reclaim the reduction.
C) The buyer must return the slave.
D) The buyer gets a full refund.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between natural and medicated recovery to determine if a seller can reclaim a reduction for a defect.

Recovering a Reduction Given as Compromise for a Defect Claim
Single case ruling on recovery of sulh payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a seller can recover a price reduction given as sulh for a defect claim that proves false.

Core Idea
A reduction given as sulh can be recovered if the claimed defect is proven false.

This lesson examines a case from Fatawa Qadikhan. When a buyer claims a defect and the seller agrees to a price reduction as a compromise (sulh), the transaction is not final if the claim turns out to be false. Understanding the nature of the sulh is critical to determining the seller's right to recovery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the claim
The buyer purchased a slave-girl and claimed she does not menstruate, implying a defect (barrenness).
2
Understand the action taken
The seller gave a reduction in price as a compromise (sulh) for the alleged defect.
Sulh is a settlement contract that resolves the dispute over the defect.
3
Note the subsequent event
Later, the slave-girl menstruated, proving the buyer's claim false.
4
Determine the ruling
The seller has the right to recover the reduction amount.
The ruling hinges on the reduction being given as sulh.
5
Identify the condition
The reduction must have been given specifically as a compromise for the defect, not as a simple discount.
If it was a simple discount, the ruling might differ.

Recurring Fever in a Purchased Slave
Determines if recurring fever is a defect allowing return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a recurring defect (fever) in a purchased slave must occur at the same time (season) as at the seller's to be considered a pre-existing defect warranting return.

Core Idea
A recurring fever is only the same defect if it strikes in the same season — timing determines cause.

In classical Hanafi sales law, when a purchased slave develops a fever after delivery, the buyer may wonder if this is the same illness the slave had at the seller's. The key indicator is not merely the recurrence, but the timing: if the fever occurs in the same season as it did with the seller, it is considered the same underlying defect; otherwise, it is a new illness. This lesson unpacks that reasoning through a simple rule: the time of year reveals whether the ailment is continuous or independent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the defect
The buyer takes possession of the slave, who then develops fever. The seller had previously observed fever in the slave. Determine if this fever is the same defect or a new one.
2
Examine the timing of the fever
Compare the time when the fever appears at the buyer's with the time it used to appear at the seller's. The passage uses 'al-waqt' meaning season or period.
The fever must recur at the same seasonal time (e.g., winter, summer) to be considered the same defect.
3
Apply the ruling
If the timing matches, the buyer may return the slave for defect. If it occurs at a different time, the buyer has no claim because it is a new illness.
The ruling is based on the idea that the underlying cause is the same if the pattern repeats at the same seasonal interval.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيها أيضاً : اشترى عبداً فقبضه وحم عنده وكان يحم عند البائع. قال الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل : المسألة محفوظة عن أصحابنا أنه إن حم في الوقت الذي كان يحم عند البائع كان له أن يرده أو في غيره فلا قبل له
And in it also: He bought a slave, took possession, and he had fever at his place, and he used to have fever at the seller's. Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: The matter is preserved from our companions that if he had fever at the time he used to have fever at the seller's, then he has the right to return him; otherwise (at another time), he has no claim.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage discusses a case where a purchased slave experiences fever after delivery, and it is known that the slave had fever previously at the seller's place. The question is whether this fever is the same defect or a new one. Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl reports that the Hanafi scholars (Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, etc.) ruled that if the fever occurs at the same time (season) as it did at the seller's, then it is the same underlying defect, and the buyer can return the slave. If it occurs at a different time, it is a new illness and not a defect pre-existing at sale, so the buyer has no right of return. This is based on the idea that the cause is the same.

Key Terms
حم (hamma): Fever; a recurring illness considered a defect if it was present at the seller's.
الوقت (al-waqt): The time of year or season when the fever typically occurs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the slave had fever before.
B) No, because it occurred at a different time.
C) Yes, but only if the fever is the same type.
D) No, because the buyer took possession.

Key Takeaway
A recurring defect is only actionable if it recurs at the same seasonal time as before the sale, indicating the same underlying cause.

Recurring Groundwater Seepage as a Defect
Ruling on khiyar al-'ayb for land seepage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for khiyar al-'ayb to land with recurrent water seepage, determining when the buyer may return the land.

Core Idea
Seepage is a defect only if the cause is the same — buyer's actions or new water sources change the cause.

When buying land, you may discover water seeping through the ground. If the seller had the same problem, you can return the land — but only if the cause is identical. This lesson explains how to determine if the seepage is truly the same defect or a new issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect
First, establish that the land has water seepage (nazz) and that it existed at the seller's house prior to sale.
The seepage must be a physical condition affecting the land's value.
2
Check the cause (sabab)
Determine the physical cause of the seepage — here, low elevation and proximity to groundwater. The Sameness of cause is essential for returning.
The text gives two examples of a natural cause being the same.
3
Examine buyer's actions
If the buyer raised the soil level or made other changes, the seepage after that is considered a new cause, not a defect. The buyer cannot return.
Buyer's interference severs the link to the original defect.
4
Consider external factors
If a new, overwhelming water source (e.g., an unusually heavy rain or a burst pipe) causes the seepage, it is also a new cause and not a defect.
The new source must be 'ghalib' (overwhelming) – not a normal fluctuation.
5
Handle ambiguity
If it is unclear whether the cause is the same or different, the buyer cannot return because the defect is not proven.
Burden of proof lies on the buyer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو اشترى أرضاً فنزت عند المشتري وقد كانت تنز عند البائع كان له أن يرد لأن سبب النز واحد وهو تسفل الأرض وقرب الماء إلا أن يجيء ماء غالب أو كان المشتري رفع من ترابها فيكون النز غير ذلك أو يشتبه فلا يدري أنه عينه أو غيره.
If he bought land and it seeped water at the buyer's, and it used to seep at the seller's, he has the right to return it because the cause of seepage is one: the lowness of the land and nearness of water, unless an overwhelming water comes or the buyer raised its soil, so the seepage is something else, or it is ambiguous so he does not know if it is the same or different.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage addresses a similar issue for land: if purchased land experiences water seepage (groundwater rising) and it had the same problem at the seller's, the buyer can return it due to the defect. The reasoning is that the cause is the same—the land's low elevation and proximity to the water table. However, if a new, overwhelming water source causes the seepage, or if the buyer altered the land (e.g., raised the soil level), then the seepage is considered a new cause and not the original defect. If it is ambiguous whether the cause is the same, the buyer cannot return because the defect is not proven.

Key Terms
نز (nazz): Seepage of groundwater to the surface; a defect in land.
سبب (sabab): Cause; here, the underlying reason for the seepage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A buyer purchases land that had water seepage at the seller's. After purchase, the buyer raises the soil level and seepage stops. Later, seepage occurs again. Can the buyer return the land?
A) Yes, because the seepage returned.
B) No, because the buyer's action changed the cause.
C) Yes, but only with a reduction in price.
D) No, because the seller is not responsible.

Key Takeaway
The right to return land for a recurring defect depends on whether the cause is identical, not merely the symptom.

Bedwetting as a Defect: Judge's Verification
Procedural ruling on verifying bedwetting as a defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the procedural ruling when a buyer claims a slave has a defect of bedwetting and explain the judge's role in verifying it.

Core Idea
A defect is only confirmed after investigation; the judge must appoint a just examiner.

In the Hanafi school, not every claim of a defect in a purchased slave is accepted without verification. The passage from Jami' al-Fusulayn addresses a specific example: bedwetting. Because bedwetting can be occasional or habitual, the judge must appoint a reliable person to determine its nature before any legal remedy is granted. This lesson examines that procedural ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A buyer purchases a slave and later discovers that the slave wets the bed. The buyer claims this is a defect.
Bedwetting is the alleged defect.
2
Understand the judicial procedure
The judge does not accept the claim at face value. Instead, he appoints a just person to observe the slave and determine if the bedwetting is habitual.
The text uses 'yad'ahu al-qadi 'inda adlin yanzuru fihi'.
3
Distinguish occasional vs. habitual
Only habitual bedwetting constitutes a valid defect. Occasional incidents are not grounds for return or reduction.
This distinction is crucial for the judge's investigation.
4
Apply the ruling
Once confirmed as habitual, the buyer may return the slave or seek a price reduction. If not, the claim is dismissed.
The judge's decision is based on the examiner's report.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He bought him and found him urinating in the bed; the judge places him with a just person to examine him.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage from Jami' al-Fusulayn states a procedural ruling: if a buyer discovers that a purchased slave has a habit of bedwetting, the judge should appoint a reliable and just person to investigate whether this is a chronic condition or an isolated incident. Bedwetting is considered a defect only if it is habitual. The judge's role is to verify the nature of the problem before allowing the buyer to return the slave or claim a reduction.

Key Terms

Whiteness in a Slave-Girl's Eye: The Ruling on a Recurring Defect
Intra-Hanafi dispute on defect recurrence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a defect which disappears and reappears may be treated as the same or a new defect depending on the buyer's knowledge and location, and determine the buyer's right of return.

Core Idea
A defect that disappears and reappears may be the same or new; the buyer's knowledge changes the ruling.

This passage from al-Ziyadat presents two scenarios of a slave-girl with a white spot (cataract) in her eye. The spot disappears and then returns, but the buyer's right to return depends on whether it is considered the same defect. The key factors are the buyer's knowledge and where the spot returns. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying rules of defects (uyub) in sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage describes two distinct scenarios: (1) the buyer is unaware of the whiteness at purchase; (2) the buyer is aware and consents. In each, the whiteness clears and then returns, but at different locations (buyer's vs seller's).
Focus on the differences: knowledge and location of return.
2
Note the ruling in each case
In both cases, the buyer cannot return the slave-girl. However, the reasoning differs: in case one, the returned whiteness is considered a new defect; in case two, it is considered the same defect the buyer consented to.
The outcome is identical, but the legal characterization differs.
3
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil)
In case one (unaware), if the returned whiteness were the same defect, the buyer would have the right to return because he did not know. To prevent that, the ruling treats it as a new defect, which was not present at sale, so no return. In case two (aware), if it were a new defect, the buyer could return because he did not consent to it. To prevent that, it is treated as the same defect he consented to.
The reasoning is outcome-driven to maintain consistency with principles of consent and knowledge.
4
Understand the scholarly difficulty
Al-Qadi al-Imam found this ruling problematic because the two characterizations seem inconsistent. He consulted Shams al-A'imma but received no clear distinction. The conclusion in Fath al-Qadir is that the buyer cannot return in either case, accepting the tension as part of the legal reasoning.
This shows that even great scholars grappled with nuanced cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال القاضي الإمام : يشكل ما في الزيادات اشترى جارية بيضاء إحدى العينين ولا يعلم ذلك فانجلى البياض عنده ثم عاد ليس له أن يرد وجعل الثاني غير الأول. ولو اشترى جارية بيضاء إحدى العينين وهو يعلم بذلك فلم يقبضها حتى انجلى ثم عاد عند البائع ليس للمشتري الرد وجعل الثاني عين الأول الذي رضي به إذا كان الثاني عند البائع، ولم يجعله عينه إذا عاد البياض عند المشتري وقال لا . يرده. ثم قال القاضي الإمام: كنت أشاور شمس الأئمة الحلواني وهو يشاورني فيما كان مشكلاً إذا اجتمعنا فشاورته في هذه المسألة فما استفدت منه فرقاً ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. فالحاصل ليس له الرد في المسألتين لكن في الأولى لجعله غير الأول إذ لو كان عينه لملك الرد لعدم العلم به، وفي الثانية لجعله عين الأول إذ لو كان غيره لملك الرد لكونه لم يرض به
Al-Qadi al-Imam said: What is in al-Ziyadat is problematic: He bought a slave-girl with whiteness in one eye, not knowing that, then the whiteness cleared at his place, then returned; he may not return, and they made the second other than the first. And if he bought a slave-girl with whiteness in one eye while knowing that, and he did not take possession until it cleared, then returned at the seller's; the buyer may not return, and they made the second the same as the first which he was pleased with, when the second was at the seller's; and they did not make it the same if the whiteness returned at the buyer's, and said he may not return. Then al-Qadi al-Imam said: I used to consult Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani and he would consult me about what was problematic when we met; so I consulted him about this issue, but I did not benefit from a distinction from him; thus in Fath al-Qadir. The conclusion: he has no return in both issues, but in the first because they made it other than the first, for if it were the same he would have the right to return due to lack of knowledge; and in the second because they made it the same as the first, for if it were other he would have the right to return because he did not consent to it.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage, from al-Qadi al-Imam, discusses a problematic ruling from al-Ziyadat regarding a slave-girl with whiteness (cataract) in one eye. Two cases: 1) The buyer is unaware of the whiteness at purchase. The whiteness clears at the buyer's, then returns. The ruling says the buyer cannot return, treating the returned whiteness as a new defect (different from the original). 2) The buyer knows of the whiteness and accepts it. Before delivery, the whiteness clears at the seller's, then returns while still with the seller. Here also, the buyer cannot return, but the ruling treats the returned whiteness as the same defect the buyer consented to. The inconsistency is that in the first case, it is considered a new defect, while in the second, the same defect. The Qadi al-Imam consulted Shams al-A'imma but got no distinction. Fath al-Qadir concludes that the buyer cannot return in either case, but the reasoning differs: in the first, because it is deemed a new defect (so no pre-existing defect); in the second, because it is deemed the same defect the buyer consented to.

Key Terms
انجلى (injilā): Cleared; the whiteness in the eye disappeared.
البياض (al-bayāḍ): Whiteness; a cataract or white spot in the eye, considered a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a buyer knew of the whiteness and it cleared before delivery then returned at the seller's, why can the buyer not return?
A) Because the whiteness is considered a new defect.
B) Because the buyer consented to the original defect, and the returned whiteness is considered the same.
C) Because the buyer took possession.
D) Because the seller is not responsible.

Key Takeaway
A defect that disappears and reappears may be legally characterized as the same or a new defect to achieve a consistent outcome: the buyer cannot return in either scenario.

Return Due to Recurring Defect Before Delivery
Ruling on recurring eye defect relative to possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on returning a sold item when a defect clears and reappears, and distinguish between pre- and post-delivery recurrence.

Core Idea
A defect that returns before delivery is a new defect with the seller, entitling the buyer to return.

A seemingly contradictory ruling is resolved: if a defect disappears and then returns before the buyer takes possession, the second occurrence is a new defect in the seller's hand, giving the buyer the right to return. Understanding the timing of the recurrence relative to delivery is crucial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and list the two cases: (1) whiteness found, clears, then returns, then buyer takes possession; (2) buyer takes possession while whiteness present (unaware), then it clears, then returns.
Focus on the order of events.
2
Locate the ruling for each scenario
Determine the legal outcome: in case (1) the buyer can return; in case (2) he cannot.
The text explicitly states the rulings.
3
Analyze the distinguishing factor
Find the reason ('الفرق'): the second whiteness is a new occurrence. Its location (hand of seller or buyer) determines liability.
The difference is not the nature of the defect but when and where it arises.
4
Apply the distinction to a new scenario
Consider: what if the whiteness reappears after delivery but the buyer had no knowledge? The ruling remains no return because it occurred in the buyer's hand.
The buyer bears the risk after delivery (qabḍ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الواقعات الحسامية : اشترى جارية فوجد في إحدى عينيها بياضاً فانجلى البياض ثم عاد فقبض المشتري وهو لا يعلم بذلك ثم علم فله هذا أن يرد، فرق بين وبين ما إذا قبض وفي إحدى عينيها بياض وهو لا يعلم ثم انجلى البياض ثم عاد ليس له أن يرد، والفرق أن البياض الثاني غير الأول حقيقة إلا أن في الصورة الأولى الثاني حدث في يد البائع فيوجب الرد، وفي الثانية البياض الثاني حدث في يد المشتري
And in al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyyah: He bought a slave-girl and found whiteness in one eye; the whiteness cleared, then returned, and he took possession not knowing that; then he knew; he has the right to return. There is a distinction between this and the case where he took possession and there was whiteness in one eye while he did not know, then the whiteness cleared, then returned; he does not have the right to return. And the difference is that the second whiteness is truly different from the first, except that in the first scenario the second occurred in the hand of the seller, so it necessitates return; in the second scenario, the second whiteness occurred in the hand of the buyer.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage from al-Waqi'at al-Husamiyyah introduces a distinction that clarifies the earlier problematic cases. If the whiteness clears and then reappears before the buyer takes possession (while still in the seller's control), the second whiteness is considered a new defect occurring with the seller, so the buyer can return. However, if the buyer takes possession while the whiteness is present (even if he doesn't know), and then it clears and later returns while with the buyer, the buyer cannot return because the second occurrence is in his own hand and thus not a pre-existing defect. This resolves the confusion mentioned earlier: the key is whether the second defect occurred with the seller or with the buyer.

Key Terms
القبض (al-qabḍ): Taking possession of the purchased item; delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer takes possession of a slave-girl with whiteness in her eye (without knowing), then the whiteness clears and later returns, can the buyer return her?
A) Yes, because the whiteness returned.
B) No, because the second whiteness occurred after delivery.
C) Yes, if the buyer did not know at the time of purchase.
D) No, because the whiteness cleared.

Key Takeaway
A defect that reappears before delivery is a new defect in the seller's hand entitling return; if after delivery, it is the buyer's risk.
