Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Kufr as the Worst Defect in Sales
Explaining that kufr is a defect and the Hanafi position against Shafi'i/Ahmad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-ayb when a buyer stipulates kufr and finds Islam, and distinguish the Shafi'i/Hanbali counter-argument.

Core Idea
Stipulating kufr waives the right to return if the slave is Muslim, because the condition is to absolve the seller from defect, not to require disbelief.

In Hanafi fiqh, kufr (disbelief) is considered a defect in a slave because it repels Muslim buyers and prevents the slave from being used for certain expiatory manumissions. This lesson examines the implications for khiyar al-ayb (option of defect) when the buyer stipulates a particular religion. We will explore the Hanafi ruling, its proof, and the opposing view of al-Shafi'i and Ahmad ibn Hanbal.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: kufr is a defect entitling the buyer to return the slave, unless the buyer stipulated kufr, in which case finding Islam does not allow return.
The key is that the condition is to absolve (tabarri) from the defect, not to guarantee kufr.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad hold that if the buyer stipulated kufr and finds Islam, he can still return the slave because he may have wanted the slave for lowly tasks.
Their proof is based on the buyer's potential benefit from having a kafir slave.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the opponent's dalil: the buyer's stipulation of kufr indicates a need for a slave who can be used in demeaning work, so finding Islam frustrates that purpose.
This is a practical consideration about the buyer's intent.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the condition is for tabarri, not a promise of kufr; analogous to buying a defective item and finding it sound, the buyer cannot return it.
The Hanafi school emphasizes the legal function of stipulations to absolve liability.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in the Hanafi school the sale stands, while in the Shafi'i/Hanbali school the buyer has the option to return.
This affects the validity of the sale and the buyer's rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكفر أقبح العيوب لأن المسلم ينفر عن صحبته ولا يصلح للإعتاق في بعض الكفارات فتختل الرغبة أطلقه فشمل كفر الغلام والجارية والنصراني واليهودي والمجوسي كما في النهاية . وما إذا شرط إسلامه فظهر كفره. أو أطلق وما إذا كان قريباً من بلاد الكفر أو من بلاد الإسلام ولو شرط كفره فظهر إسلامه لا يرده لأن الشرط للتبري من عيبه فصار كما إذا اشتراه على أنه معيب فإذا هو سليم، وحالفنا الشافعي وأحمد نظراً إلى أنه ربما اشترط كفره ليستخدمه في محقرات الأمور ولم أر حكم ما إذا وجده خارجاً عن مذهب أهل السنة كالمعتزلي والرافضي وينبغي أن يكون كالكافر لأن السني ينفر عن صحبته وربما قتله الرافضي لأن الرافضة يستحلون قتلنا. وفي السراج الوهاج : الكفر عيب ولو اشتراها مسلم أو ذمي .اهـ. وهو غريب الذمي قوله : (وهو غريب في الذمي قال الرملي نقلاً . الشيخ محمد الغزي: ليس بغريب لما تقرر أن العيب ما نقص الثمن عند التجار، ولا شك أن الكفر
And kufr is the ugliest of defects because the Muslim shuns his company and he is not valid for emancipation in some expiations, so desire is impaired. He [the author] made it absolute, so it includes the kufr of a boy, a girl, a Christian, a Jew, and a Magian, as in al-Nihayah. And [it includes] when he stipulated his Islam and he appeared to be a kafir, or [when he] was absolute, and when he was near the land of kufr or near the land of Islam. And if he stipulated his kufr and he appeared to be Muslim, he does not return him because the condition is to absolve from his defect, so it becomes like when he bought it with the condition that it was defective and it turned out sound. And al-Shafi'i and Ahmad opposed us, considering that perhaps he stipulated his kufr so that he could use him in lowly tasks. And I have not seen the ruling if he finds him outside the Sunni school like a Mu'tazili or Rafidi; it ought to be like a kafir because the Sunni shuns his company and perhaps the Rafidi kills him because the Rafida consider our killing lawful. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: Kufr is a defect even if a Muslim or a dhimmi buys her. End. And it is strange regarding the dhimmi. He said: 'And it is strange regarding the dhimmi.' Al-Ramli narrated from Sheikh Muhammad al-Ghazzi: It is not strange because it is established that a defect is whatever reduces the price among merchants, and no doubt that kufr...
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about whether kufr (disbelief) is a defect entitling the buyer to return a slave. The author first states the Hanafi position: kufr is a defect because it repels Muslims and prevents the slave from being used for expiatory manumission, which impairs the buyer's desire. This applies to any non-Muslim, including Christians and Jews. If the buyer stipulated Islam and found kufr, he can return; if he stipulated kufr and found Islam, he cannot return because the condition was to absolve from the defect, not to require kufr. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools disagree: they argue that the buyer might have stipulated kufr to use the slave for menial tasks, so finding Islam gives him a benefit. The author also notes that he hasn't seen a ruling on Mu'tazilites or Rafidis, but they should be treated like kafirs because Sunnis shun them and Rafidis even kill Sunnis. The passage ends with a quotation from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj stating kufr is a defect even for Muslim or dhimmi buyers, followed by a comment from al-Ghazzi explaining that a defect is anything that reduces price, and kufr does that.

Key Terms
الكفر (al-kufr): Disbelief; any state of being other than Muslim, including Christian, Jew, Magian, or heretic.
العيب (al-ayb): A defect that reduces the value or impairs the buyer's desire.
الشرط للتبري (al-shart li al-tabarri): A condition to absolve the seller from liability for a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer stipulates that a slave is a kafir and later finds the slave is Muslim, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The buyer can return the slave because the condition was not met.
B) The buyer cannot return the slave because the condition was to absolve from the defect.
C) The buyer can return the slave only if the seller knew.
D) The sale is void.

Key Takeaway
Stipulating a known defect (like kufr) in a sale absolves the seller from liability for that defect, so the buyer cannot return the item if the defect is absent.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Lack of Menstruation as a Defect in Sale
Explaining when lack of menstruation and istihadah are defects, with intra-Hanafi differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the conditions under which lack of menstruation is considered a defect in a slave-girl, and EXPLAIN the intra-Hanafi disputes regarding maximum age, minimum period, and the requirement of a specified cause (illness or pregnancy).

Core Idea
Lack of menstruation is a defect only if it indicates illness, not natural cessation.

This passage tackles a subtle area of khiyar al-ayb (option of defect) in the sale of a slave-girl: when does the absence of menstruation or the presence of istihadah (irregular bleeding) constitute a valid defect? The author, Ibn Nujaym, navigates the opinions of the early Hanafi masters and later commentators, revealing internal disagreements on age limits, the minimum period of stoppage, and whether the buyer must specify the cause (illness or pregnancy). Mastering this discussion will sharpen your ability to differentiate between the ruling itself and the procedural requirements for a claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its basis
Locate the statement that lack of menstruation and istihadah are defects. Note the causal reasoning: menstruation is the original healthy state, so absence indicates illness.
This is the foundational talil (causal reasoning) that governs the entire section.
2
Extract the intra-Hanafi disagreements
List the differences: (a) maximum age for considering stoppage (Abu Hanifa 17, Sahibayn 15); (b) minimum period for a valid claim (Abu Yusuf 3 months, Muhammad 4 months 10 days, Qadikhan 1 month); (c) whether the buyer must allege a cause (illness/pregnancy) for the claim to be heard.
These are not contradictory but reflect different methodological priorities.
3
Analyze the procedural framework
Trace the steps: the buyer's claim → specification of cause (if required) → questioning of seller → oath (or refusal) → possible return of the slave-girl. Note that in Zahir al-Riwayah the slave-girl's statement is not accepted; only Muhammad’s opinion adopts it.
Procedural rules affect the practical outcome even if the substantive ruling is the same.
4
Evaluate the Ibn al-Humam critique and the author’s response
Ibn al-Humam objected to the condition of alleging a cause, claiming Qadikhan did not require it. The author shows that Qadikhan’s two statements are not contradictory: the first governs hearing the lawsuit, the second governs the defect itself. Thus no conflict exists.
This demonstrates the method of reconciling apparent contradictions in fiqh texts.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical consequence: under the stricter view, a buyer who claims stoppage without specifying a cause does not have his claim heard; under Qadikhan’s view, if the stoppage lasts one month, the claim may be heard without a cause. The author leans toward the traditional view but notes Qadikhan’s preference in Fath al-Qadir.
The mufta bihi may vary by time and place; later Hanafi practice often follows Qadikhan.

قوله: (وعدم الحيض والاستحاضة) لأن انقطاع الحيض أو استمرار الدم علامة الداء لأن الحيض هو الأصل في بنات آدم وهو دم صحة فإذا لم تحض فالظاهر أنه عن داء بها ولهذا قالوا: لا تسمع دعواه بانقطاعه إلا إذا ذكر ما وجد عنده، فإن نكل ردت عليه وفي القنية : ولو وجد الجارية تحيض في كل ستة أشهر مرة فله الرد طم : إن كانت مغنية فله الرد اهـ. ثم اعلم أنه قد وقع من ابن الهمام خبط عجيب فإنه رد على الشارحين في موضعين : He said: 'And lack of menstruation and istihadah' because the stopping of menstruation or continuance of blood is a sign of illness, because menstruation is the original state in daughters of Adam and it is blood of health, so if she does not menstruate, the apparent cause is illness. For this reason they said: His claim that it has stopped is not heard unless he mentions its cause from illness or pregnancy. And for the elevation [of menstruation] the maximum age of puberty is considered: seventeen years according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and fifteen according to the two companions. This is known by the statement of the female slave because no one else knows it, but she is not believed without an oath from the seller; so she is returned by his refusal to take an oath if after possession, and if before it, similarly in the correct view. And if he claims it in a short period, it is not heard; the minimum is three months according to the second [Abu Yusuf?], and four months and ten days according to the third [Muhammad?], starting from the time of purchase. The summary: when his claim is established, the seller is asked; if he confirms, she is returned; otherwise he is not made to take an oath according to Abu Hanifa, as will come. And if he admits it and denies that it was due to... for the poor slave, and there is discussion in it. He said: 'It is returned in two cases: these are 'this adulteress did' or 'this is an adulteress' with tanwin. And it is not returned in two cases: 'O adulteress' or 'this adulteress' like that.' He said: 'And it is strange regarding the dhimmi.' Al-Ramli narrated from Sheikh Muhammad al-Ghazzi: It is not strange because it is established that a defect is whatever reduces the price among merchants, and no doubt that kufr... When he swears and refuses to take an oath, she is returned; and evidence is not accepted that the stoppage was with the seller because of certainty of their lying, unlike testimony on istihadah because it is the flowing of blood. And reference in pregnancy is to the statement of women, and in illness to physicians, and they are two just witnesses. This is what the commentator mentioned following al-Nihayah and al-Dirayah, but in them the reference to the statement of the female slave is only the saying of Muhammad, whereas in Zahir al-Riwayah, the female slave has no say in that. End. And from what we have established, it is apparent that the stoppage of menstruation is not a defect except when it occurs in its time, but its stoppage in childhood or menopause is not a defect, by agreement, as in al-Mi'raj. Qadikhan in his Fatawa considered the period of stoppage as a month, and this was preferred in Fath al-Qadir. Therefore Qadikhan did not require for the validity of a claim of stoppage to specify that it is due to illness or pregnancy. And he preferred it in Fath al-Qadir because even if it is not due to illness, it is a path to it. And the way to direct the lawsuit, according to what is correct in Fath al-Qadir, is that he claims its stoppage at present and its existence with the seller. If he denies its existence with him and admits the stoppage at present, the slave-girl is questioned; if she mentions that she is stopped, the lawsuit is directed, and he is made to swear that it was not with him; if he refuses, she is returned. And in al-Qunyah: If he finds the slave-girl menstruating every six months once, he has the option to return. Tam: If she is a singer, he has the option to return. End. Then know that Ibn al-Humam made a strange error, for he refuted the commentators in two places: first, their condition that the stoppage must be due to illness or pregnancy, and he claimed that the insightful jurist Qadikhan did not address it, but it is not as he claimed; rather Qadikhan in the Fatawa explicitly stated it first: He said: If a man buys a slave-girl and takes possession, then says she does not menstruate, the Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: The buyer's claim is not heard unless he claims the elevation of menstruation due to pregnancy or due to illness. If he claims due to pregnancy, the judge shows her to women; if they say she is pregnant, the seller is made to swear that it was not with him; if they say she is not pregnant, no oath. And in knowing illness, reference is to physicians, etc. This, as you see, is clear in what they transmitted, so how can his statement that he did not address it be correct? But he had another expression in the Fatawa after this by a page: He said: A man bought a slave-girl and took possession, and she did not menstruate with the buyer for a month or forty days. The Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: The elevation of menstruation is a defect, and its minimum is one month; if this amount is elevated with the buyer, he has the right to return if he proves that it was with the seller. End. So the two statements are from one person, Sheikh Imam Abu Bakr, but the first is for hearing the lawsuit with the judge, and the second is for establishing the defect itself, not for explaining its cause, so there is no contradiction.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage discusses the defect of lack of menstruation (adam al-hayd) and irregular bleeding (istihadah) in a purchased slave-girl. The author explains that the absence of menstruation or continuous bleeding indicates illness because menstruation is the natural state and signifies health. The buyer's claim of this defect is only heard if he specifies a cause like illness or pregnancy. The maximum age for considering the stoppage as a defect differs: Abu Hanifa says 17 years, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say 15. The slave-girl's statement is considered but an oath is required from the seller. The minimum period for a claim varies: three months (Abu Yusuf) or four months and ten days (Muhammad). If the claim is established, the seller is questioned; if he admits the defect, the girl is returned; otherwise, no oath is required from the seller according to Abu Hanifa. The passage also includes a detailed discussion about verifying the claim through the slave-girl's statement and the seller's oath. After that, the author critiques Ibn al-Humam's objection to the condition that the stoppage must be due to illness or pregnancy, showing that Qadikhan actually required that condition, but later gave another statement focusing on the defect itself rather than the cause, so there is no contradiction. The minimum duration for considering the stoppage as a defect is also disputed: Qadikhan considered one month sufficient, while others required longer. The passage also notes that if the slave-girl menstruates only every six months, the buyer has the option to return, especially if she is a singer.

Key Terms
عدم الحيض (adam al-hayd): Absence of menstruation in a slave-girl of menstruating age, considered a defect if it indicates illness.
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Irregular bleeding not considered menstruation; continuous bleeding is a sign of illness and a defect.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted opinions of the school's founders; in this context, it holds that the slave-girl's statement is not accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a necessary condition for a claim of defect due to lack of menstruation to be heard (according to the traditional view)?
A) The slave-girl must be under 15 years old.
B) The stoppage must have lasted at least one month.
C) The buyer must specify whether the stoppage is due to illness or pregnancy.
D) The seller must admit the defect.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi khiyar al-ayb, lack of menstruation is a defect only when it occurs during the age of menstruation and indicates illness; however, internal disputes exist on age limits, minimum duration, and the need to specify a cause, which must be understood to properly apply the ruling.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
12 min
Absence of Menstruation as a Defect: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on defect due to absence of menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi mufta bihi position considers one month of amenorrhea a defect and compare the opinions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Qadikhan on the minimum duration.

Core Idea
Absence of menstruation for a month is a defect even without proven cause—the repugnance itself gives the right to return.

When a buyer purchases a slave-girl and discovers she does not menstruate, does this constitute a defect (ayb) giving the right to return? The Hanafi school has internal differences on the minimum duration and whether the buyer must prove a specific cause (disease or pregnancy). This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rulings, the conflicting narrations, and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the general ruling on the defect
Begin by noting that cessation of menstruation during the usual age is considered a defect, but not if at childhood or menopause. Identify the procedural rule: the seller's oath is required, and evidence from witnesses is not accepted to prove the defect existed with him.
This establishes the default framework.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on minimum duration
List the different durations: Abu Hanifa and Zufar (2 years), Abu Yusuf (4 months 10 days then 2 months 5 days), Qadikhan (1 month), others (2 months, 9 months, etc.). Recognize that the relied-upon position is one month (or 40 days) according to Qadikhan and Fath al-Qadir.
Note the shift from istibra' reasoning to a straightforward defect claim.
3
Analyze the dispute on whether cause must be specified
Examine the two statements from Ibn al-Fadl: one requires specifying disease/pregnancy for the claim to be heard; the other treats absence alone as a defect. The text reconciles these as applying to different stages: first for hearing the claim, second for establishing the defect. The mufta bihi view does not require specification of cause.
This is the key point of the dispute.
4
Follow the response to Ibn al-Humam's criticism
Understand Ibn al-Humam's charge that earlier commentators erred in stipulating cause. The passage shows Qadikhan indeed mentioned cause in one place, but his second statement overrides for the defect itself. The criticism is rebutted as a gross error.
This reinforces the authority of the one-month, no-cause position.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical effect: if the buyer observes a month of amenorrhea, he can return the slave-girl without proving the underlying cause. This eases the buyer's burden compared to requiring a longer minimum or proof of specific illness.
Recognize why the preferred position favors the buyer.

Source Text

كتاب البيع باب خيار العيب
عنده حلف، فإن نكل ردت عليه ولا تقبل البينة على أن الانقطاع كان عند البائع للتيقن بكذبهم بخلاف الشهادة على الاستحاضة لأنها درور الدم والمرجع في الحبل إلى قول النساء، وفي الداء إلى قول الأطباء وهم عدلان؛ كذا ذكر الشارح تبعاً للنهاية والدراية ولكن فيها أن الرجوع فيها إلى قول الأمة إنما هو قول محمد، أما في ظاهر الرواية فلا قول للأمة ذلك في اهـ. وبما قررناه ظهر أن انقطاع الحيض لا يكون عيباً إلا إذا كان في أوانه، أما انقطاعه في سن الصغر أو الإياس فلا اتفاقاً كما في المعراج واعتبر قاضيخان في فتاواه مدة الانقطاع بشهر ورجحه في فتح القدير ولذا لم يشترط قاضيخان لصحة دعوى الانقطاع تعيين أن يكون داء أو حبل. ورجحه في فتح القدير لأنه وإن لم يكن عن داء فهو طريق إليه، وطريق توجه الخصومة على ما صححه في فتح القدير أن يدعي انقطاعه للحال ووجوده عند البائع فإن أنكر وجوده عنده واعترف بالانقطاع في الحال استخبرت الجارية، فإن ذكرت أنها منقطعة اتجهت الخصومة فيحلف ما وجد عنده، فإن نكل ردت عليه وفي القنية : ولو وجد الجارية تحيض في كل ستة أشهر مرة فله الرد طم : إن كانت مغنية فله الرد اهـ. ثم اعلم أنه قد وقع من ابن الهمام خبط عجيب فإنه رد على الشارحين في موضعين : الأول في اشتراطهم أن يكون الانقطاع عن داء أو حبل وزعم أن فقيه النفس قاضيخان لم يتعرض له وليس كما زعم بل قاضيخان في الفتاوى صرح به أولاً فقال : لو اشترى جارية وقبضها ثم قال إنها لا تحيض قال الشيخ الإمام أو بكر محمد بن الفضل : لا تسمع دعوى المشتري إلا أن يدعي ارتفاع الحيض بالحبل أو بسبب الداء، فإن ادعى بسبب الحبل يريها القاضي النساء إن قلن هي حبلى يحلف البائع أن ذلك لم يكن عنده، وإن قلن ليست بحبلى فلا يمين. وفي معرفة داء في إلى الأطباء إلى آخره. فهذا كما ترى صريح فيما نقلوه فكيف يصح قوله إنه لم يرجع يتعرض له لكن وقع له عبارة أخرى في الفتاوى بعد هذه بصفحة قال : رجل اشترى جارية وقبضها ولم تحض عند المشتري شهراً أو أربعين يوماً قال القاضي الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل : ارتفاع الحيض عيب وأدناه شهر واحد وإذا ارتفع هذا القدر عند المشتري كان له أن يرد إذا أثبت أنه كان عند البائع .اهـ. فالعبارتان لواحد وهو الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر لكن الأولى لسماع الدعوى عند القاضي. والثانية لتحقيق العيب في نفسه لا لبيان سببه فلا مخالفة
باطنها .
بهذه المثابة لأن المسلم ينفر عنه وغيره لا يرغب في شرائه لعدم الرغبة فيه من الكل اهـ. ويؤيده أنها لو ظهرت مغنية له الرد مع أن بعض الفسقة يرغب فيها ويزيد ثمنها عنده لذلك، وسيأتي أن ترك الصلاة وغيرها من الذنوب عيب قوله : فكذلك في الصحيح احترز به عما روي عن ابن يوسف أنها ترد قبل القبض بقولها مع شهادة القابلة وعما عن محمد إذا كانت الخصومة قبل القبض يفسخ بقول النساء؟ كذا في فتح القدير قوله : (والثانية لتحقيق العيب في نفسه الخ) يعني أنها لمجرد بيان
بينهما . الثاني في نقلهم أنه لا بد من مدة مديدة سنتان أو أربعة أشهر وعشر أو ثلاثة أشهر محتجاً بالعبارة الثانية لقاضيخان ولا اعتبار بها مع صريح النقل عن الأئمة الثلاثة ويمكن حملها على رواية أخرى، فنسبته لهم إلى الغلط غلط فاحش منه فالمعتمد ما نقله الشارحون في النهاية والعناية والدراية والبناية والتبيين والكافي وغيرهم. وفي البزازية : ارتفاعه بدون أحد هذين لا يعد عيباً. ونقل عن أبي مطيع أنه قدر المدة بتسعة أشهر، وسفيان بحولين. وفي التحفة : قدره بشهرين كما في غاية البيان فهي سبعة أقوال . ثم اعلم أنه لا منافاة بين قولهم يعتبر قول الأمة وبين قولهم والمرجع في الحبل إلى قول النساء وفي الداء إلى قول الأطباء لأن اعتبار قول الأمة إنما هو لأجل انقطاع الدم لتوجه الخصومة إلى البائع فإذا توجهت إليه بقولها وعين المشتري أنه عن حبل رجعنا إلى قول النساء العالمات بالحبل لتوجه اليمين على البائع وإن
أن ارتفاع الحيض عيب يثبت له به الرد، وهذه العبارة لا تنافي اشتراط بيان السبب في ثبوت الرد له وسماع دعواه فهي مطلقة فتحمل على الأولى لكن قال في النهر : ورأيت في المحيط أن اشتراط ذكر السبب رواية النوادر وعليه يحمل ما في الخانية اهـ . قلت : وفي شرح العلامة المقدسي نقل العلامة الرئيس قاسم بن قطلو بغا في شرحه للنقاية قال قاضيخان رجل اشترى جارية وقبضها فلم تحض عند المشتري شهراً أو أربعين يوماً قال القاضي الإمام : هذا ارتفاع الحيض وهو عيب وأدناه شهر واحد إذا ارتفع عند المشتري كان له أن يرد إذا ثبت أنه كان عند البائع، وهذا أوجه مما ذكره قاضيخان عن ابن الفضل ولو اشترى جارية وقبضها الخ». وقال في ملتقى الأبحر : وكذا عدم حيض بنت سبع عشرة سنة لا أقل، ويعرف ذلك بقول الأمة فترد إذا انضم إليه نكول البائع قبل القبض وبعده هو الصحيح. وقال في البدائع : وإن كان العيب لا يطلع عليه إلا الأمة لا يثبت بقولها لكونها متهمة وإن كان في داخل فرجها فلا طريق للوقوف عليه أصلاً فكان الطريق في هذين استحلاف البائع بالله ليس به هذا العيب للحال .اهـ. قوله : (الثاني في نقلهم أنه لا بد الخ) أقول : ذكر في الذخيرة أما إذا ادعى المشتري انقطاع حيضها وأراد ردها بهذا السبب لا يوجد لهذا رواية في المشاهير ثم قال : وبعد هذا يحتاج إلى بيان الحد الفاصل بين المدة اليسيرة والكثيرة قالوا: ويجب أن تكون هذه المسألة مدة الاستبراء إذا انقطع الحيض وفيها الرواية مختلفة فعن أبي يوسف أنه قدر الكثيرة بأربعة أشهر وعشر ثم رجع إلى شهرين وخمسة أيام، وعن أبي حنيفة وزفر سنتان الخ. وقد نبه على ذلك المحقق ابن الهمام فإنه بعد ما مر عن الخانية من تقدير المدة بشهر قال : وينبغي أن يعول عليه وما تقدم خلاف بينهم في استبراء ممتدة ،الطهر والرواية هناك تستدعي ذلك الاعتبار فإن الوطء ممنوع شرعاً إلى الحيض لاحتمال الحبل فيكون ساقياً ماءه زرع غيره فقدره أبو حنيفة وزفر بسنتين لأنه أكثر مدة الحمل وهو أقيس والحكم هنا ليس إلا كون الامتداد عيباً فلا يتجه إناطته بسنتين أو غيرهما من المدد لأن كونه عيباً كونه يؤدي إلى الداء وطريقاً إليه وذلك لا يتوقف على مضي مدة معينة مما ذكر .اهـ. ملخصاً وحاصل كلامه منازعة بعض المشايخ في قياس المدة لثبوت
النوعين هو .
Book of Sale, Chapter of Option of Defect. He [the seller] swears, and if he refuses, the [slave-girl] is returned to him, and evidence is not accepted that the [defect] was present with the seller due to certainty of their lying, contrary to testimony on istihada because it is a flow of blood. The reference in pregnancy is to the sayings of women, and in disease to the sayings of physicians, and they are [considered] just. Thus the commentator mentioned following al-Nihaya and al-Diraya, but in them that the reference to the sayings of the slave-girl is the saying of Muhammad, while in Zahir al-Riwayah there is no saying of the slave-girl in that. By what we have established it appears that the cessation of menstruation is not a defect unless it is in its [usual] time. As for its cessation at the age of childhood or menopause, it is not [a defect] by agreement, as in al-Mi'raj. Qadikhan in his Fatawa considered the duration of cessation as a month, and it was preferred in Fath al-Qadir, and therefore Qadikhan did not stipulate for the validity of the claim of cessation to specify whether it is due to disease or pregnancy. And he preferred it in Fath al-Qadir because even if it is not due to disease, it is a path to it. The path for the litigation, according to what Fath al-Qadir corrected, is that he claims her cessation at present and its existence with the seller. If he denies its existence with him and admits the cessation at present, the slave-girl is questioned. If she says she is cessated, the litigation is directed, and he [the seller] swears that it was not present with him. If he refuses, she is returned to him. And in al-Qunyah: If he finds the slave-girl menstruating every six months, he has the right to return. Also: if she is a singer, he has the right to return. Then know that Ibn al-Humam made a strange mistake: he criticized the commentators in two places. First, in their stipulation that the cessation is due to disease or pregnancy, and he claimed that the faqih Qadikhan did not address it. It is not as he claimed; rather Qadikhan in al-Fatawa explicitly stated it first: 'If someone buys a slave-girl, takes possession, then says she does not menstruate, said the shaykh imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl: The claim of the buyer is not heard unless he claims cessation of menstruation due to pregnancy or due to disease. If he claims due to pregnancy, the judge shows her to women; if they say she is pregnant, the seller swears that it was not with him; if they say she is not pregnant, no oath. And in knowing disease, it is to physicians, etc.' This is explicit in what they transmitted, so how can his statement be correct that he did not address it? But he had another phrase in al-Fatawa after this by a page: 'A man bought a slave-girl and took possession, and she did not menstruate with the buyer for a month or forty days. Said the judge imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl: Cessation of menstruation is a defect, and the minimum of it is one month. When it rises to this amount with the buyer, he has the right to return if he proves it was with the seller.' So the two phrases are from one, the shaykh imam Abu Bakr, but the first is for hearing the claim before the judge, and the second for establishing the defect itself, not for explaining its cause, so there is no contradiction in their inner meaning. Because a Muslim is repulsed by it and others do not desire to buy it due to lack of desire from everyone. It is supported that if she appears to be a singer, he has the right to return, even though some immoral people desire it and its price increases with them for that reason. It will come that abandonment of prayer and other sins are defects. His saying: 'Similarly in the correct [view]' is a reservation from what is narrated from Ibn Yusuf that she is returned before possession by her statement with the testimony of the midwife, and from what is from Muhammad if the litigation is before possession, it is annulled by the statement of women. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. His saying: 'and the second for establishing the defect itself' means it is merely for explanation. Between them, the second is in their transmission that a long period is necessary: two years, or four months and ten, or three months, using as evidence the second phrase of Qadikhan. But it is not to be considered given the explicit transmission from the three Imams. It can be carried on another narration, so his attribution of error to them is a gross error. What is relied upon is what the commentators transmitted in al-Nihaya, al-Inaya, al-Diraya, al-Binaya, al-Tabyin, al-Kafi, and others. In al-Bazzaziyyah: its cessation without one of these two [causes?] is not considered a defect. It is transmitted from Abu Muti' that he estimated the duration as nine months, and from Sufyan as two years. In al-Tuhfah: he estimated it as two months as in Ghayat al-Bayan. So these are seven opinions. Then know that there is no contradiction between their statement 'the saying of the slave-girl is considered' and their statement 'the reference in pregnancy is to the sayings of women and in disease to the sayings of physicians' because considering the saying of the slave-girl is only for the cessation of blood to direct the litigation to the seller. When it is directed by her saying and the buyer specifies that it is due to pregnancy, we revert to the sayings of women knowledgeable about pregnancy to direct the oath on the seller. Even though cessation of menstruation is a defect that establishes for him the right to return, this phrase does not contradict the stipulation of stating the cause in establishing the right to return and hearing his claim. It is absolute, so it is carried on the first. But in al-Nahr: 'I saw in al-Muhit that stipulating the mention of the cause is a narration from al-Nawadir, and upon it is carried what is in al-Khaniyyah.' I say: In the commentary of al-Maqdisi, transmitted by al-Qasim ibn Qatlubugha in his commentary on al-Nuqayah, Qadikhan said: 'A man bought a slave-girl, took possession, and she did not menstruate with the buyer for a month or forty days. The judge imam said: This is cessation of menstruation and it is a defect, and the minimum is one month. When it rises with the buyer, he has the right to return if he proves it was with the seller. This is more correct than what Qadikhan mentioned from Ibn al-Fadl.' And in Multaqa al-Abhar: 'Similarly, lack of menstruation of a girl of seventeen years or more is [a defect], and it is known by the saying of the slave-girl, so she is returned if accompanied by the seller's refusal to take an oath, before and after possession; this is the correct [view].' And in al-Bada'i': 'If the defect is not known except by the slave-girl, it is not established by her saying because she is suspect, and if it is inside her vagina, there is no way to know it at all, so the way in these two is to make the seller swear by Allah that this defect is not present now.' His saying: 'the second in their transmission that it is necessary' I say: In al-Dhakhirah: 'If the buyer claims cessation of her menstruation and wants to return her for this reason, there is no narration about this in the well-known [books]. Then he said: After this, there is need for clarification of the boundary between the short and long duration. They said: This issue must correspond to the period of istibra' when menstruation ceases, and in it the narrations differ: from Abu Yusuf that he estimated the long [duration] as four months and ten, then he reverted to two months and five days; from Abu Hanifa and Zufar: two years, etc. The verifying scholar Ibn al-Humam noted that after what passed from al-Khaniyyah of estimating the duration as a month, he said: 'He should rely on it. The previous difference among them is in istibra' of prolonged purity, and the narration there requires that consideration because intercourse is prohibited until menstruation due to the possibility of pregnancy, so he would be watering another's crop. Abu Hanifa and Zufar estimated it as two years because that is the maximum duration of pregnancy, and it is more analogical. But the ruling here is only that the length is a defect, so it does not require linking it to two years or other durations because its being a defect is that it leads to disease and is a path to it, and that does not depend on passing a specific period from what was mentioned.' Summarized. The gist of his speech is a dispute of some scholars in measuring the duration for establishing the two types.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a detailed intra-Hanafi discussion on whether the absence of menstruation (irtifaa' al-hayd) in a purchased slave-girl constitutes a defect giving the buyer the right to return her (khiyar al-'ayb). The text begins by outlining the general rule: if the buyer claims a defect of this nature, the seller is made to swear. If he refuses, the slave-girl is returned. Evidence from witnesses is not accepted to prove the defect existed with the seller because of the presumption of lying (since the seller would deny). This is distinguished from testimony on istihada (irregular bleeding) which is accepted. The reference for determining pregnancy is the statements of women, and for disease, the statements of physicians. However, there is a nuance: according to Imam Muhammad, the statement of the slave-girl herself is considered in this matter, but in Zahir al-Riwayah (the primary transmitted corpus), her statement is not considered. The commentator clarifies that cessation of menstruation is only a defect if it occurs during the usual age of menstruation; if it occurs at an age of childhood or menopause, it is not a defect by agreement. Qadikhan in his Fatawa considered a duration of one month as sufficient for the defect, and this was preferred in Fath al-Qadir. Therefore, Qadikhan did not require the buyer to specify whether the cessation is due to disease or pregnancy. The reasoning is that even if not from disease, it is a path to disease. The procedure for litigation is that the buyer claims the cessation exists currently and existed with the seller. If the seller denies existence with him but admits current cessation, the slave-girl is asked; if she confirms, the seller is made to swear; if he refuses, she is returned. Additional scenarios: if she menstruates only every six months, the buyer can return; also if she is a singer (since this is considered undesirable by upright people). The passage then critiques Ibn al-Humam's criticism of earlier commentators. Ibn al-Humam allegedly claimed that Qadikhan did not stipulate the cause (disease/pregnancy), but the text shows Qadikhan did explicitly mention it in one place, and in another place he mentioned a different statement that seems to omit the cause—but this is reconciled: the first is for hearing the claim, the second for establishing the defect itself. Thus no contradiction. Various scholars estimated different minimum durations: one month (Qadikhan), two months (al-Tuhfah), three months, four months and ten (Abu Yusuf), nine months (Abu Muti'), two years (Abu Hanifa, Zufar, Sufyan), etc. The relied-upon position according to the commentators is that the minimum is one month, and the condition of specifying the cause is a narration from al-Nawadir (rare transmissions). The passage also notes that the issue is analogous to the period of istibra' (waiting to ensure no pregnancy), but the ruling here is about defect, not prohibition of intercourse. Ibn al-Humam's conclusion is that the length is a defect because it leads to disease, and it does not depend on a specific duration; however, the practical ruling is that a month is sufficient. The overall mufta bihi position is that absence of menstruation for a month (or 40 days) is a defect, and the buyer can return the slave-girl if he proves it existed with the seller, without needing to specify the cause.

Key Terms
ارتفاع الحيض (irtifaa' al-hayd): Cessation or absence of menstruation; considered a potential defect in a slave-girl.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option of the buyer to return a purchased item due to a defect present at the time of sale.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The primary transmitted corpus of the Hanafi school, comprising the six books of Imam Muhammad.
الاستبراء (al-istibra'): A waiting period to ensure a slave-girl is not pregnant from her previous master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in this passage, which of the following is required for cessation of menstruation to be a defect in a purchased slave-girl?
A) The buyer must prove the cessation is due to a specific disease.
B) The cessation must last at least two years.
C) The cessation must occur during the menstruating age and last at least one month.
D) The slave-girl must confirm the cessation in court.

Key Takeaway
Absence of menstruation for one month during the menstruating age is a defect in a slave-girl, entitling the buyer to return her without specifying the cause.

Minimum Duration for Cessation of Menstruation as a Defect: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on intra-Hanafi dispute over the defect of prolonged cessation of menstruation with seven opinions and tarjih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven Hanafi opinions on the minimum duration for cessation of menstruation to be a defect and explain why the mufta bihi is one month based on the harm rationale.

Core Idea
Cessation of menstruation is a defect but its minimum duration is disputed: the mufta bihi is one month, not two years.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a heated intra-Hanafi dispute over how long a slave girl must stop menstruating for it to be considered a defect ('ayb) allowing the buyer to return her. Seven opinions are recorded, ranging from two years to one month. The commentator, Ibn al-Humam, favors one month because the defect is about harm and disease, not about preventing pregnancy. Understanding this tarjih is essential for grasping how later Hanafi scholars weigh conflicting analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Recognize that the cessation of menstruation (irtifa' al-hayd) is being evaluated as a defect ('ayb) that gives the buyer the right of return. The core question: how long must the cessation last to qualify?
This is not about the validity of the sale, but about the buyer's right to rescind.
2
List the seven opinions from the text
Extract each opinion mentioned: (1) Abu Hanifah and Zufar: two years; (2) Abu Yusuf: four months ten days (initial), then retracted to two months five days; (3) some: three months; (4) another: two months; (5) al-Qadi al-Imam: one month (preferred by Ibn al-Humam); (6) Abu Muti': nine months; (7) Sufyan: two years again. Count them carefully.
The text says 'seven statements' (سبعة أقوال).
3
Understand the basis of each opinion
Note that most opinions are derived by qiyas (analogy) on the istibra' period (waiting period to avoid mixing lineages). For example, Abu Hanifah used the maximum pregnancy duration (two years) for istibra', so he applied it here. In contrast, al-Qadi al-Imam focused on the harm (darrar) caused by prolonged absence, which does not require such a long time.
This is a clash of analogies: istibra' analogy vs. direct harm analogy.
4
Trace the tarjih mechanism
Ibn al-Humam argued that linking the defect to istibra' is inappropriate because istibra' aims to prevent pregnancy, while the defect is about physical harm and disease. The harm can manifest in a shorter time, so he adopted the one-month opinion from al-Qadi al-Imam as the most consistent with the rationale.
The tarjih is based on a critique of the illa (effective cause).
5
Apply the practical consequence
Under the mufta bihi, if a buyer notices that a slave girl has not menstruated for one month, he may return her (provided the defect existed at the time of sale). Under other opinions, he would have to wait much longer or lose the right.
This affects both the buyer's diligence and the seller's liability.

المدة بتسعة أشهر، وسفيان بحولين. وفي التحفة : قدره بشهرين كما في غاية البيان فهي سبعة أقوال . ثم اعلم أنه لا منافاة بين قولهم يعتبر قول الأمة وبين قولهم والمرجع في الحبل إلى قول النساء وفي الداء إلى قول الأطباء لأن اعتبار قول الأمة إنما هو لأجل انقطاع الدم لتوجه الخصومة إلى البائع فإذا توجهت إليه بقولها وعين المشتري أنه عن حبل رجعنا إلى قول النساء العالمات بالحبل لتوجه اليمين على البائع وإن أن ارتفاع الحيض عيب يثبت له به الرد، وهذه العبارة لا تنافي اشتراط بيان السبب في ثبوت الرد له وسماع دعواه فهي مطلقة فتحمل على الأولى لكن قال في النهر : ورأيت في المحيط أن اشتراط ذكر السبب رواية النوادر وعليه يحمل ما في الخانية اهـ . قلت : وفي شرح العلامة المقدسي نقل العلامة الرئيس قاسم بن قطلو بغا في شرحه للنقاية قال قاضيخان رجل اشترى جارية وقبضها فلم تحض عند المشتري شهراً أو أربعين يوماً قال القاضي الإمام : هذا ارتفاع الحيض وهو عيب وأدناه شهر واحد إذا ارتفع عند المشتري كان له أن يرد إذا ثبت أنه كان عند البائع، وهذا أوجه مما ذكره قاضيخان عن ابن الفضل ولو اشترى جارية وقبضها الخ». وقال في ملتقى الأبحر : وكذا عدم حيض بنت سبع عشرة سنة لا أقل، ويعرف ذلك بقول الأمة فترد إذا انضم إليه نكول البائع قبل القبض وبعده هو الصحيح. وقال في البدائع : وإن كان العيب لا يطلع عليه إلا الأمة لا يثبت بقولها لكونها متهمة وإن كان في داخل فرجها فلا طريق للوقوف عليه أصلاً فكان الطريق في هذين استحلاف البائع بالله ليس به هذا العيب للحال .اهـ. قوله : (الثاني في نقلهم الخ) أقول : ذكر في الذخيرة أما إذا ادعى المشتري انقطاع حيضها وأراد ردها بهذا السبب لا يوجد لهذا رواية في المشاهير ثم قال : وبعد هذا يحتاج إلى بيان الحد الفاصل بين المدة اليسيرة والكثيرة قالوا: ويجب أن تكون هذه المسألة مدة الاستبراء إذا انقطع الحيض وفيها الرواية مختلفة فعن أبي يوسف أنه قدر الكثيرة بأربعة أشهر وعشر ثم رجع إلى شهرين وخمسة أيام، وعن أبي حنيفة وزفر سنتان الخ. وقد نبه على ذلك المحقق ابن الهمام فإنه بعد ما مر عن الخانية من تقدير المدة بشهر قال : وينبغي أن يعول عليه وما تقدم خلاف بينهم في استبراء ممتدة ،الطهر والرواية هناك تستدعي ذلك الاعتبار فإن الوطء ممنوع شرعاً إلى الحيض لاحتمال الحبل فيكون ساقياً ماءه زرع غيره فقدره أبو حنيفة وزفر بسنتين لأنه أكثر مدة الحمل وهو أقيس والحكم هنا ليس إلا كون الامتداد عيباً فلا يتجه إناطته بسنتين أو غيرهما من المدد لأن كونه عيباً كونه يؤدي إلى الداء وطريقاً إليه وذلك لا يتوقف على مضي مدة معينة مما ذكر .اهـ. ملخصاً وحاصل كلامه منازعة بعض المشايخ في قياس المدة لثبوت النوعين هو
Between them. The second regarding their transmission that there must be a considerable period: two years, or four months and ten days, or three months, citing the second phrase of Qadikhan, and there is no consideration for it along with the explicit transmission from the three imams, and it is possible to interpret it as another narration. So attributing error to them is a clear error from him. So the relied-upon is what the commentators transmitted in al-Nihaya, al-‘Inaya, al-Diraya, al-Binaya, al-Tabyin, al-Kafi, and others. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: Its cessation without one of these two is not considered a defect. And it is transmitted from Abu Muti‘ that he estimated the period as nine months, and Sufyan as two years. And in al-Tuhfah: he estimated it as two months, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. So these are seven statements. Then know that there is no contradiction between their statement 'the statement of the slave girl is considered' and their statement 'the reference in pregnancy is to women and in disease to doctors' because consideration of the slave girl's statement is only for the cessation of blood to direct the litigation to the seller. So when litigation is directed to him by her statement and the buyer specifies that it is due to pregnancy, we return to the women who know about pregnancy to direct the oath upon the seller. And that the cessation of menses is a defect by which he is entitled to return. And this statement does not contradict the condition of explaining the cause in establishing the right of return and hearing his claim; it is absolute so it is interpreted as the first. But he said in al-Nahr: I saw in al-Muhit that stipulating mention of the cause is a narration from al-Nawadir, and on that is based what is in al-Khaniyyah. End. I say: And in the commentary of al-‘Allamah al-Maqdisi, a transmission from al-‘Allamah al-Ra’is Qasim bin Qutlubugha in his commentary on al-Nuqayah: Qadikhan said: A man bought a slave girl and took possession, and she did not menstruate with the buyer for a month or forty days. Al-Qadi al-Imam said: This is cessation of menses and it is a defect, and its minimum is one month. If it ceases after the buyer, he may return if it is established that it was with the seller. And this is more appropriate than what Qadikhan mentioned from Ibn al-Fadl, 'and if he bought a slave girl and took possession...' End. And in Multaqa al-Abhur: Similarly, the lack of menstruation of a seventeen-year-old girl, no less, and that is known by the statement of the slave girl, so she is returned if the seller's refusal is added, before or after possession; that is the correct view. And in al-Bada’i‘: If the defect is not known except by the slave girl, it is not proven by her statement because she is accused; and if it is inside her vagina, there is no way to know it at all, so the method in these two cases is to have the seller swear an oath by Allah that he does not have this defect at present. End. His statement: (The second regarding their transmission...). I say: In al-Dhakhirah: If the buyer claims cessation of her menses and wishes to return her for this reason, there is no transmission on this among the well-known (mashahir) reports. Then he said: After this, there is need to clarify the boundary between a short and a long period. They said: This issue should be the period of istibra’ when menses ceases, and in that the transmissions differ. According to Abu Yusuf, he estimated the long period as four months and ten days, then he retracted to two months and five days. According to Abu Hanifah and Zufar, two years, etc. And al-Muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam drew attention to this, for after what was mentioned from al-Khaniyyah of estimating the period as a month, he said: It is appropriate to rely on it. What preceded is a disagreement among them regarding istibra’ of an extended period of purity, and the transmission there necessitates that consideration, because sexual intercourse is legally prohibited until menstruation due to the possibility of pregnancy, so he would be watering the crop of another. So Abu Hanifah and Zufar estimated it as two years because it is the maximum period of pregnancy, and it is more analogical. And the ruling here is only that extension is a defect, so it is not appropriate to link it to two years or other periods, because its being a defect is its leading to disease and a path to it, and that does not depend on the passing of a specific period from what was mentioned. End. Summarized. The conclusion of his words is a dispute of some mashayikh regarding analogizing the period for establishing the two types.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that the cessation of menstruation in a slave girl is a defect ('ayb) giving the buyer the right to return her. The author discusses the dispute over the minimum period that constitutes a defect. Several opinions are presented: two years (Abu Hanifah and Zufar), four months and ten days (Abu Yusuf, later retracted to two months and five days), three months, two months, one month (al-Qadi al-Imam, relied upon by Ibn al-Humam), nine months (Abu Muti‘), and two years again (Sufyan). The relied-upon (mu'tamad) according to the commentators is the transmission from the three imams that a considerable period is needed, but Ibn al-Humam favors one month. The passage also addresses the reliance on the testimony of the slave girl and medical experts, and distinguishes between the defect ruling and the istibra' ruling harmoniously.

Key Terms
ارتفاع الحيض (irtifa' al-hayd): Cessation of menstruation, considered a defect in a slave girl if prolonged.
عيب ('ayb): Defect that gives the buyer the option to return the item in a sale.
استبراء (istibra'): Waiting period to confirm a slave girl is not pregnant before intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mu'tamad) position in the Hanafi school as per this passage, what is the minimum duration for the cessation of menstruation to be considered a defect?
A) Two years
B) One month
C) Four months and ten days
D) Nine months

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's mufta bihi is that one month of ceased menstruation is sufficient for a defect, diverging from longer periods based on istibra'.

Defects in a Slave: Chronic Cough, Debt, and Eye Diseases
Enumeration of defects with conditions for the right of return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a chronic cough, debt, or eye condition qualifies as a defect giving the right of return, and apply these criteria to new scenarios.

Core Idea
A condition is a defect only if it arises from disease and is severe, excluding habitual or minor issues.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq continues the list of defects ('uyūb) that allow a buyer to return a slave or claim compensation. The author specifies precise conditions: a chronic cough is a defect only if from disease and severe; debt is a defect if due immediately, not if deferred; any eye condition impairing sight is a defect. The text also refutes al-Shāfi‘ī’s distinction on deferred debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect and its definition
Read the text for each defect (cough, debt, eye diseases) and note how the author defines it. For example, 'chronic cough' (al-su‘āl al-qadīm) is defined as coming from disease (an dā'), not habitual.
The word 'qadīm' (old) here means 'from disease', not just old in time.
2
Determine the condition for it being a defect
For each defect, extract the condition that makes it a defect. For cough: must be from disease and severe (fahish). For debt: must be due immediately (ḥāl), not deferred (mu’ajjal). For eye diseases: any condition that weakens sight.
The text contrasts with al-Shāfi‘ī who considers deferred debt a defect; Hanafi rejects that.
3
Identify exceptions and nuances
Note when a defect is not considered: mild cough, negligible debt, or if the defect is cured (e.g., debt paid before return). Also note the additional defects from al-Mi‘rāj and their conditions.
The author adds many defects: warts, ugly moles, extreme redness of hair, etc., each with conditions.
4
Apply the criteria to a new scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., a slave has a mild cough not from disease), decide whether it is a defect. Use the conditions from the text.
Apply the principle: severity and disease-origin are key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القديم والدين والشعر والماء في العين فلو حدث آخر عند المشتري رجع بنقصانه أو رد برضا عين كان فتح في أنه لمين من كان ما داء رجعنا إلى قول الأطباء كذلك كما لا يخفى قوله : (والسعال القديم) وهو عن عن داء أما المعتاد فلا كما في فتح القدير. وظاهر الكتاب أن الحادث منه ليس بعيب ولو كان موجوداً عندهما، والظاهر أن ما كان داء فهو قديم وأن هذا هو مراده من كونه قديماً فالمنظور إليه كونه عن داء لا القدم ولذا قال في جامع الفصولين: السعال عيب إن فحش وإلا فلا اهـ (حكاية) في المستظرف : خطب المأمون بمرو فسعل الناس فنادى ألا به سعال فليتداو بشرب خل الخمر ففعلوا فانقطع عنهم السعال قوله : (والدين) لأن ماليته تكون مشغولة به والغرماء مقدمون على المولى. أطلقه فشمل دين العبد والجارية وما إذا كان مطالباً به للحال أو متأخراً إلى ما بعد العتق وفرق بينهما الشافعي وهو حسن إذ لا ضرر على المولى في الثاني وجوابه أنه يلحقه ضرر بنقصان ميراثه منه حيث كان وارثاً له؛ كذا في القدير. وهو بحث منه مخالف للنقل . قال مسكين والدين أي الدين الذي يطالب به الحال، أما الدين المؤجل فإنه ليس بعيب؛ كذا في الذخيرة والمراد المؤجل إلى العتق . وفي القنية : الدين عيب إلا إذا كان يسيراً لا يعد مثله نقصاناً. وفي السراج الوهاج : إذا كان على العبد دين أو في رقبته جناية فهو عيب لأنه يجب بيعه فيه ودفعه فيها فتستحق رقبته بذلك، ويتصور هذا فيما إذا حدثت به الجناية بعد العقد قبل القبض، أما إذا كانت قبل العقد فبالبيع يصير البائع مختاراً للجناية، فإن قضى المولى الدين قبل الرد سقط الرد لأن المعنى الموجب للرد قد زال .اهـ. وكذا إذا أبرأ الغريم كما في البزازية قوله : (والشعر والماء في العين) لأنهما يضعفان البصر ويورثان العمى ولا خصوصية لهما بل كل مرض بالعين فهو عيب ومنه السبل كما في المعراج وكثرة الدمع. وقد ذكر المصنف أولاً ضابط العيب ثم ذكر عدداً من العيوب ولم يستوفها لكثرتها فلا بأس بتعداد ما اطلعنا عليه في كلامهم تكثيراً للفوائد ولكثرة الاحتياج إليها في المعاملات. ففي المعراج الثؤلول عيب، وكذا الخال إن كان قبيحاً ،منقصاً والصهوبة حمرة الشعر إذا فحش بحيث تضرب إلى البياض، والشمط وهو اختلاط البياض بالسواد في الشعر في غير أوانه دليل الداء وفي أوانه دليل الكبر، والعشى عيب وهو ضعف البصر بحيث لا يبصر في الليل والسن الساقط ضرساً كان أو غيره، وكذا السوداء والظفر الأسود المنقص للثمن، والعسر وهو العمل باليسار دون اليمين عجزاً لا أن يكون عسر يسر وهو الأضبط الذي يعمل بهما، وقد كان عمر رضي الله تعالى عنه بهذه
The old and debt and hair and water in the eye. So if another defect occurs at the buyer, he returns by its depreciation or returns with consent... The statement: (And the chronic cough) and it is from disease; as for the habitual, it is not, as in Fath al-Qadir. The apparent meaning of the book is that the newly occurring one is not a defect, even if it exists with both. And the apparent is that what is a disease is old, and this is his intent in calling it old. So the consideration is that it is from disease, not oldness. Hence it is said in Jami‘ al-Fusulayn: Cough is a defect if it is severe, otherwise no. (Anecdote) in al-Mustazraf: al-Ma’mun delivered a sermon in Marw and people coughed. So he announced: Indeed, whoever has a cough should treat it by drinking vinegar wine. So they did, and their cough stopped. His statement: (And debt) because his market value is occupied by it, and creditors are prioritized over the master. He left it general, so it includes the debt of a male slave and female slave, and whether it is due immediately or deferred until after emancipation. Al-Shafi‘i differentiated between them, and it is good because there is no harm to the master in the second. The answer is that harm occurs due to the decrease in inheritance from him if he is his heir. Thus it is in al-Qadir. And it is a discussion from him contrary to transmission. Miskin said: And debt means debt that is demanded immediately. As for deferred debt, it is not a defect. Thus in al-Dhakhirah, meaning deferred until emancipation. And in al-Qunyah: Debt is a defect unless it is insignificant, not considered a decrease. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: If a slave has a debt or a crime upon his neck, it is a defect because he must be sold to pay it, so his neck becomes entitled. This is conceivable if the crime occurs after the contract before delivery. As for if before the contract, by the sale the seller becomes liable for the crime. If the master pays the debt before return, the right of return lapses because the cause for return has ceased. Similarly if the creditor releases, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. His statement: (And hair and water in the eye) because they weaken sight and cause blindness. They are not specific; rather every eye disease is a defect, including cataract as in al-Mi‘raj and excessive tearing. The author first mentioned the definition of defect, then mentioned a number of defects and did not exhaust them because of their abundance. So it is good to enumerate what we have come across in their statements to increase benefits and due to great need in transactions. In al-Mi‘raj: Wart is a defect, also a mole if it is ugly and reducing value, redness of hair if excessive such that it inclines to whiteness, and speckled hair (shamat) which is mixing of white and black in hair at other than its time indicative of disease, and at its time indicative of old age, and night blindness (ash) is a defect, which is weakness of sight such that he does not see at night, and a fallen tooth, molar or otherwise, also a black tooth, and a black nail that reduces price, and being left-handed (usr) which is working with the left hand out of inability, not that one is ambidextrous, which is more precise and works with both. 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) had this.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage continues the enumeration of defects in a slave that give the buyer the option of return. It covers chronic cough (only if from disease and severe), debt (if due immediately or significant), and eye diseases (any condition impairing sight). The commentary explains nuances: a chronic cough is a defect only if it is severe and from disease, not habitual. Debt is a defect unless it is deferred or insignificant. Eye diseases include any weakness, not just specific ones. The author then adds additional defects from al-Mi‘raj: warts, ugly moles, extreme redness of hair, mixed gray hair at a young age, night blindness, missing teeth, black teeth, black nails, and left-handedness due to inability. The passage also refutes al-Shafi‘i's distinction between immediate and deferred debt, arguing that even deferred debt harms the master through inheritance reduction.

Key Terms
السعال القديم (al-su‘āl al-qadīm): Chronic cough that arises from disease; considered a defect if severe.
الدين (al-dayn): Debt owed by the slave; if due immediately, it is a defect; if deferred until emancipation, it is not a defect per the mu'tamad.
الشعر والماء في العين (al-sha‘r wa al-ma' fi al-‘ayn): Hair or fluid in the eye that weakens vision; considered defects, as are all eye diseases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT considered a defect in a slave?
A) A severe chronic cough from disease
B) A debt of 5 dirhams due immediately
C) A mole that is ugly and reduces value
D) A mild habit of coughing not from disease

Key Takeaway
A condition qualifies as a defect ('ayb) in a slave only if it reduces value and meets specific severity or disease-based criteria.

Defects in Sale: Definition and Examples
States the Hanafi rule for defects in sale and lists examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for recognizing defects in a sale and identify whether specific conditions (cough, debt, eye issues) qualify as defects.

Core Idea
A defect in sale is any pre-existing flaw that diminishes value or utility, including hidden diseases, debts, and eye impairments.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq explains the general rule (dabit) for defects in sale and provides practical examples. It clarifies that a defect is not limited to physical deformities; functional impairments and financial encumbrances also count. The author enumerates specific defects from earlier works, ensuring the buyer's right to return (khiyar al-ayb) is properly understood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Determine the definition of a defect: any pre-existing flaw that reduces the value or utility of the sold item.
The rule is implicit in the examples given.
2
Examine the first examples
Analyze old cough, debt, and hair/water in eye: why each is a defect. Old cough from disease; debt encumbers value; eye flaws impair sight.
Habitual cough is excluded.
3
Note exceptions and nuances
Distinguish disease-based cough vs habitual; immediate vs deferred debt; the mufta bihi on deferred debt.
There is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on deferred debt.
4
Understand the expansion
The author transitions to a longer list from other works, emphasizing that the list is not exhaustive.
Examples: warts, moles, red hair, night blindness, etc.
5
Apply the rule
Given a hypothetical scenario, determine if a condition qualifies as a defect based on the rule.
Focus on whether the flaw existed before sale and diminishes value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب خيار العيب
القديم والدين والشعر والماء في العين فلو حدث آخر عند المشتري رجع بنقصانه أو رد برضا عين كانت فتح في أنه لمين من كان ما داء رجعنا إلى قول الأطباء كذلك كما لا يخفى قوله : (والسعال القديم) وهو عن عن داء أما المعتاد فلا كما في فتح القدير. وظاهر الكتاب أن الحادث منه ليس بعيب ولو كان موجوداً عندهما، والظاهر أن ما كان داء فهو قديم وأن هذا هو مراده من كونه قديماً فالمنظور إليه كونه عن داء لا القدم ولذا قال في جامع الفصولين: السعال عيب إن فحش وإلا فلا اهـ (حكاية) في المستظرف : خطب المأمون بمرو فسعل الناس فنادى ألا به سعال فليتداو بشرب خل الخمر ففعلوا فانقطع عنهم السعال قوله : (والدين) لأن ماليته تكون مشغولة به والغرماء مقدمون على المولى. أطلقه فشمل دين العبد والجارية وما إذا كان مطالباً به للحال أو متأخراً إلى ما بعد العتق وفرق بينهما الشافعي وهو حسن إذ لا ضرر على المولى في الثاني وجوابه أنه يلحقه ضرر بنقصان ميراثه منه حيث كان وارثاً له؛ كذا في القدير. وهو بحث منه مخالف للنقل . قال مسكين والدين أي الدين الذي يطالب به الحال، أما الدين المؤجل فإنه ليس بعيب؛ كذا في الذخيرة والمراد المؤجل إلى العتق . وفي القنية : الدين عيب إلا إذا كان يسيراً لا يعد مثله نقصاناً. وفي السراج الوهاج : إذا كان على العبد دين أو في رقبته جناية فهو عيب لأنه يجب بيعه فيه ودفعه فيها فتستحق رقبته بذلك، ويتصور هذا فيما إذا حدثت به الجناية بعد العقد قبل القبض، أما إذا كانت قبل العقد فبالبيع يصير البائع مختاراً للجناية، فإن قضى المولى الدين قبل الرد سقط الرد لأن المعنى الموجب للرد قد زال .اهـ. وكذا إذا أبرأ الغريم كما في البزازية قوله : (والشعر والماء في العين) لأنهما يضعفان البصر ويورثان العمى ولا خصوصية لهما بل كل مرض بالعين فهو عيب ومنه السبل كما في المعراج وكثرة الدمع. وقد ذكر المصنف أولاً ضابط العيب ثم ذكر عدداً من العيوب ولم يستوفها لكثرتها فلا بأس بتعداد ما اطلعنا عليه في كلامهم تكثيراً للفوائد ولكثرة الاحتياج إليها في المعاملات. ففي المعراج الثؤلول عيب، وكذا الخال إن كان قبيحاً ،منقصاً والصهوبة حمرة الشعر إذا فحش بحيث تضرب إلى البياض، والشمط وهو اختلاط البياض بالسواد في الشعر في غير أوانه دليل الداء وفي أوانه دليل الكبر، والعشى عيب وهو ضعف البصر بحيث لا يبصر في الليل والسن الساقط ضرساً كان أو غيره، وكذا السوداء والظفر الأسود المنقص للثمن، والعسر وهو العمل باليسار دون اليمين عجزاً لا أن يكون عسر يسر وهو الأضبط الذي يعمل بهما، وقد كان عمر رضي الله تعالى عنه بهذه
Old [cough], debt, and hair and water in the eye. If another [defect] occurs with the buyer, he may demand its diminution or return with consent... The old cough is from a disease; as for the habitual, no. It is apparent in the book that the accidental is not a defect... Debt: because his value is occupied by it and creditors take precedence over the master... Hair and water in the eye: because they weaken sight and cause blindness. The author first mentioned the rule of defect, then mentioned a number of defects... In al-Mi'raj: wart is a defect, similarly a mole if ugly and diminishing... and has been Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) with this.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement defining defects (uyub) in the context of khiyar al-ayb (option of defect) in sales. The author states the general rule that any defect that existed before the sale gives the buyer the right to return the item or claim compensation for the reduction in value. Specific examples are given: old cough (sual qadim) which is a defect if it originates from a disease, not a habitual cough; debt on a slave is a defect because it encumbers the slave's value, and creditors take precedence over the buyer; hair and water in the eye are defects because they weaken sight. The author then transitions to enumerating many defects from various sources, starting with warts, moles, red hair, mixed grey hair, night blindness, fallen teeth, black nails, and left-handedness. The mention of Caliph Umar having a left-handedness example is noted. The passage emphasizes that the list is not exhaustive but provides practical examples for transactions.

Key Terms
الصهوبة (al-suhubah): Redness of hair that is excessive to the point of tending to whiteness, considered a defect.
الشمط (al-shamat): Mixed white and black hair out of its proper time, indicating disease; in proper time indicating old age.
العشى (al-'asha): Night blindness, weakness of sight such that one cannot see at night.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT considered a defect in a sale?
A) Old cough from disease
B) Debt on a slave
C) Habitual cough
D) Water in the eye

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a defect (ayb) is any pre-existing flaw that diminishes the value or utility of the sold item, including hidden diseases, debts, and functional impairments.

Dispute on Istibra' as a Defect in Sale
Intra-Hanafi critique on dismissing istibra' as a defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether istibra' is a defect and evaluate the author's criticism of the opposing view.

Core Idea
Istibra' as a defect is not explicitly ruled by the three imams, but later Hanafis differ.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a subtle point in Hanafi sales law: does the mandatory waiting period (istibra') for a female slave constitute a defect that gives the buyer an option to return? The author criticizes a statement that dismisses istibra' as a defect, arguing that such dismissal is unwarranted given the transmissions from the three founding imams on the issue of istibra' itself. You will learn the structure of this dispute and how later scholars handle the absence of explicit transmission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine what is being disputed: whether the period of istibra' (waiting to ascertain non-pregnancy) is considered a defect in the context of khiyar al-ayb (option of defect).
2
Locate the absence of transmission
The author notes that there is no explicit narration from the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani) on this specific point; the transmissions are about the ruling of istibra' itself, not its effect as a defect.
This absence forces later scholars to perform tarjih among differing opinions.
3
Analyze the author's criticism
The author criticizes the statement 'no consideration' (la i'tibara biha) by pointing out that since the three imams spoke about istibra', it cannot be ignored. Understand the logic: if the imams considered istibra' significant for other rulings, it may also affect the option of defect.
4
Understand the need for tarjih
Because later scholars (mashayikh) differed on the period of istibra' and its legal effect, a process of preference (tarjih) is required. Ibn al-Humam is mentioned as a key figure who engaged in this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العيب على مدة الاستبراء بإبداء الفارق بينهما، وقد علمت أن أصل المسألة لا رواية لها في المشاهير، فإذا اختلف المشايخ في تقدير هذه المدة احتيج إلى ترجيح أحد القولين، والمحقق ابن الهمام من رجال هذه الكتيبة. وبما قررناه ظهر أنه لم يوجد النقل عن أئمتنا الثلاثة في مسألتنا وإنما النقل عنهم في مسألة الاستبراء فكيف يسوغ للمؤلف أن يقول ولا اعتبار بها مع صريح النقل عن الأئمة الثلاثة
The defect regarding the period of istibra' by demonstrating the difference between them. You have known that the origin of the issue has no narration in the well-known [books]. So when the early scholars differed in estimating this period, it was necessary to prefer one of the two opinions. The scholar Ibn al-Humam is among the men of this battalion. And by what we have established, it appears that there is no transmission from our three imams in our issue; rather, the transmission from them is in the issue of istibra'. So how does the author permit himself to say 'no consideration' while there is explicit transmission from the three imams?
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the period of istibra' (waiting to ascertain non-pregnancy of a female slave) should be considered a defect in the context of khiyar al-ayb. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq criticizes another author (possibly the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) for dismissing the consideration of istibra' as a defect. He argues that there is no explicit transmission from the three founding imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani) on this specific point, but there are transmissions from them regarding the ruling of istibra' itself. Therefore, neglecting it is not justified. The passage highlights the need for tarjih (preference) among differing opinions of later scholars, and mentions Ibn al-Humam as a prominent figure who weighed in. The core of the dispute is whether the master's right to immediate use of the slave is diminished by the need to wait for istibra', thus constituting a defect that lowers the price.

Key Terms
الاستبراء (al-istibra'): The waiting period to ensure a female slave is not pregnant before sale or intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the author's criticism of the statement 'no consideration'?
A) There is explicit consensus that istibra' is a defect.
B) There is no transmission from the three imams at all on this issue.
C) There is explicit transmission from the three imams regarding istibra', so it cannot be ignored.
D) The text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq is unreliable.

Key Takeaway
When there is no direct transmission from the imams on a specific point, later scholars must perform tarjih, but transmissions on related issues can inform the ruling.
