Comprehensive List of Defects in Sales
Enumeration of defects with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule for defects in sales, identify specific defects recognized by the Hanafi school, and apply the exception for defects removable without hardship.

Core Idea
Not every flaw is a defect; removable defects do not give the buyer the right of return.

The Hanafi school has compiled an extensive list of defects in sold items, particularly in slaves and animals. This lesson presents the final portion of that enumeration, including exceptions such as defects that the buyer can easily remove. Understanding these specifics is crucial for determining the buyer's option of return or compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Find the statement in the passage that says any defect the buyer can remove without hardship does not permit return. This is the overarching principle.
Look for the phrase: 'وكل عيب يتمكن المشتري من إزالته بلا مشقة لا يرده به'.
2
Categorize the defects by type
Group the listed defects into physical (e.g., qashaf, shatar, zafar) and moral/behavioural (e.g., drinking alcohol, leaving prayer). Note any that are context-dependent (e.g., pregnancy in women vs animals).
Use the key terms provided for unfamiliar Arabic terms.
3
Note the exceptions
Identify which traits are explicitly not considered defects: e.g., black colour of a slave, ugliness, removable defects like ihram or impurity on cloth.
The exceptions limit the buyer's right to return.
4
Apply the rule to an example
Take the example of a cow with reduced appetite. Determine if it is a defect and what remedy the buyer has.
The passage states: 'وقلة الأكل في البقرة عيب'.
5
Summarize the buyer's options
Conclude when the buyer can return the item, when he can only claim compensation (arsh), and when he has no claim at all.
Return is available for most defects; compensation for removable defects if removed without notice; no claim for traits not considered defects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة فهو زيادة والقشف وهو يبوسة الجلد وتشنج في الأعضاء والكي إن كان من داء وإلا لا كما في الحبشة والحرن على وجه لا يستفز ولا ينقاد للراكب عند العطف والسير . والجمع عيب وهو أن لا يلين عند اللجام وخلع الرأس من العذار، وبل المخلاة إن نقص وهو أن يسيل لعاب الفرس على وجه يبل المخلاة إذا جعل على رأسه وفيه علفه، وقيل أن يرميها وهو نوع من الجمح والغرب في العين وهو ورم في المآقي وربما يسيل منه شيء حتى قال محمد: إنه إذا كان سائلاً فصاحبه من أصحاب الأعذار والشتر عيب وهو انقلاب في الأجفان وبه سمي الأشتر وهو لضعف البصر والحول كذلك. والحوص وهو نوع من الحول، والقبل في أنسان العين وإذا كان في جانب فهو الحوص. والظفر وهو بياض يبدو في إنسان العين وكل ذلك لضعف البصر وربما منعه أصلاً والجرب في العين وغيرها لكونه داء، والعزل وهو أن يعزل ذنبه في أحد الجانبين والمشش وهو ورم في الدابة له صلابة، والفحج وهو تباعد ما بين القدمين، والصكك وهو أن يصكك إحدى ركبتيه على الأخرى، والحبل في بنات آدم عيب لكونه منقصاً بخلافه في البهائم لكونه زيادة، والقرن عظم في المأتي مانع من الوصول والرتق وهو لحم في المأتي، والعقل وهو أن يكون المأتي منها شبه الكيس لا يلتذ الواطئ بوطئها والكل يخل بالمقصود والبرص والجذام وهو قيح يوجد تحت الجلد يوجد نتنه من بعيد والفتق وهو ريح في المثانة وربما يهيج بالمرء فيقتله ولا يكون إلا لداء في الباطن والسلعة وهي القروح التي تكون على العين، وقيل داء في الرأس يتناثر منه شعر الرأس، وقيل غدة تحت الجلد تدور بين اللحم والجلد، والدحس وهو ورم يكون في أطراف حافر الفرس والحمار والحنف وهو إقبال كل واحد من الإبهامين إلى صاحبه وهو ينقص من قوة المشي، وقيل الأحنف الذي يمشي على ظهر قدميه. والصدف التواه في أصل العنق، وقيل إقبال إحدى الركبتين إلى الأخرى والشدق وهو سعة مفرطة في الفم، والتخنث، والحمق، وكونها مغنية وشرب الخمر وترك الصلاة وغيرها من الذنوب. وكل عيب يتمكن المشتري من إزالته بلا مشقة لا يرده به كإحرام الجارية ونجاسة الثوب. وقلة الأكل في البقرة عيب. ولو اشترى زوجي الخف وأحدهما أضيق من الآخر فإنه خرج عن العادة فله الرد، وإن كان الخف لا يتسع في اللبس وقد اشتراه له فهو عيب. والتراب العادة في الحنطة الخارج عن عيب فله ردها، وليس له أن يميز التراب ويرجع بحصته، ولو خلطه بها بعد التمييز أو انتقص الكيل والوزن بالتنقية امتنع الرد وله النقصان، وإن وجد الجارية دميمة أو سوداء لا ترد وإن كانت محترقة الوجه لا يعرف جمالها وقبحها فله الرد. ولو امتنع الرد رجع بفضل ما بينهما، ولو اشترى داراً ليس لها مسيل أو أرضاً لا شرب لها فافهم. وعن هذا والله أعلم قال العلامة قاسم في شرح النقاية : إن ما نقله في الخانية ثانياً وجه .
Description is an increase; al-qashaf is dryness of skin and contraction of limbs; branding if from a disease, otherwise no; balking such that it does not shy and does not obey the rider... etc. The list continues with many defects including eye diseases, lameness, etc. And every defect that the buyer can remove without difficulty he cannot return for it, like the consecration of a female slave and the impurity of cloth. If the buyer purchases a pair of khuffs and one is narrower than usual, he may return; if the grain contains excessive soil outside the usual, he may return it. He may not separate the soil and claim its proportion. If he mixes it after separation or the measure decreases by cleaning, return is prevented and he gets compensation. If the female slave is ugly or black, no return; if her face is burned and beauty/ugliness unknown, return. If return is prevented, he claims the difference. If he buys a house without drainage or land without irrigation, understand...
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement continuing the enumeration of defects in sales from earlier Hanafi sources. It covers a wide range of specific defects in animals (horses, cattle, slaves) and other items (khuffs, grain, property). Key points include: 1) Description (sifah) is considered an increase (not a defect). 2) Dry skin (qashaf) and branding (kayy) are defects if caused by disease. 3) Various horse defects: balking, not following the bit, watering the nosebag, weeping eye, entropion (shatar), squint (hawal), etc. 4) Eye diseases: white spot (zafar), scabies (jarab), etc. 5) Tail deviation ('azl), hard swelling (mushash), bow-legged (fahaj), knocking knees (sakakk). 6) Human defects: pregnancy in women is a defect (since it reduces value), while in animals it is an increase. 7) Vaginal obstructions: qarn, ratq, 'aql. 8) Leucoderma, leprosy, hernia, pustules (sal'ah), dahs (hoof swelling), hanaf (pigeon-toed), sadaf (scoliosis), shadq (excessive mouth width). 9) Moral defects: effeminacy, stupidity, singing, drinking alcohol, abandoning prayer. 10) Defects removable by buyer without hardship (like ihram or impurity) do not permit return. 11) Low appetite in a cow is a defect. 12) Mismatched khuffs, excessive soil in grain, ugly or black slave, burned face, missing drainage or irrigation – all with specific rulings. The passage concludes with a reference to al-Khaniyya and Sharh al-Niqaya.

Key Terms
القشف (al-qashaf): Dryness of skin and contraction of limbs, considered a defect.
الحبسة (al-habsah): Branding (al-kayy) if from a disease; otherwise not considered a defect.
الغرب (al-gharab): A swelling in the corner of the eye (māqi) that may discharge; if it discharges, the person is excused from certain obligations.
الشتر (al-shatar): Eversion (turning outward) of the eyelids, named after al-Ashtar; due to weak sight.
الظفر (al-zafar): A white spot appearing on the pupil of the eye, indicating weak sight or potential blindness.
إحرام الجارية (ihram al-jariyah): The state of consecration for Hajj or Umrah of a female slave, which is a defect that the buyer can remove (by having her exit ihram), so no return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on buying a cow with reduced appetite?
A) Not a defect because appetite varies.
B) A defect, and the buyer may return the cow.
C) Only a defect if the cow is emaciated.
D) The buyer may claim compensation but not return.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi enumeration of defects in sales is comprehensive, but the buyer's right to return is limited by the buyer's ability to remove the defect without hardship.

The General Principle of Defects ('Uyub) in Sale
General rule and examples of non-actionable defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a defect ('ayb) in a sale and identify which types of defects do not give the buyer the right of return.

Core Idea
A defect is anything reducing value or intended use; easily removable faults give no return right.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational principles of khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) in Hanafi sales law. The text from Bahr al-Raiq enumerates numerous defects, but first we must understand what legally constitutes a defect and when the buyer can return the item.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule: what qualifies as a defect?
The text implies that a defect is any fault that reduces the value or impairs the intended purpose. The examples show that both physical and behavioral defects are included, but only if they are not easily removable.
Hanafi definition relies on 'urf (custom).
2
Apply the exclusion: when is a defect not actionable?
The text explicitly states that defects the buyer can remove without hardship (like ihram or impurity) do not allow return.
This is a key Hanafi nuance.
3
Understand the role of custom: how do we determine what is a defect?
The text uses examples like low appetite in a cow being a defect, while ugliness or blackness in a slave girl is not. Custom ('urf) determines what is abnormal and detrimental.
Custom varies by time and place.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الرأس من العذار، وبل المخلاة إن نقص وهو أن يسيل لعاب الفرس على وجه يبل المخلاة إذا جعل على رأسه وفيه علفه، وقيل أن يرميها وهو نوع من الجمح والغرب في العين وهو ورم في المآقي وربما يسيل منه شيء حتى قال الباطن والسلعة وهي القروح التي تكون على العين، وقيل داء في الرأس يتناثر منه شعر الرأس، وقيل غدة تحت الجلد تدور بين اللحم والجلد، والدحس وهو ورم يكون في أطراف حافر الفرس والحمار والحنف وهو إقبال كل واحد من الإبهامين إلى صاحبه وهو ينقص من قوة المشي، وقيل الأحنف الذي يمشي على ظهر قدميه. والصدف التواه في أصل العنق، وقيل إقبال إحدى الركبتين إلى الأخرى والشدق وهو سعة مفرطة في الفم، والتخنث، والحمق، وكونها مغنية وشرب الخمر وترك الصلاة وغيرها من الذنوب. وكل عيب يتمكن المشتري من إزالته بلا مشقة لا يرده به كإحرام الجارية ونجاسة الثوب. وقله الأكل في البقرة عيب. ولو اشترى زوجي الخف وأحدهما أضيق من الآخر فإنه خرج عن العادة فله الرد، وإن كان الخف لا يتسع في اللبس وقد اشتراه له فهو عيب. والتراب العادة في الحنطة الخارج عن عيب فله ردها، وليس له أن يميز التراب ويرجع بحصته، ولو خلطه بها بعد التمييز أو انتقص الكيل والوزن بالتنقية امتنع الرد وله النقصان، وإن وجد الجارية دميمة أو سوداء لا ترد وإن كانت محترقة الوجه لا يعرف جمالها وقبحها فله الرد. ولو امتنع الرد رجع بفضل ما بينهما، ولو اشترى داراً ليس لها مسيل أو أرضاً لا شرب لها فافهم. وعن هذا والله أعلم قال العلامة قاسم في شرح النقاية : إن ما نقله في الخانية ثانياً وجه . العيب كتاب البيع باب الشاة يصيب بصره أو مرتفعة لا تسقى إلا بالسكر فله الرد . اهـ ما في المعراج ونقل منه في فتح القدير ولكن يحتاج إلى ضبط بعض ألفاظ ليزول الاشتباه عنها : الثؤلول بهمزة ساكنة وزان عصفور ويجوز التخفيف والجمع الثآليل وهو من ثثل ثألاً من باب تعب فالذكر أثأل والأنثى تألاء والجمع ثؤول مثل أحمر وحمراء وحمر وهو داء يشبه الحبوب. وقال ابن فارس: الثأل داء فتسترخي أعضاؤها ؛ كذا في الصحاح والعشى من عشى عشياً من باب تعب ضعف فهو أعشى والمرأة عشاء منه أيضاً والقشف من قشف الرجل قشفاً فهو قشف من باب تعب لم يعتد النظافة وأصله خشونة العيش منه أيضاً. والجمح من جمح الفرس براكبه يجمح بفتحتين جماحاً بالكسر وجموحاً مصدر استعصى حتى غلبه فهو جموح بالفتح وجامح يستوي فيه الذكر والأنثى؛ كذا في المصباح. ولم يذكر أن مصدره الجمح ولكن في الصحاح : جمح الفرس جموحاً وجماحاً وجمحاً إذا أعثر فارسه وغلبه اهـ. فعلى هذا الجمح في كلامهم بفتح الجيم وسكون الميم. والغرب بفتح الغين المعجمة والراء الساكنة وللعين غربان؛ كذا في الصحاح . والحوص بفتحتين ضيق في مؤخر العين والرجل أحوص منه أيضاً. والقبل بفتحتين في العين إقبال السواد على الأنف والعزل بفتحتين، والأعزل من الخيل الذي يقع ذنبه في جانب ولك عادة لا خلقة وهو عيب منه أيضاً. والمشش بفتحتين وهو شيء يشخص في وطيفها حتى يكون له حجم أيضاً. والسكك بفتحتين. ولو ذكروا من العيوب أيضاً الصأك بصاد ثم همزة مفتوحة وهو من صنك الرجل يصأك صأكاً إذا عرق فهاجت منه منتنة من ذفر ريح أو غير ذلك ـ كما في الصحاح - لكان أفود، ويمكن تخصيصه بالجارية كالبخر والدفر. والسلعة بكسر السين اسم لزيادة تحدث في الجسد كالغدة تتحرك إذا حركت وتكون من حمصة إلى بطيخة والسلعة بالفتح الشجة منه أيضاً وما قدمناه من تفسيرها بعيد. والحنف بفتحتين إعوجاج في الرجل والصدف بالصاد والدال المهملتين يقال فرس أصدف إذا كان متداني الفخذين متباعد الحافرين في التواء من الرسغين وقيل الصدف ميل في الحافر إلى الشق الوحشي، وقيل أن يميل خف البعير من اليد أو الرجل إلى الجانب الوحشي، فإن مال إلى الأنسي فهو لا يعد منه أيضاً. والشدق بفتح الشين وكسر الدال سعة الشدق وهو جانب الفم منه أيضاً. وفي فتح القدير: ومن العيوب العثار في الدواب إن كان كثيراً فاحشاً، أكل الختان العذار، وعدم في الغلام والجارية المولدين البالغين بخلافهما في الصغيرين، وفي الجليب من دار الحرب لا يكون عيباً مطلقاً. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان: وهذا عندهم يعني عدم الختان في الجارية المولدة أما عندنا عدم الخفض في الجوار لا يكون عيباً اهـ. وفي السراج الوهاج : الزكام ليس بعيب والجنون عيب وكذا العمى والعور والشلل والصمم والخرس منه قوله : (وأكل العذار) في نسخة الرملي وأكل العذرة وكتب عليها فقال : وفي نسخة العذار. قوله :
The mane (صفة) is an increase; al-qashaf is dryness of skin and contraction of limbs; cauterization (al-kayy) if due to disease (is a defect), otherwise not, as in the Habashah (Ethiopians) and al-haran (a type of horse?) that does not startle and does not obey the rider at turns and pace. Al-jam' is a defect: not softening at the bit, throwing the head from the headstall; wetting the nosebag (bal al-mikhallah) if it decreases (value) – it is that the horse's saliva flows so that it wets the nosebag when placed on its head with its feed; it is said that it throws it, which is a type of bolting. Al-gharb in the eye is a tumor in the lacrimal ducts, sometimes something flows from it, so Muhammad said: if it flows, its owner is among those with excuses. Al-shatar is a defect: eversion of the eyelids, after which al-Ashtar was named; it is due to weak sight, and so is al-hawal (squint). Al-haws is a type of squint; al-qabal is (the black) approaching the nose of the eye, and if (the black) is to one side, it is al-haws. Al-zafar is whiteness appearing in the pupil of the eye – all this due to weak sight, sometimes preventing it entirely. Al-jarab (mange) in the eye and elsewhere is a defect because it is a disease. Al-'azl is turning its tail to one side; al-mushash is a hard swelling on the animal; al-fahaj is wide spacing between the feet; al-sakakk is rubbing one knee against the other. Al-habl (pregnancy) in human females is a defect because it is a decrease, unlike in animals because it is an increase. Al-qarn (horn) is bone in the vagina preventing access; al-ratq is flesh in the vagina; al-'aqal is that the vagina is like a bag – the man does not enjoy intercourse – all these defeat the purpose. Al-barас (leprosy) and al-judham (elephantiasis) – pus under the skin whose stench is smelled from afar. Al-fataq is wind in the bladder, sometimes it flares up and kills a person, and occurs only from an internal disease. Al-sul'ah is ulcers that appear on the eye; or a disease of the head from which head hair falls out; or a gland under the skin moving between flesh and skin. Al-dahs is a swelling at the tips of the hoof of a horse or donkey. Al-hanaf is turning of each big toe towards the other, which weakens walking; or the ahnf is one who walks on the tops of his feet. Al-sadaf is curvature at the base of the neck; or turning of one knee towards the other. Al-shadaq is excessive width of the mouth. Al-takhnuth (effeminacy), al-humaq (foolishness), being a singer, drinking wine, abandoning prayer, and other sins. Every defect that the buyer can remove without hardship does not allow return, like the ihrām (ritual state) of a slave girl or impurity of a garment. Low appetite in a cow is a defect. If one buys a pair of khuffs (leather socks) and one is narrower than the other, it is out of the ordinary, so he has the right of return. If the khuff does not stretch in wearing and he bought it for wearing, it is a defect. Dust in wheat that is out of the ordinary is a defect, so he may return it; he may not separate the dust and claim its share. If he mixes it after separation or the measure/weight decreases by cleaning, return is prevented, and he has (right to) the deficiency. If he finds the slave girl ugly or black, she is not returned; but if her face is burnt so that her beauty or ugliness is unknown, he has the right of return. If return is prevented, he claims the difference in value. If he buys a house without a drain or land without irrigation water, then understand. And about this – and Allah knows best – al-Allamah Qasim said in Sharh al-Niqayah: What he transmitted in al-Khaniyah is a second view. The chapter on defects in the Book of Sale: A sheep whose sight is affected or is elevated and cannot be watered except by a waterwheel – he has the right of return. End of what is in al-Mi'raj, and it is quoted in Fath al-Qadir, but some words need adjustment to remove confusion. Al-thu'lul: with a sukun on the hamza, pattern of 'usfur, it may be lightened, plural thaa'liil; it is from tha'ila, tha'lan, from the pattern of ta'iba (to be tired), so the male is ath'al, female tha'laa', plural uthul like ahmar, hamraa', humr; it is a disease resembling pimples. Ibn Faris said: al-tha'l is a disease causing relaxation of limbs; as in al-Sihah. Al-'asha: from 'ashiya 'ashan, from ta'iba, meaning weak; so he is a'sha, the woman 'ashaa' from it also. Al-qashaf: from qashifa rajulun qashafan, he is qashif, from ta'iba, not accustomed to cleanliness; originally hardship of living from it also. Al-jamah: from jamaha al-farasu bi-rakibihi yajmahu with two fathahs, jamaahan with kasra and jumuuhan as masdar, meaning it rebels until it overcomes him; so it is jamuuh with fatha, and jamiḥ (masculine and feminine are the same); as in al-Misbah. He did not mention the masdar as jamh, but in al-Sihah: jamaha al-farasu jumuuhan, jamaahan, and jamhan, if it threw its rider and overcame him. So jamh in their speech is with fatha on jim and sukun on mim. Al-gharb: with fatha on ghayn and sukun on ra'; the eye having ghurbaan; as in al-Sihah. Al-haws: with two fathahs, narrowness at the back of the eye; the man is ahwas. Al-qabal: with two fathahs in the eye, the black approaching the nose. Al-'azl: with two fathahs; al-a'zal from horses is one whose tail falls to one side, habitually not by nature, and it is a defect. Al-mushash: with two fathahs, something prominent on the pastern until it has volume. Al-sakakk: with two fathahs. If they had also mentioned from the defects al-sa'k: with sad then hamza fatha, from sanaqa al-rajulu yas'aku sa'kan, if he sweats and thus a foul smell emanates from him, like bad armpit odor or otherwise – as in al-Sihah – it would be more useful; it can be specified to slave girls like bukhr (halitosis) and dufar (offensive smell). Al-sul'ah: with kasr on sin, name for an excrescence on the body like a gland that moves when moved, from a chickpea to a watermelon; al-sul'ah with fatha is a head wound. What we previously explained of it is far-fetched. Al-hanaf: with two fathahs, curvature in the leg. Al-sadaf: with sad and dal, both unpointed; one says farasun asdaf if its thighs are close together and its hooves apart with twisting of the pasterns; or sadaf is a deviation in the hoof to the lateral side; or that the camel's foot from hand or leg leans to the lateral side; if it leans to the medial side, it is not counted. Al-shadaq: with fatha on shin and kasr on dal, width of the shidq (side of the mouth). In Fath al-Qadir: from the defects are stumbling (al-'athar) in animals if it is excessive, eating the headstall (akl al-'idhar), and lack of circumcision (al-khitan) in adolescent male and female slaves unlike in small ones, and in a captive from dar al-harb it is absolutely not a defect. In Fatawa Qadikhan: This according to them means lack of circumcision in an indigenous slave girl; but according to us, lack of female circumcision (khifad) among neighbors is not a defect. End. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: al-zukam (common cold) is not a defect; al-junun (insanity) is a defect, as are blindness, one-eyedness, paralysis, deafness, and muteness. End. His statement: (wa akl al-'idhar) in the version of al-Ramli: wa akl al-'udhrah, and he wrote on it: and in a version: akl al-'idhar. His statement:
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary (sharh) of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding on the enumeration of defects ('uyub) that give the buyer the right of return (khiyar al-'ayb) in a sale contract. The author lists numerous physical and behavioral defects in animals (especially horses, donkeys, cattle) and slaves (male and female), providing definitions and linguistic derivations. The general rule stated is: any defect that the buyer can remove without significant hardship (like a slave girl being in ihram or a garment being impure) does not allow return. Similarly, common traits like ugliness or blackness are not defects unless they prevent assessment of beauty. The text also cites various authorities (Muhammad al-Shaybani, al-Sihah, al-Misbah, al-Mi'raj, Fath al-Qadir, Fatawa Qadikhan) and notes an intra-Hanafi difference regarding whether lack of circumcision in a slave girl is a defect: according to the view of 'them' (perhaps other scholars) it is a defect, but according to Hanafi scholars ('indana) it is not. The passage serves to define the scope of khiyar al-'ayb, which is essential for Hanafi sales law.

Key Terms
العيب (al-'ayb): A physical or behavioral fault in the sold item that reduces its value or prevents its intended use, giving the buyer the right to return or seek compensation.
الإحرام (al-ihram): Ritual consecration for Hajj or Umrah; a temporary state that can be ended easily.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following would NOT constitute a defect ('ayb) giving the right of return?
A) A horse with a tumor in its eye causing discharge.
B) A slave girl who is in the state of ihram.
C) A cow with low appetite.
D) A khuff pair where one is narrower than the other.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a defect is any fault reducing value or intended use that is not easily removable, as determined by custom.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Specific Defects in Sold Animals and Slaves
Listing and defining specific defects in animals and slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify specific physical and behavioral defects in animals and slaves as recognized by Hanafi fiqh and distinguish between those that are and are not actionable.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh lists extensive defects; custom and medical knowledge determine what counts; circumcision dispute shows role of 'urf.

The text from Bahr al-Raiq offers a detailed compilation of defects that have been recorded in the Hanafi tradition. Understanding these examples helps the buyer and seller know their rights. Notably, there is an intra-Hanafi debate on whether lack of circumcision in a slave girl is a defect.

Guided Path
1
Categorize defects: physical vs behavioral
Physical defects include growths, discharges, deformities; behavioral include bolting, willfulness. The text provides definitions for each.
Some defects like al-jamah (bolting) are behavioral.
2
Identify defects in animals
For example, al-gharb (eye tumor), al-jamah (bolting), al-hanaf (toe turning). Each has a specific definition; the text provides linguistic notes.
Note that pregnancy is a defect in humans but not in animals.
3
Identify defects in slaves
Examples include al-habl (pregnancy), al-qarn (bone), al-ratq (flesh), and behavioral defects like being a singer or abandoning prayer.
The text distinguishes between human and animal defects.
4
Apply the exclusion: defects not actionable
As per the general rule, easily removable defects are not actionable. Also, ugliness and blackness are not defects. The text adds that lack of circumcision for indigenous slaves is not a defect.
This is based on 'urf.
5
Analyze the dispute on circumcision
The text cites Fath al-Qadir and Fatawa Qadikhan: Hanafi holds that lack of circumcision (khifad) is not a defect for indigenous slaves, but some scholars disagree. The evidence is based on custom.
This is a key ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة فهو زيادة والقشف وهو يبوسة الجلد وتشنج في الأعضاء والكي إن كان من داء وإلا لا كما في الحبشة والحرن على وجه لا يستفز ولا ينقاد للراكب عند العطف والسير . والجمع عيب وهو أن لا يلين عند اللجام وخلع الرأس من العذار، وبل المخلاة إن نقص وهو أن يسيل لعاب الفرس على وجه يبل المخلاة إذا جعل على رأسه وفيه علفه، وقيل أن يرميها وهو نوع من الجمح والغرب في العين وهو ورم في المآقي وربما يسيل منه شيء حتى قال محمد: إنه إذا كان سائلاً فصاحبه من أصحاب الأعذار والشتر عيب وهو انقلاب في الأجفان وبه سمي الأشتر وهو لضعف البصر والحول كذلك. والحوص وهو نوع من الحول، والقبل في أنسان العين وإذا كان في جانب فهو الحوص. والظفر وهو بياض يبدو في إنسان العين وكل ذلك لضعف البصر وربما منعه أصلاً والجرب في العين وغيرها لكونه داء، والعزل وهو أن يعزل ذنبه في أحد الجانبين والمشش وهو ورم في الدابة له صلابة، والفحج وهو تباعد ما بين القدمين، والصكك وهو أن يصكك إحدى ركبتيه على الأخرى، والحبل في بنات آدم عيب لكونه منقصاً بخلافه في البهائم لكونه زيادة، والقرن عظم في المأتي مانع من الوصول والرتق وهو لحم في المأتي، والعقل وهو أن يكون المأتي منها شبه الكيس لا يلتذ الواطئ بوطئها والكل يخل بالمقصود والبرص والجذام وهو قيح يوجد تحت الجلد يوجد نتنه من بعيد والفتق وهو ريح في المثانة وربما يهيج بالمرء فيقتله ولا يكون إلا لداء في الباطن والسلعة وهي القروح التي تكون على العين، وقيل داء في الرأس يتناثر منه شعر الرأس، وقيل غدة تحت الجلد تدور بين اللحم والجلد، والدحس وهو ورم يكون في أطراف حافر الفرس والحمار والحنف وهو إقبال كل واحد من الإبهامين إلى صاحبه وهو ينقص من قوة المشي، وقيل الأحنف الذي يمشي على ظهر قدميه. والصدف التواه في أصل العنق، وقيل إقبال إحدى الركبتين إلى الأخرى والشدق وهو سعة مفرطة في الفم، والتخنث، والحمق، وكونها مغنية وشرب الخمر وترك الصلاة وغيرها من الذنوب. وكل عيب يتمكن المشتري من إزالته بلا مشقة لا يرده به كإحرام الجارية ونجاسة الثوب. وقله الأكل في البقرة عيب. ولو اشترى زوجي الخف وأحدهما أضيق من الآخر فإنه خرج عن العادة فله الرد، وإن كان الخف لا يتسع في اللبس وقد اشتراه له فهو عيب. والتراب العادة في الحنطة الخارج عن عيب فله ردها، وليس له أن يميز التراب ويرجع بحصته، ولو خلطه بها بعد التمييز أو اهـ ما في المعراج ونقل منه في فتح القدير ولكن يحتاج إلى ضبط بعض ألفاظ ليزول الاشتباه عنها : الثؤلول بهمزة ساكنة وزان عصفور ويجوز التخفيف والجمع الثآليل وهو من ثثل ثألاً من باب تعب فالذكر أثأل والأنثى والأنثى؛ كذا في المصباح. ولم يذكر أن مصدره الجمح ولكن في الصحاح : جمح الفرس جموحاً وجماحاً وجمحاً إذا أعثر فارسه وغلبه اهـ. فعلى هذا الجمح في كلامهم بفتح الجيم وسكون الميم. والغرب بفتح الغين المعجمة والراء الساكنة وللعين غربان؛ كذا في الصحاح . والحوص بفتحتين ضيق في مؤخر العين والرجل أحوص منه أيضاً. والقبل بفتحتين في العين إقبال السواد على الأنف والعزل بفتحتين، والأعزل من الخيل الذي يقع ذنبه في جانب ولك عادة لا خلقة وهو عيب منه أيضاً. والمشش بفتحتين وهو شيء يشخص في وطيفها حتى يكون له حجم أيضاً. والسكك بفتحتين. ولو ذكروا من العيوب أيضاً الصأك بصاد ثم همزة مفتوحة وهو من صنك الرجل يصأك صأكاً إذا عرق فهاجت منه منتنة من ذفر ريح أو غير ذلك ـ كما في الصحاح - لكان أفود، ويمكن تخصيصه بالجارية كالبخر والدفر. والسلعة بكسر السين اسم لزيادة تحدث في الجسد كالغدة تتحرك إذا حركت وتكون من حمصة إلى بطيخة والسلعة بالفتح الشجة منه أيضاً وما قدمناه من تفسيرها بعيد. والحنف بفتحتين إعوجاج في الرجل والصدف بالصاد والدال المهملتين يقال فرس أصدف إذا كان متداني الفخذين متباعد الحافرين في التواء من الرسغين وقيل الصدف ميل في الحافر إلى الشق الوحشي، وقيل أن يميل خف البعير من اليد أو الرجل إلى الجانب الوحشي، فإن مال إلى الأنسي فهو لا يعد منه أيضاً. والشدق بفتح الشين وكسر الدال سعة الشدق وهو جانب الفم منه أيضاً. وفي فتح القدير: ومن العيوب العثار في الدواب إن كان كثيراً فاحشاً، أكل الختان العذار، وعدم في الغلام والجارية المولدين البالغين بخلافهما في الصغيرين، وفي الجليب من دار الحرب لا يكون عيباً مطلقاً. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان: وهذا عندهم يعني عدم الختان في الجارية المولدة أما عندنا عدم الخفض في الجوار لا يكون عيباً اهـ. وفي السراج الوهاج : الزكام ليس بعيب والجنون عيب وكذا العمى والعور والشلل والصمم والخرس منه قوله : (وأكل العذار) في نسخة الرملي وأكل العذرة وكتب عليها فقال : وفي نسخة العذار. قوله :
The mane (صفة) is an increase; al-qashaf is dryness of skin and contraction of limbs; cauterization (al-kayy) if due to disease (is a defect), otherwise not, as in the Habashah (Ethiopians) and al-haran (a type of horse?) that does not startle and does not obey the rider at turns and pace. Al-jam' is a defect: not softening at the bit, throwing the head from the headstall; wetting the nosebag (bal al-mikhallah) if it decreases (value) – it is that the horse's saliva flows so that it wets the nosebag when placed on its head with its feed; it is said that it throws it, which is a type of bolting. Al-gharb in the eye is a tumor in the lacrimal ducts, sometimes something flows from it, so Muhammad said: if it flows, its owner is among those with excuses. Al-shatar is a defect: eversion of the eyelids, after which al-Ashtar was named; it is due to weak sight, and so is al-hawal (squint). Al-haws is a type of squint; al-qabal is (the black) approaching the nose of the eye, and if (the black) is to one side, it is al-haws. Al-zafar is whiteness appearing in the pupil of the eye – all this due to weak sight, sometimes preventing it entirely. Al-jarab (mange) in the eye and elsewhere is a defect because it is a disease. Al-'azl is turning its tail to one side; al-mushash is a hard swelling on the animal; al-fahaj is wide spacing between the feet; al-sakakk is rubbing one knee against the other. Al-habl (pregnancy) in human females is a defect because it is a decrease, unlike in animals because it is an increase. Al-qarn (horn) is bone in the vagina preventing access; al-ratq is flesh in the vagina; al-'aqal is that the vagina is like a bag – the man does not enjoy intercourse – all these defeat the purpose. Al-barас (leprosy) and al-judham (elephantiasis) – pus under the skin whose stench is smelled from afar. Al-fataq is wind in the bladder, sometimes it flares up and kills a person, and occurs only from an internal disease. Al-sul'ah is ulcers that appear on the eye; or a disease of the head from which head hair falls out; or a gland under the skin moving between flesh and skin. Al-dahs is a swelling at the tips of the hoof of a horse or donkey. Al-hanaf is turning of each big toe towards the other, which weakens walking; or the ahnf is one who walks on the tops of his feet. Al-sadaf is curvature at the base of the neck; or turning of one knee towards the other. Al-shadaq is excessive width of the mouth. Al-takhnuth (effeminacy), al-humaq (foolishness), being a singer, drinking wine, abandoning prayer, and other sins. Every defect that the buyer can remove without hardship does not allow return, like the ihrām (ritual state) of a slave girl or impurity of a garment. Low appetite in a cow is a defect. If one buys a pair of khuffs (leather socks) and one is narrower than the other, it is out of the ordinary, so he has the right of return. If the khuff does not stretch in wearing and he bought it for wearing, it is a defect. Dust in wheat that is out of the ordinary is a defect, so he may return it; he may not separate the dust and claim its share. If he mixes it after separation or the measure/weight decreases by cleaning, return is prevented, and he has (right to) the deficiency. If he finds the slave girl ugly or black, she is not returned; but if her face is burnt so that her beauty or ugliness is unknown, he has the right of return. If return is prevented, he claims the difference in value. If he buys a house without a drain or land without irrigation water, then understand. And about this – and Allah knows best – al-Allamah Qasim said in Sharh al-Niqayah: What he transmitted in al-Khaniyah is a second view. The chapter on defects in the Book of Sale: A sheep whose sight is affected or is elevated and cannot be watered except by a waterwheel – he has the right of return. End of what is in al-Mi'raj, and it is quoted in Fath al-Qadir, but some words need adjustment to remove confusion. Al-thu'lul: with a sukun on the hamza, pattern of 'usfur, it may be lightened, plural thaa'liil; it is from tha'ila, tha'lan, from the pattern of ta'iba (to be tired), so the male is ath'al, female tha'laa', plural uthul like ahmar, hamraa', humr; it is a disease resembling pimples. Ibn Faris said: al-tha'l is a disease causing relaxation of limbs; as in al-Sihah. Al-'asha: from 'ashiya 'ashan, from ta'iba, meaning weak; so he is a'sha, the woman 'ashaa' from it also. Al-qashaf: from qashifa rajulun qashafan, he is qashif, from ta'iba, not accustomed to cleanliness; originally hardship of living from it also. Al-jamah: from jamaha al-farasu bi-rakibihi yajmahu with two fathahs, jamaahan with kasra and jumuuhan as masdar, meaning it rebels until it overcomes him; so it is jamuuh with fatha, and jamiḥ (masculine and feminine are the same); as in al-Misbah. He did not mention the masdar as jamh, but in al-Sihah: jamaha al-farasu jumuuhan, jamaahan, and jamhan, if it threw its rider and overcame him. So jamh in their speech is with fatha on jim and sukun on mim. Al-gharb: with fatha on ghayn and sukun on ra'; the eye having ghurbaan; as in al-Sihah. Al-haws: with two fathahs, narrowness at the back of the eye; the man is ahwas. Al-qabal: with two fathahs in the eye, the black approaching the nose. Al-'azl: with two fathahs; al-a'zal from horses is one whose tail falls to one side, habitually not by nature, and it is a defect. Al-mushash: with two fathahs, something prominent on the pastern until it has volume. Al-sakakk: with two fathahs. If they had also mentioned from the defects al-sa'k: with sad then hamza fatha, from sanaqa al-rajulu yas'aku sa'kan, if he sweats and thus a foul smell emanates from him, like bad armpit odor or otherwise – as in al-Sihah – it would be more useful; it can be specified to slave girls like bukhr (halitosis) and dufar (offensive smell). Al-sul'ah: with kasr on sin, name for an excrescence on the body like a gland that moves when moved, from a chickpea to a watermelon; al-sul'ah with fatha is a head wound. What we previously explained of it is far-fetched. Al-hanaf: with two fathahs, curvature in the leg. Al-sadaf: with sad and dal, both unpointed; one says farasun asdaf if its thighs are close together and its hooves apart with twisting of the pasterns; or sadaf is a deviation in the hoof to the lateral side; or that the camel's foot from hand or leg leans to the lateral side; if it leans to the medial side, it is not counted. Al-shadaq: with fatha on shin and kasr on dal, width of the shidq (side of the mouth). In Fath al-Qadir: from the defects are stumbling (al-'athar) in animals if it is excessive, eating the headstall (akl al-'idhar), and lack of circumcision (al-khitan) in adolescent male and female slaves unlike in small ones, and in a captive from dar al-harb it is absolutely not a defect. In Fatawa Qadikhan: This according to them means lack of circumcision in an indigenous slave girl; but according to us, lack of female circumcision (khifad) among neighbors is not a defect. End. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: al-zukam (common cold) is not a defect; al-junun (insanity) is a defect, as are blindness, one-eyedness, paralysis, deafness, and muteness. End. His statement: (wa akl al-'idhar) in the version of al-Ramli: wa akl al-'udhrah, and he wrote on it: and in a version: akl al-'idhar. His statement:
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary (sharh) of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding on the enumeration of defects ('uyub) that give the buyer the right of return (khiyar al-'ayb) in a sale contract. The author lists numerous physical and behavioral defects in animals (especially horses, donkeys, cattle) and slaves (male and female), providing definitions and linguistic derivations. The general rule stated is: any defect that the buyer can remove without significant hardship (like a slave girl being in ihram or a garment being impure) does not allow return. Similarly, common traits like ugliness or blackness are not defects unless they prevent assessment of beauty. The text also cites various authorities (Muhammad al-Shaybani, al-Sihah, al-Misbah, al-Mi'raj, Fath al-Qadir, Fatawa Qadikhan) and notes an intra-Hanafi difference regarding whether lack of circumcision in a slave girl is a defect: according to the view of 'them' (perhaps other scholars) it is a defect, but according to Hanafi scholars ('indana) it is not. The passage serves to define the scope of khiyar al-'ayb, which is essential for Hanafi sales law.

Key Terms
الجمح (al-jamah): Bolting: a horse that runs away with its rider, refusing to obey, considered a behavioral defect.
الحبل (al-habl): Pregnancy; in humans a defect, in animals not.
الختان (al-khitan): Circumcision; its absence is not a defect for indigenous slaves in Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT considered a defect that allows return?
A) A horse that bolts frequently (al-jamah).
B) A slave girl who is pregnant.
C) A horse with a tumor in its eye (al-gharb).
D) A slave girl who lacks circumcision (indigenous).

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh provides an extensive list of defects; custom ('urf) and utility determine which faults are actionable, including an intra-madhhab dispute on circumcision.

Lexical Definitions of Defects in Sale (Thu'lul, 'Asha, etc.)
Author defines Arabic terms for physical defects in animals and slaves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key Arabic terms for defects in sale and apply their lexical meanings to determine whether a condition qualifies as a defect under Hanafi law.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the right to return a defective item depends on precise lexical definitions of defects drawn from classical Arabic dictionaries.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents lexical definitions of various physical defects in animals and slaves, such as thu'lul (pimple-like disease), 'asha (night blindness), and jamh (stubborn horse). These definitions are not merely linguistic curiosities; they are legally operative because the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb) requires that the condition be a recognized 'ayb (defect) under the law. Understanding these terms allows you to correctly identify defects when they appear in fiqh texts and apply the rulings accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the role of lexical definitions
Understand that the Hanafi school uses precise Arabic lexical meanings to define what constitutes a defect for khiyar al-'ayb. The author quotes dictionaries to establish the exact meaning of each term.
These definitions come from al-Sihah, al-Misbah, and Ibn Faris.
2
Parse a defect term
For each term, identify its root, pattern, plural, and the description of the condition. For example, thu'lul is from th-'[-?] with a pattern of 'usfur, meaning a disease resembling pimples.
The author often gives morphological details to avoid confusion.
3
Determine legal significance
Assess whether the condition makes the item defective. If it does, the buyer has the right to return the item (khiyar al-'ayb) unless they explicitly waived it.
Not every flaw is a legal defect; only those listed or analogous are considered.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a description of an animal's condition, match it to the appropriate term and decide if it qualifies as a defect. For example, a horse that throws its rider matches 'jamh'.
Use the dictionary meanings as the standard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العيب كتاب البيع باب الشاة يصيب بصره أو مرتفعة لا تسقى إلا بالسكر فله الرد . اهـ ما في المعراج ونقل منه في فتح القدير ولكن يحتاج إلى ضبط بعض ألفاظ ليزول الاشتباه عنها : الثؤلول بهمزة ساكنة وزان عصفور ويجوز التخفيف والجمع الثآليل وهو من ثثل ثألاً من باب تعب فالذكر أثأل والأنثى تألاء والجمع ثؤول مثل أحمر وحمراء وحمر وهو داء يشبه الحبوب. وقال ابن فارس: الثأل داء فتسترخي أعضاؤها ؛ كذا في الصحاح والعشى من عشى عشياً من باب تعب ضعف فهو أعشى والمرأة عشاء منه أيضاً والقشف من قشف الرجل قشفاً فهو قشف من باب تعب لم يعتد النظافة وأصله خشونة العيش منه أيضاً. والجمح من جمح الفرس براكبه يجمح بفتحتين جماحاً بالكسر وجموحاً مصدر استعصى حتى غلبه فهو جموح بالفتح وجامح يستوي فيه الذكر والأنثى؛ كذا في المصباح. ولم يذكر أن مصدره الجمح ولكن في الصحاح : جمح الفرس جموحاً وجماحاً وجمحاً إذا أعثر فارسه وغلبه اهـ. فعلى هذا الجمح في كلامهم بفتح الجيم وسكون الميم. والغرب بفتح الغين المعجمة والراء الساكنة وللعين غربان؛ كذا في الصحاح . والحوص بفتحتين ضيق في مؤخر العين والرجل أحوص منه أيضاً. والقبل بفتحتين في العين إقبال السواد على الأنف والعزل بفتحتين، والأعزل من الخيل الذي يقع ذنبه في جانب ولك عادة لا خلقة وهو عيب منه أيضاً. والمشش بفتحتين وهو شيء يشخص في وطيفها حتى يكون له حجم أيضاً. والسكك بفتحتين. ولو ذكروا من العيوب أيضاً الصأك بصاد ثم همزة مفتوحة وهو من صنك الرجل يصأك صأكاً إذا عرق فهاجت منه منتنة من ذفر ريح أو غير ذلك ـ كما في الصحاح - لكان أفود، ويمكن تخصيصه بالجارية كالبخر والدفر. والسلعة بكسر السين اسم لزيادة تحدث في الجسد كالغدة تتحرك إذا حركت وتكون من حمصة إلى بطيخة والسلعة بالفتح الشجة منه أيضاً وما قدمناه من تفسيرها بعيد. والحنف بفتحتين إعوجاج في الرجل والصدف بالصاد والدال المهملتين يقال فرس أصدف إذا كان متداني الفخذين متباعد الحافرين في التواء من الرسغين وقيل الصدف ميل في الحافر إلى الشق الوحشي، وقيل أن يميل خف البعير من اليد أو الرجل إلى الجانب الوحشي، فإن مال إلى الأنسي فهو لا يعد منه أيضاً. والشدق بفتح الشين وكسر الدال سعة الشدق وهو جانب الفم منه أيضاً.
Defects. Book of Sale. Chapter. The sheep whose sight is affected or [udder] elevated and not watered except with [السكر?] then he has the right to return. End. What is in al-Mi'raj and quoted from it in Fath al-Qadir, but it needs regulation of some words to remove ambiguity about them: Thu'lul: with a silent hamza, measure of 'usfur, and it is permissible to lighten it, plural thalalil, and it is from thu'l, th-'[-?] from the category of ta'iba, so the masculine is ath'al and the feminine tha'la', plural thuw'l like ahmar, hamra', and humr, and it is a disease resembling pimples. Ibn Faris said: al-tha'lu is a disease that causes its limbs to become relaxed; likewise in al-Sihah. And al-Asha: from 'asha ya'sha 'ashyan from the category of ta'iba meaning weakness, so he is a'sha and the woman is 'ashya' from it. And al-Qashaf: from qashifa al-rajulu qashafan, so he is qashif from the category of ta'iba: he is not accustomed to cleanliness, and its origin is roughness of life also from it. And al-Jamh: from jamaha al-farasu bi-rakibihi yajmahu jamahan and jumuhan, source, it became stubborn until it overpowered him, so it is jamuh and jamih, same for male and female; likewise in al-Misbah. And he did not mention that its source is al-jamh, but in al-Sihah: jamaha al-farasu jamuhan and jamahan and jamhan when it throws its rider and overpowers him, end. So based on this, al-jamh in their speech is with fath of jim and sukun of mim. And al-Gharab: with fath of the letter ghain and ra' sakina, and for the eye gharab?; likewise in al-Sihah. And al-Haws: with two faths, narrowness at the back of the eye, and the man is ahaws from it. And al-Qabal: with two faths in the eye: turning of the blackness towards the nose. And al-'Azal: with two faths, and al-a'zal from horses: whose tail falls to one side, and it is a habit not creation, and it is a defect also from it. And al-Mashash: with two faths, and it is something that stands out in its leg until it has a size also. And al-Sakak: with two faths. And if they mentioned among defects also al-Sa'k: with sad then open hamza, and it is from sanaqa al-rajulu yas'aku sa'kan when he sweats, then a foul smell emanates from him from a stench or other than that — as in al-Sihah — it would be more useful, and it can be specified to the slave-girl like bukhr and dufar. And al-Sil'a: with kasr of the sin, name for a swelling that occurs in the body like a gland that moves when moved and is from a chickpea to a melon size, and al-sal'a with fath is the scar also from it, and what we presented of its interpretation is far. And al-Hanaf: with two faths, crookedness in the leg. And al-Sadaf: with sad and dal, both unpointed, it is said a horse is asdaf if it is with close thighs and far apart hooves with twisting of the pasterns, and it is said al-sadaf is a tilt in the hoof towards the outer side, and it is said that the camel's foot tilts from the hand or leg to the outer side, and if it tilts to the inner side then it is not considered from it. And al-Shadq: with fath of shin and kasr of dal, width of the shidq which is the side of the mouth also from it.

Commentary
This passage is from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq's commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author begins by noting a ruling from al-Mi'raj about a ewe with defective sight or an elevated udder, then states that Fath al-Qadir quotes it but feels that some words need clarification. So he proceeds to give lexical definitions of various Arabic terms used for defects in animals and slaves. These definitions are drawn from classical Arabic dictionaries like al-Sihah, al-Misbah, and the works of Ibn Faris. The purpose is to enable precise understanding when these terms appear in fiqh texts. The author defines terms such as thu'lul (a pimple-like disease), 'asha (night blindness), qashaf (lack of cleanliness), jamh (a stubborn horse that rears), gharab (thirst in the eye?), haws (narrowness of the eye), qabal (turning of the black of the eye towards the nose), 'azal (a horse whose tail falls to one side), mashash (something protruding in the leg), sakak (undefined), sa'k (foul odor from sweat), sil'a (a lump like a gland), hanaf (crooked leg), sadaf (a hoof tilted outward), and shadq (wide mouth). Each definition gives the root, pattern, plural, and sometimes the source. This lexical work is essential because the legal ruling of khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) depends on correctly identifying whether a condition qualifies as a defect under the law.

Key Terms
الثؤلول (al-thu'lul): A disease resembling pimples, affecting animals and slaves, considered a defect giving right of return.
العشى (al-'asha): Night blindness or weak vision; the person is a'sha.
القشف (al-qashaf): Lack of cleanliness or roughness of life; considered a defect in slaves.
الجمح (al-jamh): A horse that is stubborn and overpowers its rider; a defect in animals.
السلعة (al-sil'a): A lump on the body that moves when touched, from chickpea to melon size; a defect.
الصدف (al-sadaf): A tilt in the hoof or camel's foot towards the outer side; considered a defect.
الشدق (al-shadq): Width of the side of the mouth; also considered a defect.

D) shadq (wide mouth)

Key Takeaway
Lexical precision from classical dictionaries determines whether a condition is a legal defect in Hanafi sale law.

Rulings on Defects in Sale: 'Athar, Lack of Circumcision, and Other Conditions
Author lists defects and non-defects from multiple Hanafi sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which conditions are considered defects in a sale according to the Hanafi school and apply the distinction between defects that give the buyer the right of return and those that do not.

Core Idea
Not every flaw is a defect; the Hanafi school distinguishes between trivial and significant defects based on custom and degree.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq and other Hanafi works enumerates specific conditions that are or are not defects in a sale. Understanding these examples helps you apply the general rule that a defect is something that diminishes value or desirability in the eyes of people. The passage also highlights differences of opinion within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the items and conditions
List the types of items mentioned (animals, slaves) and the conditions discussed (e.g., tripping, lack of circumcision, cold, insanity).
Note that the source treats animals and slaves separately.
2
Classify each condition as defect or not
For each condition, determine whether the ruling states it is a defect (ʿayb) or not. Pay attention to qualifiers like 'if excessive' or 'absolutely'.
Excessive tripping is a defect; a common cold is not.
3
Note distinctions based on age and origin
Observe that defects may differ for adults vs. small children, and for slaves born in Islamic lands vs. imported from Dar al-Harb.
Lack of circumcision is a defect in adults but not in small children; imported slaves are treated differently.
4
Examine the textual variant
The text notes a variant reading for 'akl al-'idhar/al-'udhra. This illustrates the importance of manuscript verification.
Such variants may affect the precise legal ruling.

وفي فتح القدير: ومن العيوب العثار في الدواب إن كان كثيراً فاحشاً، أكل الختان العذار، وعدم في الغلام والجارية المولدين البالغين بخلافهما في الصغيرين، وفي الجليب من دار الحرب لا يكون عيباً مطلقاً. وفي فتاوى قاضيخان: وهذا عندهم يعني عدم الختان في الجارية المولدة أما عندنا عدم الخفض في الجوار لا يكون عيباً اهـ. وفي السراج الوهاج : الزكام ليس بعيب والجنون عيب وكذا العمى والعور والشلل والصمم والخرس منه قوله : (وأكل العذار) في نسخة الرملي وأكل العذرة وكتب عليها فقال : وفي نسخة العذار. قوله :
And in Fath al-Qadir: Among defects is al-'athar (tripping) in beasts if it is much and excessive; eating of the ختان؟ (prepuce?); and absence [of circumcision] in a young boy and a born slave-girl who are adult, unlike in the two small ones; and in the imported from Dar al-Harb it is not a defect absolutely. And in Fatawa Qadikhan: This in their view means absence of circumcision in a born slave-girl, but in our view absence of خفض? in the slave-girl is not a defect, end. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: A cold is not a defect; insanity is a defect, likewise blindness, one-eyedness, paralysis, deafness, and muteness. From it: his saying (eating of al-'idhar) in the Ramli copy: and 'eating of al-'udhra' and he wrote on it and said: and in a copy: al-'idhar. His saying:
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of defects. The author cites several authorities. From Fath al-Qadir: al-'athar (stumbling or tripping) in animals is a defect only if it is frequent and excessive. 'Akl al-khitan al-'idhar (eating of the foreskin of the prepuce? unclear) is a defect. Lack of circumcision in a grown boy or an adult born slave-girl is a defect, but not in small children. For slaves imported from Dar al-Harb, it is never a defect. From Fatawa Qadikhan: the absence of circumcision in a born slave-girl is considered a defect according to some; but the author's view (of the Hanafi school) is that absence of خفض (female circumcision?) in a slave-girl is not a defect. From al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: a common cold (zukaam) is not a defect; insanity, blindness, being one-eyed, paralysis, deafness, and muteness are defects. Then a textual note about the phrase 'akl al-'idhar': in the Ramli copy it is 'akl al-'udhra', and in another copy it is 'al-'idhar'. This shows textual variants.

Key Terms
العثار (al-'athar): Stumbling, tripping in animals; considered a defect if excessive.
الجنون (al-junun): Insanity; clearly a defect giving option of return.
الزكام (al-zukam): Common cold; not a defect according to Sirj al-Wahhaj.
الخرس (al-khurs): Muteness; a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is explicitly stated as NOT a defect?
A) Insanity
B) Blindness
C) A cold
D) Muteness

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school carefully defines defects based on degree and custom; only significant flaws affecting value give the right of return.

Physical Defects in Slaves and Animals
List of physical defects with rulings on option to return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which physical defects in slaves and animals give the buyer the option to return the item, and apply the Hanafi distinction between intrinsic defects and temporary conditions.

Core Idea
Any permanent physical abnormality that diminishes value or utility is a defect giving the option to return.

The Hanafi school recognizes a wide range of physical defects in slaves and animals that entitle the buyer to return the item. These include missing limbs, chronic illnesses, and other visible or hidden impairments. In this lesson, we will explore the specific defects listed in the text and the conditions under which the option to return exists.

Guided Path
1
Categorize the defect
Determine whether the condition is a physical abnormality (e.g., extra finger, swelling) or a chronic illness (e.g., night blindness).
Some conditions like a missing tooth (not mentioned here) are also defects by analogy.
2
Check permanence and severity
Defects must be permanent or recurrent; temporary illnesses like a passing fever may not qualify unless concurrent with the seller's possession.
The text mentions a fever in the hand: if the same fever occurred in the seller's hand at the same time, it is a defect.
3
Consider the purpose of purchase
Some defects are judged relative to the intended use. A cow that does not give milk is a defect if bought for milk, but not if bought for meat.
Similarly, a slow animal is only a defect if the buyer stipulated speed.
4
Apply customary exceptions
Certain conditions are not considered defects if the local custom does not treat them as such. Ear piercing is a defect in Turkish custom but not in Indian custom.
Custom ('urf) can override general rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المشتري فقال البائع كان لها زوج عندي غير هذا الرجل أبانها أو مات عنها قبل البيع كان القول قول البائع .اهـ. وفي البزازية : التخنث نوعان: بمعنى الرديء من الأفعال وهو عيب . الثاني الرعونة واللين في الصوت والتكسر في المشي فإنه قل لا يرد وإن كثر رده. ولو اشترى غلاماً أمرد فوجده محلوق اللحية يرده، وعدم استمساك البول عيب. ولو اشترى حبلى فولدت عند المشتري لا خصومة له مع البائع، فإن ماتت في نفاسها رجع بنقصان الحبل إن لم يعلم به عند الشراء اشتراها على أنها صغيرة فإذا بالغة لا يردها. والثقب في الأذنين إن واسعاً فهو عيب في التركية إن عدوه عيباً لا في الهندية. وإن وجد الحنطة مسوسة يرد لا رديئة وجع الضرس مرة بعد مرة عيب، وإذا كانت إحدى العينين زرقاء والأخرى غير زرقاء أو إحداهما كحلاء والأخرى بيضاء فهو عيب. وإذا كانت البقرة لا تحلب إن كان مثلها يشتري للحلب ردها وإن للحم لا ، إن كانت تمص إحدى (وكونها وكون العبد أكولاً الخ) عبارة الفتح وقلة الأكل في البقرة ونحوها وكثرته في الإنسان، وقيل
And the extra finger and missing one, and ulcers, and head wounds, and all illnesses, and al-adar is a defect which is swelling of the testicles, and al-'asha is a defect which is one who does not see at night, and likewise al-'amash, and al-'unnin, and al-khasi. And if he bought it on the condition that it is castrated and found it uncastrated, there is no option for him. Lying and backbiting are defects in them [slaves]. And lack of eating in beasts but not in humans. And the marriage of a slave-girl and a boy: if her husband divorces her revocably, then he has the right to return; if it was irrevocable, it drops. And if he finds her prohibited to him by breastfeeding or in-law relationship like his sister or his wife's mother, it is not a defect because he can benefit by marrying her off and taking the compensation. And if he finds her not good at cooking and baking, it is not a defect. And if he finds in the mushaf a missing part or error, it is a defect. And if she is in 'iddah from an irrevocable divorce, it is not a defect because the husband has no way to approach her, and the prohibition is temporary like the prohibition of a menstruating woman, end. And in al-Khaniyya: If he buys a slave-girl and takes possession, then claims she has a husband and wants to return her, and the seller says she had a husband who divorced her or died before the sale, the seller's word is accepted and she is not returned. If the buyer brings evidence of the existence of marriage, his evidence is not accepted. If he brings evidence of the seller's admission of that, his evidence is accepted. If the seller says her husband was my slave so-and-so who divorced her before the sale, and the buyer denies the divorce, the seller's word is accepted. If the one acknowledged as husband appears and denies the divorce, the buyer may return her. If the seller says she had a husband, my slave, on the day of sale, and he divorced her or died before or after delivery, and the buyer denies the divorce, the buyer may return the slave-girl. If she had a husband according to the buyer, and the seller says she had a husband with me other than this man, and he divorced her or died before the sale, the seller's word is accepted, end. And in al-Bazzaziyya: Al-takhnuth (effeminacy) is two types: one meaning bad actions, which is a defect. The second is lightheadedness, softness in voice, and frailty in walking; if it is little, it is not returned, but if it is much, it is returned. If he buys a beardless boy and finds him with a shaved beard, he returns him. Lack of retention of urine is a defect. If he buys a pregnant slave-girl and she gives birth in the buyer's possession, he has no case against the seller. If she dies in childbirth, he recovers the reduction in value for the pregnancy if he did not know of it at the time of purchase. If he bought her on the understanding she was young and then she is found to be mature, he does not return her. Piercing the ears, if it is large, is a defect according to the Turkish custom if they consider it a defect, but not in the Indian custom. And if he finds the wheat worm-eaten, he returns it; not if it is inferior. Toothache once in a while is a defect. And if one eye is blue and the other not, or one is black and the other white, it is a defect. If a cow does not give milk, if the like of it is bought for milk, he returns it; if for meat, no. If it sucks one of its teats, he may return it. If an animal is slow in walking, it is not returned unless he stipulated that it is fast. And the slave being a big eater is not a defect, but in a slave-girl it is a defect because she spoils the bed. If he buys a slave and he developed a fever in his hand, and it was also in the seller's hand, if the two times are the same, he returns; if different, no. A large hole in a wall is a defect. Likewise, ants' nests in a vineyard, if excessive, is a defect. Likewise, if there is a passage for others or a drainage. One of them is... (And her being and the slave being a big eater, etc.) is the wording of al-Fath: and little eating in a cow and the like, and much eating in a human, and it was said...
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists many defects and case studies. The defects include extra or missing fingers, ulcers, head wounds, all illnesses, swelling of testicles (adar), night blindness ('asha), weakness of sight ('amash), impotence ('unnin), castration (khasi), lying, backbiting, marriage, inability to cook/bake, errors in mushaf, being in 'iddah, etc. The text also covers special cases: if a slave is bought on the condition of being castrated but is not, no option. If a slave-girl's husband divorces her revocably, the buyer may return her; if irrevocably, the option is lost. Prohibition by breastfeeding or in-law relationship is not a defect because the buyer can still benefit by marrying her off. The Khaniyya section focuses on the problem when a slave-girl has a husband and the buyer wants to return her due to this defect. Complex rules of evidence and qawl (the seller's word) are given. Bazzaziyya mentions effeminacy (takhannuth) as a defect if involving bad actions; if just mannerisms, it depends on frequency. Other rulings: a beardless boy with shaved beard can be returned; incontinence is a defect; pregnant slave-girl gives buyer no claim if she gives birth; piercing ears is defect according to Turkish custom but not Indian; worm-eaten wheat can be returned; toothache if recurring is a defect; different eye colors is a defect; cow not giving milk is defect if bought for milk; slow walking animal not returned unless stipulated fast; slave being a big eater is not a defect for male but is for female; fever in hand that matches time of seller's possession allows return; large hole in wall is defect; ants' nests in vineyard if excessive is defect, etc. The passage concludes with a note from Fath al-Qadir about eating.

Key Terms
الأصبع الزائدة (al-isba' al-za'idah): Extra finger; a physical defect in a slave.
الأدر (al-adar): Swelling of testicles; a defect.
العشا (al-'asha): Night blindness; a defect.
العنين (al-'unnin): Impotence; a defect.
الخصي (al-khasi): Castration; a defect if not disclosed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a defect in a male slave?
A) Night blindness (al-'asha)
B) Being a big eater (akul)
C) Impotence (al-'unnin)
D) Swelling of testicles (al-adar)

Key Takeaway
Physical defects in slaves and animals entitle the buyer to return only if they are permanent, diminish value or utility, and are not excluded by custom or stipulation.

Marriage, Pregnancy, and Prohibition Defects
Rulings on marriage, 'iddah, and prohibition defects in slave-girls

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when the existence of a marriage, pregnancy, or a prohibition relationship in a purchased slave-girl constitutes a defect entitling the buyer to return her.

Core Idea
A slave-girl's existing marriage or close relationship with the buyer is not always a defect; the key is whether the buyer can still benefit from her.

When buying a slave-girl, defects may include her being married, in 'iddah, or related to the buyer by fosterage or marriage. The Hanafi school makes nuanced distinctions: an irrevocable divorce removes the defect, and certain prohibitions can be overcome by selling her off. Let's examine the specific cases from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of relationship
Determine if the girl is married, in 'iddah, or related to the buyer by breastfeeding or in-law.
Each has a different ruling.
2
Check the nature of divorce
If the marriage ended by revocable divorce, the buyer may return; if irrevocable, the defect is removed.
Because after irrevocable divorce, the seller had no right to her, and the buyer also cannot marry her during 'iddah.
3
Evaluate benefit despite prohibition
If the girl is prohibited to the buyer (e.g., his sister by fosterage), it is not a defect because he can still marry her off and take the compensation.
The benefit is financial, not personal use.
4
Pregnancy and childbirth
A pregnant slave-girl does not give the buyer a claim after birth; if she dies in childbirth, the buyer recovers the depreciation due to pregnancy if unaware.
Pregnancy is a defect only if it caused loss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Piercing the ears, if it is large, is a defect according to the Turkish custom if they consider it a defect, but not in the Indian custom. And if he finds the wheat worm-eaten, he returns it; not if it is inferior. Toothache once in a while is a defect. And if one eye is blue and the other not, or one is black and the other white, it is a defect. If a cow does not give milk, if the like of it is bought for milk, he returns it; if for meat, no. If it sucks one of its teats, he may return it. If an animal is slow in walking, it is not returned unless he stipulated that it is fast. And the slave being a big eater is not a defect, but in a slave-girl it is a defect because she spoils the bed. If he buys a slave and he developed a fever in his hand, and it was also in the seller's hand, if the two times are the same, he returns; if different, no. A large hole in a wall is a defect. Likewise, ants' nests in a vineyard, if excessive, is a defect. Likewise, if there is a passage for others or a drainage. One of them is... (And her being and the slave being a big eater, etc.) is the wording of al-Fath: and little eating in a cow and the like, and much eating in a human, and it was said...
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq lists many defects and case studies. The defects include extra or missing fingers, ulcers, head wounds, all illnesses, swelling of testicles (adar), night blindness ('asha), weakness of sight ('amash), impotence ('unnin), castration (khasi), lying, backbiting, marriage, inability to cook/bake, errors in mushaf, being in 'iddah, etc. The text also covers special cases: if a slave is bought on the condition of being castrated but is not, no option. If a slave-girl's husband divorces her revocably, the buyer may return her; if irrevocably, the option is lost. Prohibition by breastfeeding or in-law relationship is not a defect because the buyer can still benefit by marrying her off. The Khaniyya section focuses on the problem when a slave-girl has a husband and the buyer wants to return her due to this defect. Complex rules of evidence and qawl (the seller's word) are given. Bazzaziyya mentions effeminacy (takhannuth) as a defect if involving bad actions; if just mannerisms, it depends on frequency. Other rulings: a beardless boy with shaved beard can be returned; incontinence is a defect; pregnant slave-girl gives buyer no claim if she gives birth; piercing ears is defect according to Turkish custom but not Indian; worm-eaten wheat can be returned; toothache if recurring is a defect; different eye colors is a defect; cow not giving milk is defect if bought for milk; slow walking animal not returned unless stipulated fast; slave being a big eater is not a defect for male but is for female; fever in hand that matches time of seller's possession allows return; large hole in wall is defect; ants' nests in vineyard if excessive is defect, etc. The passage concludes with a note from Fath al-Qadir about eating.

Key Terms
النكاح في الجارية (al-nikah fi al-jariyah): Marriage of a slave-girl; considered a defect if the marriage still allows the husband to reclaim her (revocable divorce).
الطلاق الرجعي (al-talaq al-raj'i): Revocable divorce; the husband can take back his wife during 'iddah.
الطلاق البائن (al-talaq al-ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage contract.
العدة (al-iddah): Waiting period after divorce or death; a woman cannot remarry during this period.
المصحف (al-mushaf): Copy of the Quran; if it contains errors or missing parts, it is a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following situations is considered a defect in a purchased slave-girl?
A) She is in 'iddah from an irrevocable divorce
B) She is a foster sister of the buyer
C) She is married and her husband divorces her revocably
D) She does not know how to bake bread

Key Takeaway
A marital or prohibition defect exists only if it permanently prevents the buyer from enjoying the primary benefits of the slave, not if the barrier is temporary or can be overcome by sale.

Behavioral, Customary, and Property Defects
List of behavioral, customary, and property defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which behavioral traits, customary practices, and property conditions constitute defects in Hanafi law, and how custom and frequency affect the ruling.

Core Idea
Behavioral defects (lying, backbiting, effeminacy) and property defects (wall hole, ants nests) are judged by frequency and local custom.

Not all undesirable traits are defects. The Hanafi school considers lying, backbiting, and effeminacy as defects, but only when they are habitual or extreme. Similarly, defects in property like a large hole in a wall or excessive ants in a vineyard require customary assessment. This lesson explores these nuanced rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trait or condition
Determine if it is a behavioral flaw (e.g., lying) or a property defect (e.g., wall hole).
Behavioral defects apply to slaves; property defects apply to real estate or items.
2
Assess frequency and severity
For behavioral defects, habitual lying or backbiting is a defect; occasional may not be. For effeminacy, slight mannerisms are not a defect, but major effeminate actions are.
The text distinguishes between bad actions (defect) and mere softness (only if excessive).
3
Consider custom and local practice
Some defects are recognized only if local custom considers them as such. Ear piercing is a defect in Turkish custom but not Indian. Similarly, ants' nests in a vineyard are a defect if excessive.
Custom ('urf) plays a key role in determining defects.
4
Determine the impact on utility
A defect must impair the intended use. Being a big eater is not a defect for a male slave (since it doesn't affect work), but for a female slave it is because it spoils her bed (marriage prospects).
The purpose of purchase is critical.

Physical Defects in Sales: The Right to Return (Khiyar al-'Ayb)
Listing of physical defects with exception for castration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which physical defects in slaves and animals give the buyer the right to return under Hanafi fiqh, and state the exception for castration.

Core Idea
Defects diminish value, so the buyer may return; but a 'better' condition than bargained is no defect.

In Hanafi sales law, the buyer has a right to rescind the contract if the purchased item has a hidden physical defect that diminishes its value. This lesson lists the specific defects recognized for slaves and animals, and highlights a key exception: if the buyer expressly wanted a castrated animal but received a virile one, there is no right to return because the animal is actually more valuable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recognize that the passage states the principle: the presence of certain physical defects gives the buyer the option to return the item (khiyar al-‘ayb).
This is a general rule of sales law.
2
List the specific defects
Read through the Arabic text and list each defect mentioned: extra/missing finger, sores, wounds, all diseases, hernia, night blindness, weak eyesight, impotence, and castration.
These are examples; the phrase 'all diseases' indicates the list is illustrative.
3
Note the exception for castration
Understand that castration (al-khasi) is generally a defect, unless the buyer purchased on the condition that the item is castrated and finds it virile—then no option exists.
The rationale is that virility is superior to castration, so no defect exists.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Take a scenario (e.g., buying a horse with night blindness) and determine if the buyer has the right to return based on the listed defects.
Ensure the defect is covered by the list or falls under 'all diseases'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصبع الزائدة والناقصة والقروح والشجاج والأمراض كلها والأدر عيب وهو انتفاخ الانثيين والعشا عيب وهو الذي لا يبصر بالليل، وكذا العمش والعنين والخصي. ولو اشتراه على أنه خصي فوجده فحلاً لا خيار له
The extra finger and missing finger, and sores, and wounds, and all diseases, and hernia (al-adar) is a defect—it is swelling of the testicles—and night blindness (al-‘asha) is a defect, which is one who does not see at night, and likewise weak eyesight (al-‘amash), and impotence (al-‘innin), and castration (al-khasi). And if he bought it on condition that it is castrated and found it virile, there is no option for him.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage lists physical defects in slaves and animals that give the buyer the right to return the item (khiyar al-‘ayb). The defects include extra or missing fingers, open sores, head wounds, all diseases, hernia (swelling of the testicles), night blindness, weak eyesight, impotence, and castration. However, if a buyer purchases an animal or slave specifically on the condition that it is castrated (e.g., a gelded animal) and later finds it to be virile (i.e., not castrated), he does not have the option to return it because the condition was not fulfilled as he believed—the item is actually better than what he bargained for. This exception highlights that khiyar al-‘ayb applies when the defect diminishes value or utility; a virile animal is more valuable than a castrated one, so no defect exists.

Key Terms
الأدر (al-adar): A disease involving swelling of the testicles (hernia), considered a defect giving the right to return.
العشا (al-‘asha): Night blindness, i.e., inability to see at night, considered a defect.
العمش (al-‘amash): Weak eyesight or constant watering of eyes, considered a defect.
العنين (al-‘innin): Impotence, i.e., inability to perform sexual intercourse, considered a defect.
الخصي (al-khasi): Castration, removal of testicles, considered a defect unless the buyer agreed to it.
خيار (khiyar): In this context, the option to rescind a sale due to a defect (khiyar al-‘ayb).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man buys a slave on the condition that the slave is castrated. After the sale, the slave is found to be virile. What is the ruling under Hanafi fiqh?
A) The buyer may return the slave because the condition was not fulfilled.
B) The buyer has no option to return the slave because the slave is actually more valuable.
C) The buyer may return the slave only if he can prove the seller knew the condition.
D) The buyer must accept the slave but can claim compensation for the difference.

Key Takeaway
The buyer has the right to return a purchased slave or animal for defined physical defects, except when the defect is actually an improvement (e.g., virile instead of castrated).

Partial Return for Separable Defects in Mixed Goods
Ruling on separable defects with analogy to wheat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on finding foreign matter in purchased goods and apply it to similar cases of separable defects.

Core Idea
Foreign matter separable from the good may be returned proportionally without returning the whole.

When you buy a product and find something else mixed in, do you have to return everything or just the defective part? The Hanafi school gives a precise answer based on separability. This lesson unpacks the ruling from al-Bahr al-Raiq on lead in musk, salt in fat, and the analogy to wheat, showing how the buyer's right of partial return operates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defect and its separability
First, determine whether the foreign matter is physically separable from the good without damaging the whole. In the passage, lead in musk is separable; salt in fat and suet in oil are also given as separable examples.
If the foreign matter cannot be separated (e.g., dissolved), the ruling differs.
2
Apply the analogy to the established wheat case
The text says 'like wheat' – referring to the well-known ruling that if wheat is mixed with dirt, the buyer separates the dirt and returns it proportionally. This analogy extends to musk, fat, and oil.
The illa (effective cause) is separability without destroying the good.
3
Calculate the proportional refund
The refund is based on the share (ḥiṣṣa) of the foreign matter relative to the whole. For example, if 5% of the weight is defective lead, the buyer refunds 5% of the price.
The refund is a portion of the price paid for the whole good.
4
Distinguish from inseparable defects
If the foreign matter cannot be separated (e.g., salt dissolved in fat), the buyer cannot claim a partial return. Instead, the buyer may return the entire good for a full refund or keep it with compensation for the defect.
This distinction is crucial in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو وجد في المسك رصاصاً ميزه ورده بحصته قل أو كثر، ولو وجد في الشحم ملحاً كثيراً أو وجد في الدهن ودكاً كثيراً فكالحنطة.
And if he finds in musk lead, he separates it and returns it by its share, little or much. And if he finds in fat much salt, or finds in oil much suet, then it is like wheat.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This is a ruling on defects in mixed or compound goods. The principle: if a foreign substance is found that can be separated without damaging the whole, the buyer may separate it and return it to the seller, receiving a proportional refund. The example of wheat (الحنطة) is a reference to the established ruling that if wheat is mixed with dirt or other defects, the buyer may return a proportional part. This applies to musk, fat, and oil analogously.

Key Terms
عيب (ʿayb): A defect in the goods that reduces value or desirability, giving the buyer an option (khiyar al-ʿayb).
حصة (ḥiṣṣa): The proportional share of the defective part relative to the whole, used to calculate the refund.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a buyer do if he finds a large amount of salt in fat?
A) Return the entire fat for a full refund.
B) Keep the fat and demand a full refund.
C) Separate the salt and return it, receiving a proportional refund.
D) Do nothing, because salt in fat is not considered a defect.

Key Takeaway
If a defect is physically separable, the buyer need only return the defective part, receiving a proportional refund.
