Seller's Acknowledgment of a Defect After Sale
Ruling on buyer's remedy after seller's post-sale admission of defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a seller admits a defect after the sale and identify the buyer's entitlement under Hanafi law.

Core Idea
After sale, seller's admission of a defect does not void the sale; the buyer claims only the difference in value.

In Hanafi commercial law, the seller's post-sale acknowledgment of a defect does not automatically rescind the sale. Instead, as this passage explains, the buyer is limited to claiming the deficiency (nuqsan). This ruling balances fairness with finality of contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the seller's admission after the sale of melted butter that a mouse died in it. The ruling is that the buyer is entitled to compensation (nuqsan), not rescission.
2
Note the condition
The admission occurs after the sale is complete. This is key: if the defect were known before sale, the buyer would have the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb).
3
Identify the remedy
The buyer claims the difference in value between the sound goods and the defective goods (al-nuqṣān). This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
4
Note the authority
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), this is the ruling, and the fatwa is upon it. Abu Hanifa may have differed, but the formal position is the Sahibayn's.
5
Understand the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa possibly held that the sale is void, but the Sahibayn's view is adopted for fatwa. This illustrates that not all differences invalidate the operative ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقر البائع بعد بيع السمن الذائب بموت فأرة فيه عليه المشتري بالنقصان عندهما وعليه الفتوى .اهـ.
The seller acknowledged after selling melted butter the death of a mouse in it. Upon him (the seller) is for the buyer the difference (in value) according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and upon it is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling: after selling melted butter (samn dha'ib), the seller admits that a mouse died in it. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the buyer is entitled to claim the difference in value (al-nuqsan) from the seller, but the sale is not automatically void. The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is this ruling. The buyer does not have the right to return the butter because the defect is discovered after the sale and the seller's admission is not a basis for rescission.

Key Terms
النقصان (al-nuqṣān): The difference in value between the sound price and the defective price.
السمن الذائب (al-samn al-dhāʾib): Melted butter, a liquid fat used in cooking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller admits after selling melted butter that a mouse died in it, what is the buyer entitled to according to the fatwa?
A) Return the butter and get a full refund.
B) Claim the difference in value from the seller.
C) Rescind the sale unilaterally.
D) Nothing, because the admission is not binding.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's remedy after seller's post-sale admission of a defect is limited to claiming the difference in value; the sale itself remains valid.

Defects of Character in a Slave: Gambling, Sorcery, and Drinking
Defining defects based on immoral behavior in a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which character defects in a slave constitute legal defects in a sale according to Hanafi fiqh, and apply the criterion of openness and addiction to distinguish between actionable defects and non-actionable private sins.

Core Idea
A slave's character defect is only recognized in sale if it is habitual and openly practiced, diminishing market value — occasional secret sin is not a defect.

In Hanafi sales law, the buyer has the right to return an item if a defect is discovered. But what counts as a defect? This passage from Jami' al-Fusulayn clarifies that moral vices such as gambling and sorcery are defects because they cause harm, while drinking wine is only a defect if done openly and addictively. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining when a buyer can exercise the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb).

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
Recognize that this passage discusses defects in a slave as a commodity in a sale transaction.
2
List the three vices
Note the three character defects mentioned: gambling, sorcery, and drinking wine.
Gambling includes dice and chess.
3
Determine the defect criterion for each
For gambling and sorcery, the mere practice is a defect. For drinking, only if it is open and addictive, not secret occasional.
4
Identify the underlying principle
Extract that the defect must affect the value or desirability of the slave; only persistent, public vices do that.
5
Apply to hypotheticals
Test scenarios: a slave who gambles occasionally vs. habitually; a slave who drinks secretly vs. openly.
This solidifies the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي جامع الفصولين : وكونه رجع مقامراً إن كان يعد عيباً كقمار نرد و شطرنج ونحوهما فهو عيب، وكذا السحر عيب فيهما لما فيه من الضرر. وشرب الخمر عيب على سبيل الإعلان والإدمان لا على الكتمان أحياناً.
And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: And his being a habitual gambler: if it is considered a defect like gambling with dice and chess and the like, then it is a defect. Similarly, sorcery is a defect in them (the buyer and seller?) because of the harm. And drinking wine is a defect if done openly and addictively, not if done secretly sometimes.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage from Jami' al-Fusulayn discusses defects of character in a slave (or similar commodity). If a slave is known to be a habitual gambler (muqamir), especially with dice or chess, that is a defect because it indicates a vice that reduces value. Similarly, if the slave practices sorcery (sihr), that is a defect due to the harm it causes. Drinking wine (sharb al-khamr) is only a defect if the slave does it openly and addictively (on the basis of i'lan and idman); if it is occasional and secret, it is not considered a defect. This shows that defects are evaluated by their impact on value and desirability in the market.

Key Terms
مقامر (muqāmir): A habitual gambler, especially in games like dice and chess.
سحر (siḥr): Sorcery or magic, considered harmful and thus a defect.
إدمان (idmān): Addiction, habitual consumption of wine, making it a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Whenever it occurs.
B) Only if done secretly.
C) Only if done openly and addictively.
D) Only if done once in public.

Key Takeaway
Only habitual, open vices are recognized as defects in Hanafi sales law; occasional secret sin does not affect the buyer's right to return.

Returning a Horse or Slave for Old Age: The Importance of Stipulating Youth
Ruling on returning a horse or slave due to old age

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether old age is a defect in a purchased animal or slave, and explain the role of stipulation (shart) in giving the buyer a right to return.

Core Idea
Old age is not a defect unless the buyer stipulated youth – the contract governs expectations.

When you buy a horse or a slave, you might assume that old age is a hidden defect that allows you to return the item. However, the Hanafi school takes a different view: old age is visible and not automatically a defect. The buyer must explicitly stipulate that the animal or slave is young to have the right to return. This lesson unpacks that ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and category
The scenario is a sale of a horse (or slave girl) where the buyer later discovers the item is old. This falls under khiyar al-aib (option of defect) in Hanafi law.
The key question is whether old age qualifies as a hidden defect (aib khafi) that triggers the right to return.
2
State the general ruling
The text says 'he should not return it' (la turaddu). This means old age alone is not sufficient grounds for return.
The ruling is based on the fact that old age is apparent and not a hidden defect.
3
Identify the exception
The buyer can only return if he explicitly stipulated (sharat) that the item is young. The stipulation makes youth a binding condition.
The Arabic 'illa idha sharata sighar al-sinn' clearly shows the exception.
4
Note the analogy
The text extends the ruling from horse to slave girl by saying 'ka al-jariyah' – the same rule applies.
This analogy (qiyas) shows the principle is general for living beings where age is observable.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Consider buying a camel without mentioning age. According to this ruling, you cannot return it for being old unless you stipulated youth. The same logic applies to any animal where age is visible.
The key is whether the characteristic is visible or hidden. Old age is visible, so it's not a hidden defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشترى فرساً فوجده كبير السن قيل ينبغي أن لا ترد إلا إذا شرط صغر السن كالجارية إذا وجدها كبيرة السن .اهـ.
He bought a horse and found it old. It was said: he should not return it unless he stipulated young age, like a slave girl if he finds her old.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This ruling discusses whether old age (kibar al-sinn) is a defect that allows return. The opinion cited (qil) is that the buyer cannot return the horse solely for being old, unless the buyer explicitly stipulated that the horse is young (sharat sighar al-sinn). Similarly, if a man buys a slave girl and finds her old, he cannot return her unless youth was stipulated. This indicates that old age is not inherently a defect; it depends on the buyer's expectations and clear stipulation. The analogy: both horse and slave girl, age is not a hidden defect unless conditioned.

Key Terms
كبر السن (kibar al-sinn): Old age, which is not automatically a defect unless the buyer stipulated youth.
شرط (sharaṭa): To stipulate a condition in the sale contract, making it binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If the horse is very old.
B) If the buyer stipulated that the horse is young.
C) If the seller knew the age.
D) Never, old age is not a defect.

Key Takeaway
Old age is not a hidden defect; without a stipulation of youth, the buyer cannot return a horse or slave.

Physical Defects in Horses and Animals
Detailed list of horse defects from al-Zahiriyyah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and define the physical defects in horses that constitute a legal defect (ʿayb) in Hanafi sales law, and explain why even superstitious or aesthetic flaws are included.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a defect is any imperfection that reduces the animal's value, including superstitious marks like the haqʿa ring.

When buying a horse, the buyer has the right to return it if a hidden defect is found. But what counts as a defect? The Hanafi scholars provided a detailed list of physical imperfections. This lesson examines the definitions from al-Zahiriyyah, showing how even a mole on the wrong spot or a ring believed to be ill-omened can give the buyer the option of return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defects listed
Scan the Arabic text and note each distinct defect term mentioned, such as al-dafan, al-ajhar, al-dahs, etc.
There are approximately eight defects explicitly named.
2
Understand the definitions
For each defect term, match the Arabic word with the English description provided in the translation and commentary.
Pay attention to anatomical locations and specific conditions.
3
Note the inclusion of aesthetic and superstitious defects
Observe how the mole on the nose vs. cheek, and the ring on the chest (haq'a), are treated as defects even though they are not purely physical impairments.
This illustrates the Hanafi approach that any reduction in market value, even from superstition, is legally relevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية والدفن عيب وهو أن يسيل الماء من المنخرين. والأجهر عيب وهو من لا يبصر في النهار والدحس وهو ورم يكون في أطرة حافر الفرس والأطرة دور الحافر، والفدع عوج في الرسغ بينه وبين الساعد وفي القدم عوج بين عظم الساق، وفي الفرس التواء الرسغ من الجانب الأيمن. والجرذ عيب وهو بالذال المعجمة كل ما حدث في عرقوب الدابة من ترند أو انتفاخ عصب والهقعة وهي دائرة في عرض زور يعد عيباً ويتشاءم به ومنه يقال اتقوا الخيل المهقوع والزور أعلى الصدر وفسره في المنتقى فقال : المهقوع الذي إذا سار سمع ما بين حاصرتيه وفرجه صوت والانتشار وهو انتفاخ العصب عند الإعياء وتحرك الشظى كانتشار العصب غير أن الفرس لانتشار العصب أشد احتمالاً منه لتحرك الشظى والشظى عظم ملتزق بالذراع والشامة إن كانت على الخد كانت زينة فإن كانت على الأرنبة كانت قبحاً .اهـ.
And in al-Zahiriyyah: al-dafan is a defect, which is water flowing from the nostrils. And al-ajhar is a defect, which is one who does not see in the daytime. And al-dahs is a swelling in the rim of the horse's hoof, and the rim is the circle of the hoof. And al-fada' is a bend in the pastern between it and the forearm, and in the foot a bend between the shin bone. And in the horse, a twist of the pastern from the right side. And al-jaradh is a defect, which by the letter dhal (the pointed) is everything that occurs in the hock of the animal from a rupture or swelling of the nerve. And al-haq'a is a ring in the width of the upper chest, considered a defect and considered ill-omened, and from it they say 'beware of the haq' horses'. And al-zawr is the upper chest. And he explained in al-Muntaqa: al-mahqu' is one who, when he walks, a sound is heard between his flanks and his groin. And al-intishar is swelling of the nerve at fatigue and movement of the shadha (splint bone) like the spread of the nerve, except that the horse is more tolerant of nerve spread than of movement of the splint bone. And the splint bone is a bone attached to the forearm. And al-shamma (mole): if it is on the cheek, it is adornment; if it is on the tip of the nose, it is ugliness.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage from al-Zahiriyyah lists numerous physical defects in horses and other animals. Each is defined as a defect (ʿayb) that may give the buyer the option of return. The list includes: dafan (nasal discharge), ajhar (day-blindness), dahs (swelling of the hoof rim), fada' (bent pastern), jaradh (hock issues), haq'a (a ring on the chest considered ill-omened), intishar (nerve swelling), and shadha (splint bone issue). Even a mole (shama) on the nose tip is considered ugly and a defect, while on the cheek it is adornment. This shows the detailed casuistry of Hanafi fiqh in defining defects.

Key Terms
الدفن (al-dafan): Nasal discharge, water flowing from nostrils, considered a defect.
الأجهر (al-ajhar): Day-blindness, inability to see in daylight, a defect.
الفدع (al-fadaʿ): Bend or twist in the pastern or foot, a defect.
الهقعة (al-haqʿa): A ring on the horse's chest, considered ill-omened and a defect.
الدحس (al-dahs): Swelling in the rim of the horse's hoof.
الجرذ (al-jaradh): Any issue in the hock (e.g., rupture or nerve swelling), a defect.
الانتشار (al-intishar): Swelling of the nerve at fatigue; the horse is more tolerant of this than splint bone movement.
الشظى (al-shadha): Splint bone attached to the forearm; its movement is a defect.
الشامة (al-shamma): Mole; on the cheek it is adornment, on the nose tip it is ugliness (defect).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT considered a defect in a horse?
A) Al-dafan
B) Al-ajhar
C) A mole on the cheek
D) Al-haq'a

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines defect broadly, including physical, aesthetic, and even superstitious flaws, as long as they reduce the animal's market value.

Inscription on Shop Door: Not a Legal Defect
Ruling on inscription as defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a written inscription on a shop door claiming endowment does not constitute a legal defect giving the right to return.

Core Idea
A sign (ʿalāma) does not build legal rulings; the buyer cannot return based on a mere inscription.

The buyer of a shop finds a written claim on the door stating the shop is a waqf for a mosque. Can he return the shop? The Hanafi ruling in al-Qunyah says no, because a mere inscription is not legally binding. This lesson unpacks the principle that signs do not establish substantive legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alleged defect
The buyer claims the shop is defective because of the written inscription that it is a waqf. An inscription is a physical mark, but its legal significance must be evaluated.
The inscription is not a change in the shop's inherent quality.
2
Determine the nature of the inscription
The inscription is a statement, not a legal document. It merely claims that the shop is a waqf. Hanafi law distinguishes between a sign (ʿalāma) and legal proof.
A sign does not establish a legal reality; only proper documentation or testimony can.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
The text states: 'because it is a sign that does not build rulings upon.' This means one cannot base a legal claim (like return) on such an inscription alone.
The ruling presumes the sale is valid; the inscription is irrelevant unless substantiated.
4
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, the buyer cannot return the shop. The defect claim fails because the inscription is not a recognized defect.
If the shop were actually a waqf, the buyer would have recourse through invalidation, not return for defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : اشترى حانوتاً فوجد بعد القبض على بابه مكتوباً وقف على مسجد كذا لا يرده لأنها علامة لا تبنى الأحكام عليها. اشترى أرضاً فظهر أنها ميشومة فينبغي أن يتمكن من الرد لأن الناس لا يرغبون فيها، ولو اشترى حماراً لا ينهق فهو عيب. وترك الصلاة في العبد لا يوجب الرد .اهـ.
And in al-Qunyah: He bought a shop and found after possession on its door written 'endowment for such-and-such mosque'; he does not return it because it is a sign that does not build rulings upon. He bought land and it appeared that it contains dead bodies; so he should be able to return because people do not desire it. And if he bought a donkey that does not bray, it is a defect. And leaving prayer in a slave does not impose return.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage from al-Qunyah presents several distinct rulings. First, if a buyer purchases a shop and finds an inscription on the door stating it is a waqf (endowment) for a mosque, he cannot return the shop because the inscription is merely a sign (ʿalama) and does not legally establish the endowment; it cannot be built upon. Second, if one buys land and discovers it contains human remains (mayshuma = filled with dead bodies), the buyer should have the right of return because people generally do not desire such land (a de facto defect). Third, a donkey that does not bray (la yanhiq) is considered defective. Fourth, if a slave neglects prayer (tark al-salah), that does not give the right to return the slave because it is not considered a legal defect in the sale context (perhaps because it is not inherent but a voluntary act).

Key Terms
علامة (ʿalāma): A sign or inscription, which does not establish legal rulings by itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can the buyer not return the shop with the waqf inscription?
A) Because the shop is still usable.
B) Because the inscription is a sign that does not build rulings.
C) Because the buyer knew about it before purchase.
D) Because the inscription is true.

Key Takeaway
A mere inscription on a shop door is not a legal defect; the buyer cannot return based on it.

Land with Dead Bodies: A Defect Allowing Return
Ruling on land with dead bodies as defect

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Land with dead bodies (mayshuma) is a defect because people do not desire it; the buyer may return it.

The buyer purchases land and later discovers it contains dead bodies. Is this a defect? The Hanafi ruling says yes, because it repels potential buyers. Let's examine the reasoning that makes this a valid defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object and alleged defect
The object is land, and the alleged defect is the presence of dead bodies (mayshuma). This is a physical condition of the land.
Unlike the shop inscription, this is a real physical change.
2
Understand the basis for considering it a defect
The text says: 'because people do not desire it.' In Hanafi law, a defect (ayb) is something that diminishes the value or desirability of the item in the market.
Desirability is a key criterion.
3
Contrast with the shop inscription
The inscription did not affect the physical condition, but here the land physically contains remains, making it repugnant to buyers.
Physical vs. informational difference.
4
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, the buyer has the right to return the land due to this defect.
The return is by khiyar al-ayb.

Donkey That Does Not Bray: A Physical Defect
Ruling on donkey that does not bray as defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify that a donkey's inability to bray is considered a physical defect entitling the buyer to return.

Core Idea
A donkey that does not bray is defective because it lacks a normal attribute expected in such animals.

If a person buys a donkey and it does not bray, can they return it? The Hanafi ruling says yes, because braying is a natural and expected trait of healthy donkeys. This lesson explores what constitutes a physical defect in livestock.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item and the defect
The item is a donkey, and the defect is that it does not bray (la yanhiq). Braying is a normal characteristic of donkeys.
This is a functional defect, not a cosmetic one.
2
Understand why it is a defect
The text simply states 'it is a defect' (huwa ʿayb). In Hanafi law, any departure from the normal expected qualities of an animal is a defect if it affects utility or value.
No further reasoning is given; it is a direct ruling.
3
Compare with human slave's prayer neglect
Note that a slave's religious practice is not considered a defect because it is a voluntary act, not an inherent quality. The donkey's inability to bray is innate, not a matter of choice.
This contrast highlights the nature of defects.
4
Conclude the ruling
The buyer may return the donkey due to this defect.
The return is via khiyar al-ayb.

Source Text

Slave's Prayer Neglect: Not a Defect
Ruling on slave's prayer neglect as defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a slave's neglect of prayer does not give the right to return the slave under Hanafi law.

Core Idea
A slave's personal religious practice is not an inherent defect; it is a voluntary act that can be corrected.

If a purchased slave neglects prayer, can the buyer return the slave for that reason? The Hanafi ruling says no, because prayer neglect is a matter of personal conduct, not a physical or innate defect. This lesson examines why voluntary acts do not constitute defects in the context of sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item and the alleged defect
The item is a slave, and the alleged defect is that he neglects prayer (tark al-ṣalāh). This is a behavioral issue, not a physical condition.
The slave was purchased as a human being with free will.
2
Understand why it is not a defect
The text states: 'does not impose return.' The reason is that prayer neglect is a voluntary act; it is not an inherent quality of the slave. The buyer can encourage the slave to pray but cannot return for this reason.
Hanafi law distinguishes between inherent defects and behavioral choices.
3
Contrast with physical defects
Unlike a donkey that does not bray (inherent), the slave's prayer neglect is changeable. The defect must be intrinsic to the item at the time of sale.
The slave's religious observance is not a fixed attribute.
4
Conclude the ruling
The buyer cannot return the slave. He may attempt to correct the behavior but has no legal recourse for return.
The sale is valid and final in this regard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : اشترى حانوتاً فوجد بعد القبض على بابه مكتوباً وقف على مسجد كذا لا يرده لأنها علامة لا تبنى الأحكام عليها. اشترى أرضاً فظهر أنها ميشومة فينبغي أن يتمكن من الرد لأن الناس لا يرغبون فيها، ولو اشترى حماراً لا ينهق فهو عيب. وترك الصلاة في العبد لا يوجب الرد .اهـ.
And in al-Qunyah: He bought a shop and found after possession on its door written 'endowment for such-and-such mosque'; he does not return it because it is a sign that does not build rulings upon. He bought land and it appeared that it contains dead bodies; so he should be able to return because people do not desire it. And if he bought a donkey that does not bray, it is a defect. And leaving prayer in a slave does not impose return.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage from al-Qunyah presents several distinct rulings. First, if a buyer purchases a shop and finds an inscription on the door stating it is a waqf (endowment) for a mosque, he cannot return the shop because the inscription is merely a sign (ʿalama) and does not legally establish the endowment; it cannot be built upon. Second, if one buys land and discovers it contains human remains (mayshuma = filled with dead bodies), the buyer should have the right of return because people generally do not desire such land (a de facto defect). Third, a donkey that does not bray (la yanhiq) is considered defective. Fourth, if a slave neglects prayer (tark al-salah), that does not give the right to return the slave because it is not considered a legal defect in the sale context (perhaps because it is not inherent but a voluntary act).

Key Terms
ترك الصلاة (tark al-ṣalāh): Neglect of prayer by the slave, not considered a defect for return.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a slave's prayer neglect not give the right to return?
A) Because prayer is not obligatory on slaves.
B) Because it is a voluntary act, not a defect.
C) Because the buyer can force the slave to pray.
D) Because the seller is not responsible for the slave's behavior.

Key Takeaway
A slave's neglect of prayer is not a defect; the buyer cannot return based on personal conduct.

Defects in Fingers and the Quran
Ruling statement on counting defects in sales of body parts and Quran manuscripts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for counting defects in body parts (fingers) and in a Quran manuscript, and apply them to determine the number of defects.

Core Idea
A single finger cut is one defect; all fingers with the palm count as one; missing letters or dots in a mushaf are defects.

This lesson explains how defects are quantified in sales of body parts (such as a slave) and in a Quran manuscript according to Fath al-Qadir. Knowing these categories helps buyers exercise the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) properly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Determine whether the ruling is about body parts (fingers) or a mushaf.
The text gives separate rules for each.
2
Count the defects for fingers
Note: one finger cut = one defect; two fingers cut = two defects; all fingers with the palm = one defect.
The palm unites the fingers into a single defect.
3
Identify defects in the mushaf
List what counts as a defect: deletion of letters, missing dots (nuqat), or missing diacritics (i'rab).
Even minor textual imperfections are defects.
4
Understand the ruling's implication
The number of defects affects the buyer's right to return the item or claim compensation.
Multiple defects may allow multiple claims if separate, but if united by the palm, only one claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي آخر الباب من فتح القدير: قطع الأصبح عيب والأصبعان عيبان والأصابح مع الكف عيب واحد، وحذف الحروف أو نقصها أو النقط أو الاعراب في المصحف عيب. له المصحف الحركات الثلاثة ذكره الكرماني من شرح كتاب الإمامة . فائدة في ميم والمصراة شاة ونحوها اشد ضرعها ليجتمع لبنها ليظن المشتري أنها كثيرة اللبن فإذا حلبها ليس ردها عندنا، ولا يرجع بالنقصان في رواية الكرخي ويرجع في رواية الطحاوي لفوات وصف مرغوب بعد زيادة منفصلة، ولو اختيرت للفتوى كان حسناً لغرور المشتري بالتصرية اهـ.
And at the end of the chapter in Fath al-Qadir: Cutting of one finger is a defect, two fingers are two defects, and the fingers with the palm are one defect. And deletion of letters, their deficiency, dots, or diacritics in the Quran is a defect. For him (?) the Quran with the three vowels, mentioned by al-Kirmani in Sharh Kitab al-Imamah. Benefit: In mis'el and the like: a goat etc. whose udder is tied to collect milk so that the buyer thinks it has much milk, then when he milks it, he cannot return it according to us, and he does not claim the deficiency in the narration of al-Karkhi, but he does claim in the narration of al-Tahawi due to loss of a desired attribute after a separate increase. And if it were chosen for fatwa, it would be good due to deceiving the buyer by mis'el.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage has two parts. First, from Fath al-Qadir: cutting off one finger is a single defect, two fingers are two defects (i.e., each is independently actionable), but all fingers with the palm are considered one defect (since the loss of the whole hand is a single injury). In a Quranic manuscript (mushaf), deletion or deficiency of letters, dots (nuqat), or diacritics (i'rab) is a defect because it affects readability and accuracy. The reference to 'three vowels' by al-Kirmani likely means that the mushaf should have the three vowel signs (fatha, kasra, damma). The second part (fa'ida) introduces the issue of mis'el (tying the udder to deceive), which is a separate topic but included here as a benefit. The Hanafi position is that the buyer cannot return the animal for mis'el, but there are two narrations about claiming the difference in value.

Key Terms
المصحف (al-muṣḥaf): A written copy of the Quran; defects include missing letters or diacritics.
الأصبع (al-iṣbaʿ): Finger; each finger is a separate defect, but the whole hand with palm is one.
العيب (al-ʿayb): A defect that gives the buyer the right of return or compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many defects does cutting off all fingers along with the palm constitute?
A) Five defects.
B) One defect.
C) Two defects.
D) Ten defects.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a single missing finger is one defect, two are two, but all fingers with the palm count as one; for the mushaf, textual imperfections like missing letters, dots, or vowels are defects.

The Ruling on Mis'el (Tying the Udder)
Fiqh ruling on mis'el with intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on mis'el, identify the two narrations (Karkhi and Tahawi) regarding compensation, and explain the preferred fatwa.

Core Idea
Mis'el is not a defect allowing return, but the buyer may claim compensation for deception in some narrations.

This lesson addresses a specific form of deception in livestock sales: tying the udder to falsely indicate high milk yield. The Hanafi school does not consider this a defect that allows return, but there are differing narrations about whether the buyer can claim the difference in value.

Guided Path
1
Define mis'el
Understand that mis'el refers to an animal whose udder is tied to collect milk, making the buyer think it is a heavy milker.
This is a form of deceit (ghurur).
2
State the Hanafi position on return
The buyer cannot return the animal for mis'el; it is not considered a defect (ayb) per school.
3
Identify the two narrations on compensation
Al-Karkhi narrates: no claim for deficiency. Al-Tahawi narrates: the buyer may claim for loss of a desired attribute. This is a dispute within the school.
The difference hinges on whether the loss is due to a separate increase (milk collection).
4
Determine the preferred fatwa
The text suggests that choosing the Tahawi narration (allowing compensation) is good practice because it protects the deceived buyer.
Tarjih based on public interest and preventing ghurur.

Source Text

And at the end of the chapter in Fath al-Qadir: Cutting of one finger is a defect, two fingers are two defects, and the fingers with the palm are one defect. Commentary
This passage has two parts. First, from Fath al-Qadir: cutting off one finger is a single defect, two fingers are two defects (i.e., each is independently actionable), but all fingers with the palm are considered one defect (since the loss of the whole hand is a single injury). الغُرور (al-ghurūr): Deception; a cause for liability in Hanafi law.

Mis'el: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Returning a Deceptively Enlarged Udder Animal
Ruling on mis'el and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the majority Hanafi view and Abu Yusuf's view on mis'el, and apply the distinction between mughtarr and maghrur.

Core Idea
A buyer deceived by mis'el cannot return the animal in the majority Hanafi view, but Abu Yusuf allows return with compensation of a sa' of dates.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explores the case of mis'el – an animal whose udder is tied to simulate high milk yield. The text presents the dominant Hanafi position that this is not a defect (ʿayb) allowing return, yet acknowledges a conflicting view from Abu Yusuf that aligns with a well-known hadith. The key legal distinction is between being merely deceived (mughtarr) versus being fraudulently misled (maghrur).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal classification
Mis'el is the act of tying an animal's udder to make it appear to have more milk. Determine whether this constitutes a defect (ʿayb) in the sold item.
The passage explicitly says 'mis'el is not a defect according to us'.
2
State the majority Hanafi position
The buyer cannot return the animal. Compensation for the deficiency is not due according to al-Karkhi, but is due according to al-Tahawi. The preferred fatwa leans toward compensation.
There are two narrations within the school.
3
Compare with Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that the buyer may return the animal and receive the value of a sa' of dates, while keeping the milk. This is closer to the hadith of mis'el.
The hadith obligates a sa' of dates; Abu Yusuf obligates its value.
4
Analyze the hadith evidence
The hadith (Bukhari and Muslim) gives the buyer an option to keep the animal with a sa' of dates. The majority interprets this as compensation for the milk, not a return. Abu Yusuf interprets it as a condition for return.
The passage notes that Abu Yusuf's view is closer to the hadith.
5
Apply the mughtarr/maghrur distinction
The buyer is considered mughtarr (deceived) not maghrur (defrauded), so he cannot rescind the sale. Similar analogies: blackened fingertips of a slave, baker's clothing.
The distinction is crucial: maghrur can rescind, mughtarr cannot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة في ميم والمصراة شاة ونحوها اشد ضرعها ليجتمع لبنها ليظن المشتري أنها كثيرة اللبن فإذا حلبها ليس ردها عندنا، ولا يرجع بالنقصان في رواية الكرخي ويرجع في رواية الطحاوي لفوات وصف مرغوب بعد زيادة منفصلة، ولو اختيرت للفتوى كان حسناً لغرور المشتري بالتصرية اهـ. وفي الظهيرية التصرية ليست بعيب عندنا وكذا لو سود أنامل عبده وأجلسه على المعرض حتى ظنه المشتري كاتباً أو ألبسه ثياب الخبازين حتى ظنه خبازاً فليس له أن يرده لأنه مغتر وليس بمغرور .اهـ. وفي الحاوي القدسي في المصراة وعن أبي يوسف أنه يردها وقيمة صاع من تمر ويحبس لبنها لنفسه .اهـ. وهو أقرب إلى حديث المصراة الثابت في الصحيحين إلا أن الحديث أوجب رد الصاع وهو أوجب قيمته
Benefit: In mis'el, a goat etc. whose udder is tied to collect milk so that the buyer thinks it has much milk, then when he milks it, he cannot return it according to us, and he does not claim the deficiency in the narration of al-Karkhi, but he does claim in the narration of al-Tahawi due to loss of a desired attribute after a separate increase. And if it were chosen for fatwa, it would be good due to deceiving the buyer by mis'el. And in al-Zahiriyyah: mis'el is not a defect according to us. Similarly, if he blackened the fingertips of his slave and seated him at the display so that the buyer thought he was a writer, or dressed him in baker's clothes so that he thought he was a baker, then he (the buyer) cannot return him because he is deceived (mughtarr) but not defrauded (maghrur). And in al-Hawi al-Qudsi regarding mis'el: from Abu Yusuf that he returns her and the value of a sa' of dates, and he keeps her milk for himself. And it is closer to the hadith of mis'el established in the two Sahihs, except that the hadith obligated the return of a sa' (of dates), and he obligated its value.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This fa'ida (benefit) discusses the issue of mis'el, i.e., artificially enlarging an animal's udder by tying it to collect milk and deceive the buyer into thinking it produces much milk. The Hanafi position (according to the text) is that the buyer cannot return the animal (la raddaha), and according to al-Karkhi's narration, the buyer cannot even claim the difference in value. However, in al-Tahawi's narration, he can claim compensation because a desired attribute (the milk) is lost after a separate increase (the tying). The preferred fatwa is to allow compensation due to the deception. Al-Zahiriyyah confirms that mis'el is not a defect (laysat bi-ʿayb), drawing an analogy: if a seller makes a slave appear literate by blackening his fingertips or dresses him as a baker, the buyer cannot return because he is merely deceived (mughtarr) but not fraudulently misled (maghrur) in a legal sense. A contrary view from Abu Yusuf (recorded in al-Hawi al-Qudsi) allows return plus a sa' of dates as compensation, which aligns more closely with the hadith on mis'el in Sahih Bukhari and Muslim. The hadith orders the return of a sa' of dates (not its value), but Abu Yusuf's ruling replaces the sa' with its value.

Key Terms
المصراة (al-muṣarrāh): An animal whose udder is tied to deceive the buyer about milk production.
مغتر (mughtarr): One who is deceived, but not legally defrauded (maghrur). The sale is valid.
مغرور (maghrūr): One who is legally defrauded, which gives the right to rescind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view (as presented in this passage), what is the ruling for the buyer of a goat that was tied (mis'el)?
A) The buyer can return the goat and claim a refund.
B) The buyer cannot return the goat but may claim the difference in value.
C) The buyer must keep the goat and cannot claim anything.
D) The buyer can return the goat and receive a sa' of dates.

Key Takeaway
The majority Hanafi view on mis'el distinguishes mughtarr from maghrur: no return, only compensation in some narrations.

New Defect at Buyer: Forced Return Barred
Ruling on subsequent defects affecting return rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a new defect arises at the buyer after discovering an old defect, and apply it to distinguish between forced return and compensation for the deficiency.

Core Idea
A new defect at the buyer after discovering an old defect bars forced return; only compensation is owed unless the seller consents.

When you buy something and later discover a hidden defect, you normally have the right to return it. But what if a new defect occurs after you take possession? This lesson unpacks that scenario: the Hanafi school generally prevents you from forcing the seller to take it back, limiting you to a claim for the loss in value caused by the old defect. However, if the seller agrees, return is allowed. Several sub-rules depend on who caused the new defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two defects
Separate the old defect that existed at the time of sale from the new defect that arises after the buyer takes possession.
Only the old defect triggers the original right of return (khiyar al-ʿayb).
2
Apply the general rule: no forced return
If a new defect occurs, the buyer cannot force the seller to accept the goods back. The buyer's recourse is to claim compensation (al-nuqsan) for the diminution in value caused by the old defect.
The seller is protected because the item left their possession sound and would return defective.
3
Identify the exception: seller's consent
If the seller voluntarily agrees to take back the goods despite the new defect, the buyer may return them. The seller's consent overcomes the bar.
This is because the seller accepts the harm of the new defect.
4
Classify the cause of the new defect
The ruling varies based on whether the new defect was caused by the buyer, the seller, a third party, the item itself, or a heavenly affliction (natural disaster). For each, the buyer's options differ.
For example, if the buyer caused it, he must pay the full price; if the seller caused it, the buyer retains the right to return.
5
Apply to specific examples
Work through the cases given: a slave who commits a killing at seller and then at buyer; a recurring sore; fruit that spoils during transport. Determine whether forced return is allowed or only compensation.
These illustrate the nuance: the new defect must be truly new, not a recurrence of the old defect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فلو حدث آخر عند المشتري رجع بنقصانه أو رد برضا بائعه) أي حدث بعدما اطلع على العيب القديم امتنع رده جبراً على البائع لدفع الإضرار عنه لكونه خرج عن ملكه سالماً ويعود معيباً فتعين الرجوع بالنقصان إلا أن يرضى البائع بما حدث لرضاه بالضرر إلا في مسألة فإن البائع إذا رضي بالعيب الحادث فإن المشتري لا يجبر على رده وإنما يرجع بالنقصان هي ما إذا اشترى عبداً فظهر أنه قتل انساناً خطأ عند البائع ثم قتل آخر عند المشتري فإن البائع إذا أراد قبوله بالجنايتين لا يجبر المشتري وإنما يرجع بنقصان الجناية الأولى دفعاً للضرر عنه لأنه لو رده على بائعه كان مختار للفداء فيهما وتمامه في الولوالجية. أطلق في الحدوث فشمل ما إذا كان بآفة سماوية أو بغيرها؛ كذا في المعراج. وشمل ما إذا اشتراه مريضاً فازداد في يده فإنه ليس له الرد، وقيل ينبغي أن يرد كما في وجع السن إذا ازداد إلا إذا صار صاحب فراش ؛ كذا في خزانة الفقه. وفي جامع الفصولين : إذا تعيب عند المشتري بفعله أو بفعل أجنبي أو بآفة سماوية وظاهره أنه إذا تعيب عنده بفعل البائع لا يمتنع الرد وظاهر اطلاق الكتاب امتناع الرد جبراً أيضاً وفي القنية اشترى عبداً . وبه أثر قرحة وبرأت منه ولم يعلم به ثم عادت قرحة فاخبر الجراحون أن عودها بالعيب القديم لم يرده ويرجع بنقصان العيب وهذا بخلاف مسألة كانت به قرحة فانفجرت أو جدري فانفجر عند المشتري فله الرد لأن انفجاره ليس بعيب حادث اهـ ومن العيب الحادث ما لو اشترى ماله حمل ومؤنة في بلد فأراد أن يرده بعيب قديم في بلد آخر ليس له الرد جبر إلا في بلد العقد كالثمر ومن العيب الحادث نتف ريش الطير المذبوح فيمتنع الرد كما في القنية ثم أعلم أن حدوث العيب عند المشتري شامل لما إذا نقص عنده وحاصل ما إذا نقص المبيع إنه لا يخلوا ما أن يكون في يد البائع أو يد المشتري فإن كان الأول فعلى خمسة أوجه بفعل البائع أو بفعل المشتري أو أجنبي أو المعقود عليه أو بآفة سماوية فإن بفعل البائع خير المشتري وجد به عيباً أو لا إن شاء تركه وإن شاء أخذه وطرح من الثمن حصة النقصان وإن كان بفعل المشتري لزمه جميع الثمن وليس له أن يمسكه ويطلب النقصان ولو منعه البائع بعد جناية المشتري لأجل الثمن فللمشتري رده بالعيب ويسقط عنه الثمن إلا ما نقصه بفعله وإن كان النقصان بفعل الأجنبي فالمشتري بالخيار بعيب أو لا إن شاء رضي به بجميع الثمن واتبع الجاني بإرشه وإن شاء تركه وسقط عنه الثمن وإن كان النقصان بآفة سماوية أو بفعل المعقود
His saying: (So if another defect arises at the buyer, he returns by its deficiency or returns with the seller's consent) i.e., after he discovers the old defect, he is prevented from returning it forcibly against the seller to prevent harm to him because it left his property sound and returns defective; so recourse is to claim the deficiency unless the seller agrees to the new defect due to his acceptance of harm. Except in one case: if the seller is pleased with the new defect, the buyer is not forced to return, but rather claims the deficiency. That is when he bought a slave and it appeared that he killed a person by mistake while with the seller, then the slave killed another while with the buyer; if the seller wants to accept him with both crimes, the buyer is not forced, but rather claims the deficiency of the first crime to prevent harm. Its completion is in al-Walwalji. The (word) 'occurrence' is general, including if it is by a heavenly affliction or otherwise; as in al-Mi'raj. And it includes if he bought it sick and it increased in his hand, then he has no return. And it was said he should return, like a toothache if it increases, unless he becomes bedridden; as in Khizanat al-Fiqh. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If it is defective at the buyer by his action or by an outsider's action or by a heavenly affliction, it seems that if it becomes defective by the seller's action, return is not prevented. And the apparent absolute statement of the book prevents return forcibly. And in al-Qunyah: He bought a slave and there was a trace of a sore that healed, and he did not know, then the sore returned. The doctors informed that its return was due to the old defect; he does not return and claims the deficiency of the defect. This is different from the case if there was a sore that burst open or smallpox that burst at the buyer, then he may return because its bursting is not a new defect. And from the new defect: if he bought goods that have transport and cost in a town and wants to return due to an old defect in another town, he has no forced return except in the town of contract, like fruit. And from the new defect: plucking the feathers of a slaughtered bird; return is prevented as in al-Qunyah. Then know that the occurrence of a defect at the buyer includes if it decreases in his hand. The summary: if the goods decrease, it is either in the hands of the seller or buyer. If the former, then five ways: by action of seller, buyer, outsider, the subject matter, or heavenly affliction. If by seller's action, the buyer has the option, whether he finds a defect or not; if he wishes he leaves it, if he wishes he takes it and deducts from the price the share of the deficiency. If by buyer's action, he must pay the full price and cannot keep it and demand reduction; if the seller prevents him after the buyer's injury for the sake of the price, the buyer may return due to the defect and the price is dropped except for what he damaged by his action. If the deficiency is by an outsider's action, the buyer has the option: either keep it with full price and follow the aggressor for damages, or leave it and the price drops. If the deficiency is by a heavenly affliction or by the subject matter (the contracted thing itself)...
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This extensive passage deals with the scenario where a new defect (hadath) occurs while the goods are with the buyer after sale. The main ruling: if a new defect arises after the buyer discovers an old defect, the buyer loses the right to force the seller to take back the goods (jabran); instead, he can only claim the difference in value for the old defect. The seller's consent may allow return. An exception: if the seller caused the new defect, the buyer might retain the right of return. Various sub-scenarios are discussed: if the buyer causes the defect, he must pay full price; if an outsider causes it, the buyer has options; if it is a heavenly affliction or caused by the item itself, different rules apply. Specific examples: a slave who killed a person first at seller, then at buyer; an old sore that recurs; fruit that spoils during transport. The passage ends with a general rule categorizing the locus of the deficiency (in seller's hand or buyer's hand) and the five possible causes of loss.

Key Terms
حدوث العيب (ḥudūth al-ʿayb): A new defect arising after the sale, which affects the buyer's option of return.
آفة سماوية (āfa samāwiyya): A heavenly affliction (natural disaster) not caused by any person.
المعقود عليه (al-maʿqūd ʿalayhi): The contracted subject matter itself (e.g., the animal that causes damage).
النقصان (al-nuqsān): The compensation for the deficiency in value caused by the old defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a new defect arises at the buyer after he discovers an old defect, what is his primary recourse?
A) He can force the seller to take back the goods.
B) He can only claim the difference in value (al-nuqsan).
C) He can neither return nor claim compensation.
D) He can rescind the contract unilaterally.

Key Takeaway
A new defect at the buyer after discovering an old defect bars forced return; the buyer's remedy is compensation for the old defect, unless the seller consents.

Module 2 · Lesson 249

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
The New Defect and the Buyer's Option: Compensation vs. Return
Expansion on khiyar al-'ayb with new defects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a new defect occurs after the buyer discovers an old defect and apply the method for calculating the depreciation compensation.

Core Idea
A new defect blocks forced return but not compensation; the seller's consent reopens the return door.

When a buyer discovers a hidden defect in a purchased item, he normally has the right to return it. But what if, after learning of the defect, he himself causes or witnesses a new defect? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that the buyer cannot force the seller to take back the item, but he can claim a proportional refund for the old defect. Understanding this nuance prevents injustice to either party and is essential for anyone engaged in sales transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
Read the opening sentence: when a new defect occurs after the buyer becomes aware of the old defect, forced return (jabran) is prevented. The buyer can only claim the depreciation (nuqsan) unless the seller consents to the return.
This is the foundational principle.
2
Analyze the Exception
Note the exception regarding the slave who commits two homicides: even if the seller accepts the return, the buyer is not forced. Understand the rationale: to avoid harm to the buyer (he would have to ransom the slave).
The exception highlights the principle of avoiding harm (darar).
3
Distinguish Depreciation Before vs. After Possession
Depreciation before possession: five causes with different rulings. After possession: if the new defect is by buyer, subject matter, or act of God, return is prevented; if by seller or third party, return is not prevented.
Causation determines the buyer's remedy.
4
Learn the Compensation Calculation
The compensation is calculated by finding the ratio of the old defect's decrease in market value to the sound value, then applying that ratio to the actual price. Work through the cloth example.
This is a proportional method, not a fixed amount.
5
Examine the Revival of the Return Right
If the new defect disappears, the buyer's right to return may revive. The passage prefers this view because the barrier (the new defect) has been removed.
Compare with khiyar al-ru'yah where option falls permanently.

وأما النقصان بعد القبض فإن كان بفعله أو بفعل المقعود عليه أو بآفة سماوية لا بالعيب لأنه . يرده بعيبين ويرجع بحصة العيب إلا إذا رضي به البائع ناقصاً وإن كان بفعل البائع أو الأجنبي يجب الإرش على الجاني وأنه يمنع الرد ويرجع بحصة العيب من الثمن اهـ وفي الواقعات اطلع على عيب بالكفن لا يرده ولا يرجع بنقصان العيب إلا إذا أحدث به عيباً فله الرجوع بالنقصان وصورة الرجوع بالنقصان أن يقوم المبيع وليس به عيب قديم ويقوم وبه ذلك فينظر إلى ما نقص من قيمته لأجل النقصان وينسب إلى القيمة السليمة فإن كانت النسبة العشر رجع بعشر الثمن وإن كانت النصف فبنصف الثمن بيانه إذا اشترى ثوباً بعشرة دراهم وقيمته مائة درهم واطلع على عيب ينقصه عشرة دراهم وقد حدث به عيب عنده فإنه يرجع بعشرة الثمن وهو درهم ولو اشتراه بمائتين وقيمته مائة ونقصه العيب عشرة فإنه يرجع بعشر الثمن وذلك عشرون وإن نقصه عشرين رجع بخمس الثمن وهو الكتاب أن البائع إذا رضي برده فالخيار للمشتري بين الرد والإمساك والرجوع بالنقصان وليس كذلك بل إذا رضي البائع فإنه يخير إن شاء أمسكه ولا رجوع له بالنقصان وإن شاء رده كما في المعراج وغيره وإذا رجع رد عليه بقضاء فإنه لا خيار له لأنه قد سقط فلا يعود ومن العيب الحادث المانع من الرد ما إذا اشترى حديداً ليتخذ منه آلات النجارين وجعله في الكور ليجربه بالنار فوجد به عيباً ولا يصلح لتلك الآلات فإنه يرجع بالنقصان ولا يرده كما في القنية ومنه أيضاً بل الجلود عيب حادث يمنع الرد بقديم وكذا بل الإبريسم منه أيضاً وفي جامع الفصولين بل إبريسما فرأى عيبه يرجع بنقصه وكذا الأديم لو أنقع في الماء فرأى عيبه الرد يطرح عنه حصته فليراجع وانظر ما قدمه المؤلف في خيار الشرط عند قول المصنف «كتعيبه» . قوله : (وظاهر الكتاب أن البائع الخ إن أراد بالكتاب الكنز فهذا الظاهر غير ظاهر فتأمله .
Book of Sale: Option of Defect. Deceived (buyer) is not defrauded. etc. And in al-Hawi al-Qudsi regarding the musarra (tied-udder camel): from Abu Yusuf that he returns it and (pays) the value of a sa' of dates and keeps its milk for himself. etc. And it is closer to the hadith of musarra established in the Two Sahihs, except that the hadith obligated return of the sa' while he obligated its value. His statement: (If another (defect) occurs with the buyer, he recourse to its depreciation or returns with the consent of his seller) i.e., after he becomes aware of the old defect, forced return upon the seller is prevented to avoid harm to him because it left his ownership sound and returns defective, so recourse to the depreciation is determined, unless the seller consents to what occurred due to his acceptance of harm. Except in one issue: if the seller is content with the new defect, the buyer is not forced to return it; rather he only recourse by the depreciation. That is when he buys a slave and it appears that he killed a person by mistake with the seller, then kills another with the buyer; if the seller wants to accept him with both crimes, the buyer is not forced, rather he only recourse by the depreciation of the first crime to avoid harm to him, because if he returned him to his seller, he would have the choice of ransom in both. Its completion is in al-Wilwaljiyyah. He generalized in 'occurrence' so it includes whether it was by a heavenly affliction or by other than it; like so in al-Mi'raj. And it includes if he bought it sick and it increased in his hand, then he does not have the return; and it is said he should return, like the toothache if it increases, unless he becomes bedridden; like so in Khizanat al-Fiqh. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: if it becomes defective with the buyer by his action or by the action of a stranger or by a heavenly affliction, and its apparent meaning is that if it becomes defective with him by the action of the seller, the return is not prevented. And the apparent meaning of the absolute statement of the book is the prevention of forced return also. And in al-Qunyah: he bought a slave and there was a trace of a boil which healed from him and he did not know of it, then the boil returned; the surgeons informed that its return was due to the old defect — he does not return it and recourses by the depreciation of the defect. This is contrary to the issue in which there was a boil that burst, or smallpox that burst with the buyer — he has the return because its bursting is not a new defect. etc. And from the new defect is that if he buys something which has transport and expense in a city and wants to return it due to an old defect in another city, he does not have forced return except in the city of contract, like fruit. And from the new defect is plucking the feathers of a slaughtered bird — the return is prevented as in al-Qunyah. Then know that the occurrence of a defect with the buyer includes when it diminishes with him. The gist of when the sold item diminishes is that it is either in the hand of the seller or the buyer. If the former, then in five ways: by the action of the seller, the buyer, a stranger, the subject of the contract, or a heavenly affliction. If by the seller's action, the buyer has the option, whether he finds a defect or not: if he wishes he leaves it, if he wishes he takes it and deducts from the price the share of the depreciation. If by the buyer's action, he is liable for the entire price and he may not keep it and demand the depreciation, but if the seller prevents him after the buyer's injury for the sake of the price, then the buyer may return it due to the defect and the price is dropped except what he diminished by his action. If the depreciation is by the action of a stranger, the buyer has the option, whether with a defect or not: if he wishes he consents to it with the entire price and pursues the injurer for the arsh, if he wishes he leaves it and the price is dropped. If the depreciation is by a heavenly affliction or by the action of the subject of the contract, he returns it with the entire price or takes it, whether he finds a defect or not; and if he takes it, he deducts from it the share of the injury of the subject of the contract. As for the depreciation after possession: if it is by his action, or by the action of the subject of the contract, or by a heavenly affliction, not due to the old defect, because he returns it with two defects and recourses to the share of the old defect, unless the seller is content with it defective. If by the action of the seller or a stranger, the arsh is obligatory upon the injurer, and it prevents the return, and he recourses to the share of the (old) defect from the price. etc. And in al-Waqi'at: he became aware of a defect in a shroud — he does not return it nor recourse by the depreciation of the defect unless he caused a defect in it, then he has recourse by the depreciation. The form of recourse by the depreciation is that the sold item is valued without the old defect and valued with it; then one looks at what decreased from its value due to the depreciation and relates it to the sound value. If the ratio is a tenth, he recourses to a tenth of the price; if half, then half the price. Illustration: if he bought a cloth for ten dirhams and its value is one hundred dirhams, and he becomes aware of a defect that decreases it by ten dirhams, and a defect occurred with him, then he recourses to a tenth of the price, which is one dirham. If he bought it for two hundred and its value is one hundred and the defect decreases it by ten, then he recourses to a tenth of the price, which is twenty. If it decreases it by twenty, he recourses to a fifth of the price, which is forty. If he bought it for one hundred and it is worth one hundred and the defect decreases it by ten, he recourses to a tenth of the price, which is ten. Like so in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj attributed to al-Yanabi'. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: and in exchange, if the depreciation is a tenth of the value, a man (recourses to) a tenth of what was made as price. And the valuers must be two, giving testimony with the formula of testimony in the presence of the seller and buyer, and the valuer is an expert in every craft. etc. One needs (to note) the difference between valuation here and in every place, for they were satisfied in the valuation of damages with a single valuer, as in Sharh al-Manzumah. And the apparent meaning of the book is that if the seller consents to its return, then the buyer has the option between return and retention with recourse by the depreciation; but it is not so. Rather, if the seller consents, he (buyer) is given choice: if he wishes he retains it and has no recourse by the depreciation, and if he wishes he returns it, as in al-Mi'raj and others. And if he recourses by the depreciation, then the new defect disappears, he has the right to return the defective (item) with the (compensation for) depreciation. It is reported in al-Qunyah that there are three opinions on it: the first is what we mentioned, and he strengthened it with another book; then he numbered for the second that he does not have return; then he numbered for a third that he inclined to that he returns it if the substitute for the depreciation is extant, otherwise no. etc. What appears is the preference of the first, because the new defect was preventing from (return) with the old, and it has ceased, so the return returns. The one who says it does not return says that the return fell and what falls does not return. And bearing witness for him is their statement in khiyar al-ru'yah: if he sold it, then (the sale) was rescinded by a court order, then he has no option because it fell and does not return. And from the new defect that prevents return: if he bought iron to make tools for carpenters and placed it in the forge to test it with fire, then found it defective and not suitable for those tools — then he recourses by the depreciation and does not return it, as in al-Qunyah. And also from it is that wetting skins is a new defect that prevents return with an old one, and likewise wetting silk from it also. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: he wet silk and saw its defect — he recourses by its depreciation; likewise if he soaked hide in water and saw its defect — the return, he deducts its share; so let him refer back. And consider what the author presented in khiyar al-shart at the statement of the compiler: 'Like his making it defective.' His statement: (The apparent meaning of the book is that the seller...) — if he means by 'the book' al-Kanz, then this apparent is not apparent; so ponder it.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the rules of khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect), specifically addressing the scenario where a new defect arises after the buyer discovers an existing defect. The author begins by citing Abu Yusuf's opinion on the musarra (tied-udder camel) case, where he differs from the explicit hadith by requiring the value of a sa' of dates rather than the sa' itself. This sets the stage for the main discussion: when a new defect occurs after the buyer learns of the old defect, he cannot force the seller to take back the item; instead, he must claim compensation (nuqsan) for the depreciation, unless the seller consents to the return. An exception is provided: even if the seller accepts, the buyer is not forced to return in a case where the slave commits two homicides—the buyer can only seek compensation for the first homicide. The passage then generalizes that the new defect includes both natural calamities and other causes, and extends to illnesses worsening in the buyer's hands. Detailed categorizations follow: if depreciation occurs in the seller's hands, there are five causes (seller's act, buyer's act, third party's act, act of the subject matter, or act of God), each with specific rules for return or compensation. If depreciation occurs in the buyer's hands after possession, the rule depends on the cause: if by buyer, subject matter, or act of God, return is prevented and compensation is sought only for the old defect; if by seller or third party, the return is not prevented. The method of calculating compensation is explained: the item is valued without the old defect and with it; the ratio of decrease in value is applied to the actual price. A worked example with a cloth is provided. The passage concludes with a discussion on whether the right to return revives if the new defect disappears, preferring the view that it does, and lists examples of acts that constitute new defects preventing return (e.g., wetting skins or silk). The final note clarifies a misinterpretation about the seller's consent giving the buyer a choice between return and retention with compensation—the correct ruling is that the buyer may either retain without compensation or return.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The buyer's option to return the sold item due to a hidden defect that existed at the time of sale but was discovered later.
النقصان (al-nuqsan): The depreciation in value caused by a new defect that occurs after possession; the buyer can claim a proportional part of the price.
الرد (al-radd): The act of returning the sold item to the seller due to a defect, either by force (jabran) or with consent.
المصراة (al-musarra): A camel or other animal whose udder is tied to make it seem full of milk; a specific defect case in hadith.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer discovers an old defect in a cloth and then accidentally tears it (new defect), what is his remedy?
A) He can force the seller to take back the cloth and get a full refund.
B) He can keep the cloth and claim compensation for the old defect only.
C) He can return the cloth only if the seller agrees, otherwise he claims compensation for the old defect.
D) He has no remedy because he caused a new defect.

Key Takeaway
When a new defect arises after the buyer discovers an old defect, forced return is blocked, but the buyer can either claim proportional compensation or return with the seller's consent.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
7 min
New Defect After Purchase: Return or Compensation?
States the Hanafi ruling on the effect of a new defect on the right to return for a pre-existing defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the different causes of a new defect after purchase and state the Hanafi ruling for each scenario.

Core Idea
A new defect caused by the buyer or natural causes blocks return, but a defect caused by the seller or a third party allows return with compensation.

In a sale, if a buyer discovers a pre-existing defect after a new defect has occurred, the Hanafi school distinguishes based on who caused the new defect. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains the different outcomes, balancing justice for both parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defects
Separate the pre-existing defect (old) from the new defect that occurred after the buyer took possession.
2
Determine the cause of the new defect
Classify the cause: buyer's action, natural action of the item, natural calamity, seller's action, or third party action.
3
Apply the ruling
If the new defect is caused by buyer, item itself, or calamity: buyer keeps the item and claims compensation (arsh) for the old defect. If by seller or third party: buyer can return the item and claim arsh from the injurer.
Exception: if seller agrees to accept the item back despite the new defect, return is allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرده عليه يرده بجميع الثمن أو يأخذه وجد به عيباً أو لا ولو أخذه يطرح عنه حصة جناية المعقود عليه وأما النقصان بعد القبض فإن كان بفعله أو بفعل المقعود عليه أو بآفة سماوية لا بالعيب لأنه . يرده بعيبين ويرجع بحصة العيب إلا إذا رضي به البائع ناقصاً وإن كان بفعل البائع أو الأجنبي يجب الإرش على الجاني وأنه يمنع الرد ويرجع بحصة العيب من الثمن اهـ
He returns it to him? He returns it with all the price or takes it whether he finds a defect in it or not, and if he takes it, he deducts from it the share of the injury of the contracted object. As for the deficiency after delivery, if it is by his action or by the action of the contracted object or by a natural calamity, he does not [return] because of the defect. He returns it with two defects and claims compensation for the share of the defect unless the seller accepts it deficient. And if it is by the action of the seller or a third party, compensation is obligatory upon the injurer, and it prevents return, and he claims compensation for the share of the defect from the price. End.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
Ruling: This passage states the general rule for when a buyer discovers a pre-existing defect after a new defect has occurred post-delivery. The new defect prevents the return of the item. The buyer cannot return the item for the old defect if the new defect is caused by him, by the natural action of the object itself, or by a natural calamity (آفة سماوية). Instead, he keeps the item and claims compensation for the value lost due to the old defect. However, if the seller accepts the item as is, the buyer may return it. If the new defect was caused by the seller or a third party, the buyer can return the item and claim compensation from the injurer.

Key Terms
النقصان الحادث (al-nuqsān al-hādith): A new deficiency occurring after the buyer's possession of the item.
الإرش (al-arsh): Compensation paid due to a defect, calculated as the difference in value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer discovers a pre-existing defect in a garment after he himself has cut it (a new defect), according to the Hanafi school, what are his options?
A) He can return the garment and get a full refund.
B) He can only claim compensation for the old defect but cannot return the garment.
C) He can return the garment but must pay for the new defect.
D) He has no recourse at all.

Key Takeaway
The cause of a post-purchase defect determines whether the buyer can return the item for a pre-existing defect.
